The Three Rational Divisions of Possible Beings
Al-Razi enumerates the three rational divisions of possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational categories into which all possible beings are divided, and the definition of the first category: that which occupies space (al-mutahayyiz).

Core Idea
All possible beings fall into three rational categories: space-occupying, subsisting in it, or neither.

Al-Razi begins with a foundational rational division that organizes all possible beings. This threefold classification provides the basic framework for ontological discussions in kalam. The first category, mutahayyiz, is crucial because it is further subdivided into body and atom in subsequent discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the three categories
Read the Arabic passage and identify the three categories listed. Notice the Arabic terms: المتحيز, الحال في المتحيز, and the third category.
The third category is a negative description: neither mutahayyiz nor hall.
2
Understand the definition of mutahayyiz
Al-Razi defines mutahayyiz as that which can be pointed to sensorily. Think about what this means: anything that occupies space and can be located 'here' or 'there'.
This definition is broader than 'body'.
3
Reflect on the division logic
Consider why this division is exhaustive and mutually exclusive. Every possible being must either occupy space, subsist in something that occupies space, or be completely independent of both.
This division sets the stage for discussing God, who is the third category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو بحسب القسمة العقلية على ثلاثة أقسام المتحيز والحال في المتحيز، والذي لا يكون متحيزاً ولا حالاً في المتحيز. أما القسم الأول وهو المتحيز فاعلم أن المراد من المتحيز الذي يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية بأنه ههنا أو هناك. وإذا عرفت حقيقة المتحيز فنقول : المتحيز إما أن يكون قابلاً للقسمة، وإما أن لا يكون. فالمتحيز الذي يكون قابلاً للقسمة هو المسمى بالجسم. فعلى هذا : الجسم ما يكون مؤلفاً من جزأين فصاعداً . والمعتزلة يقولون : الجسم هو الذي يكون طويلاً عريضاً عميقاً وأقل الجسم إنما يحصل من ثمانية أجزاء. وهذا النزاع لغوي لا عقلي. وأما المتحيز الذي لا يكون منقسماً . فهو المسمى بالجوهر الفرد. والناس قد اختلفوا في إثباته. وسنذكر هذه المسألة على الاستقصاء. إن شاء الله تعالى.
And it [the possible being] is, according to rational division, into three categories: that which occupies space (al-mutahayyiz), that which subsists in that which occupies space (al-hall fi al-mutahayyiz), and that which is neither occupying space nor subsisting in that which occupies space. As for the first category, which is that which occupies space, know that the meaning of 'that which occupies space' is that which can be pointed to with a sensory indication, as being here or there. And if you understand the reality of that which occupies space, we say: That which occupies space is either divisible or not divisible. That which occupies space and is divisible is called body (jism). On this basis, the body is that which is composed of two or more parts. The Mu'tazila say: The body is that which is long, wide, and deep, and the minimum of a body is obtained from eight parts. This dispute is linguistic, not rational. As for that which occupies space and is indivisible, it is called the individual atom (al-jawhar al-fard). People have differed about its affirmation, and we will mention this issue in detail, God willing.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the rational division of possible beings into three categories: (1) that which occupies space (mutahayyiz), (2) that which subsists in what occupies space (hall), and (3) that which is neither. Al-Razi then focuses on the first category. He defines mutahayyiz as that which can be pointed to sensorily. Then he subdivides mutahayyiz into that which is divisible (body) and that which is indivisible (the atom). For a body, he gives the standard kalam definition: composed of two or more parts. He notes the Mu'tazilite definition (length, width, depth from eight parts) and says this is merely a verbal difference. He then introduces the atom (jawhar fard) as the indivisible component, noting that its existence is controversial and will be discussed later.

Key Terms
القسمة العقلية
المتحيز
الحال في المتحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the three categories are exhaustive but not exclusive, or that mutahayyiz means 'body' specifically.
Correction: Al-Razi makes clear that mutahayyiz is a broader category that includes bodies and atoms, and the three categories are both exhaustive and mutually exclusive.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi's rational division, which of the following belongs to the first category?
A) An accident like color
B) A body
C) The essence of God
D) A mental concept

Key Takeaway
All possible beings are divided into three rational categories: space-occupying, subsisting in a space-occupant, or neither.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Of the three categories al-Razi enumerates, where do you place your own soul? Is it a space-occupying thing, an attribute subsisting in something else, or something else entirely? How does your answer affect how you understand your relationship with the Creator, who is in the third category?
?
If God is neither in space nor an attribute of something else, what does that mean for how you conceive of His closeness to you? Are you tempted to imagine Him as being 'somewhere'? How will you train your mind to avoid that reduction?
?
This rational division leaves no room for 'supernatural' beings that are vaguely attached to the physical world without belonging to one of these categories. Does this challenge any hidden assumptions you hold about angels, jinn, or other unseen beings? If so, how will you reconcile your beliefs with this logical framework?

Body and Atom: Subdivisions of the Occupant of Space
Al-Razi subdivides mutahayyiz into body and atom and discusses their definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi subdivides the category of mutahayyiz into divisible (body) and indivisible (atom), and the nature of the linguistic dispute with the Mu'tazila regarding the definition of body.

Core Idea
Every space-occupant is either divisible (body) or indivisible (atom).

After defining the general category of mutahayyiz, al-Razi now focuses on its internal division. He introduces the concepts of body (jism) and individual atom (jawhar fard). He also addresses a terminological disagreement with the Mu'tazila, emphasizing that their differing definition is merely verbal, not substantive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subdivision
Read the passage and note that mutahayyiz is divided into two based on divisibility: divisible and indivisible.
This is a dichotomy: every mutahayyiz is either divisible or not.
2
Learn the definition of body
Al-Razi defines body as that which is composed of two or more parts. Contrast this with the Mu'tazilite definition which requires length, width, depth from eight parts.
Al-Razi considers this a linguistic dispute, not a rational one.
3
Understand the atom
The indivisible mutahayyiz is called the individual atom (jawhar fard). Note that its existence is controversial and will be discussed later.
The atom is the smallest possible unit of space-occupation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو بحسب القسمة العقلية على ثلاثة أقسام المتحيز والحال في المتحيز، والذي لا يكون متحيزاً ولا حالاً في المتحيز. أما القسم الأول وهو المتحيز فاعلم أن المراد من المتحيز الذي يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية بأنه ههنا أو هناك. وإذا عرفت حقيقة المتحيز فنقول : المتحيز إما أن يكون قابلاً للقسمة، وإما أن لا يكون. فالمتحيز الذي يكون قابلاً للقسمة هو المسمى بالجسم. فعلى هذا : الجسم ما يكون مؤلفاً من جزأين فصاعداً . والمعتزلة يقولون : الجسم هو الذي يكون طويلاً عريضاً عميقاً وأقل الجسم إنما يحصل من ثمانية أجزاء. وهذا النزاع لغوي لا عقلي. وأما المتحيز الذي لا يكون منقسماً . فهو المسمى بالجوهر الفرد. والناس قد اختلفوا في إثباته. وسنذكر هذه المسألة على الاستقصاء. إن شاء الله تعالى.
And it [the possible being] is, according to rational division, into three categories: that which occupies space (al-mutahayyiz), that which subsists in that which occupies space (al-hall fi al-mutahayyiz), and that which is neither occupying space nor subsisting in that which occupies space. As for the first category, which is that which occupies space, know that the meaning of 'that which occupies space' is that which can be pointed to with a sensory indication, as being here or there. And if you understand the reality of that which occupies space, we say: That which occupies space is either divisible or not divisible. That which occupies space and is divisible is called body (jism). On this basis, the body is that which is composed of two or more parts. The Mu'tazila say: The body is that which is long, wide, and deep, and the minimum of a body is obtained from eight parts. This dispute is linguistic, not rational. As for that which occupies space and is indivisible, it is called the individual atom (al-jawhar al-fard). People have differed about its affirmation, and we will mention this issue in detail, God willing.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the rational division of possible beings into three categories: (1) that which occupies space (mutahayyiz), (2) that which subsists in what occupies space (hall), and (3) that which is neither. Al-Razi then focuses on the first category. He defines mutahayyiz as that which can be pointed to sensorily. Then he subdivides mutahayyiz into that which is divisible (body) and that which is indivisible (the atom). For a body, he gives the standard kalam definition: composed of two or more parts. He notes the Mu'tazilite definition (length, width, depth from eight parts) and says this is merely a verbal difference. He then introduces the atom (jawhar fard) as the indivisible component, noting that its existence is controversial and will be discussed later.

Key Terms
الجسم
الجوهر الفرد
النزاع اللغوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilite definition of body is fundamentally different from al-Razi's definition.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states the dispute is linguistic, not rational; both definitions refer to the same category.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the definition of body?
A) That which has length, width, and depth from eight parts
B) That which is composed of two or more parts
C) That which is indivisible
D) That which can be pointed to

Key Takeaway
Every space-occupant is either divisible (a body composed of parts) or indivisible (an atom).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own body is merely a collection of parts, what is the 'you' that remains when those parts are taken away? How does this affect your attachment to your physical form?
?
Al-Razi dismisses the Mu'tazilite definition as merely linguistic. Are there disputes in your own faith or practice that you hold as essential but might actually be just words? How can you find out?
?
The atom is said to be indivisible—a limit to physical reality. What does that say about the limits of your own understanding? Are you content with not knowing what lies beyond the smallest part?

The First Proof for the Temporality of Bodies: The Dichotomy of Motion and Rest
Razi presents the first proof for temporal origination and establishes its first premise: the dichotomy of motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of Razi's first proof for the temporality of bodies and the logical necessity of the dichotomy that every body must be either moving or at rest.

Core Idea
Every body occupies space, so it must either stay in one place or move — there is no third option.

Razi now moves from preliminaries to the first actual proof in the book: proving that bodies are temporally originated. He presents a conditional argument: if bodies were eternal, they would have to be either moving or at rest in eternity. Both are impossible, so bodies are not eternal. But before he can disprove each option, he must first establish that the dichotomy itself is exhaustive. This passage introduces that first premise: every body, being spatial, must be either stationary or mobile. This seemingly trivial point is the foundation on which the entire proof rests.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of Razi's argument come through before interpreting.
Notice that Razi begins by wrapping up the introduction and then launches into the proof.
2
Identify the overall argument structure
Razi presents a conditional argument: if bodies were eternal, they would be either moving or at rest; both are false; therefore bodies are not eternal. He then says this proof requires three premises.
The first premise is the one detailed in this passage.
3
Understand the basis of the dichotomy
Razi argues that everything that occupies space (mutahayyiz) must be specific to a location. From this, he deduces that in eternity a body is either in one location (rest) or moving between locations (motion).
The key concept is 'occupying space'—any body can be pointed to as 'here' or 'there'.
4
Reflect on why this premise is necessary
Without this dichotomy, someone could claim that an eternal body is neither moving nor at rest, which would sidestep the proof. Razi closes that loophole by showing movement and rest are the only possibilities for a spatial entity.
Later premises will show that both options are impossible for an eternal being.
5
Summarize the first premise in your own words
Try to express the first premise concisely: every body, because it occupies space, must be either in a fixed location (rest) or changing location (motion).
This will help solidify the logical step before moving to the next premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا آخر الكلام في شرح هذه المقدمات التي يجب تقديمها على الشروع في البراهين. البرهان الأول : في إثبات حدوث الأجسام. وهو أنا نقول: الأجسام لو كانت أزلية لكانت في الأزل، إما أن تكون متحركة، أو ساكنة والقسمان باطلان، فالقول بكونها أزلية باطل فنفتقر في تقرير هذا البرهان إلى إثبات مقدمات ثلاث : المقدمة الأولى : في إقامة الدليل على الحصر : فنقول : الدليل عليه : أن كل ما كان متحيزا فلا بد وأن يكون مختصا بحيز معين. والمراد منه : أنه لا بد وأن يكون بحيث يصح أن يشار إليه بأنه ههنا أو هناك، إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنه في الأزل إما أن يكون باقيًا في حيز واحد أو لا يكون كذلك، بل يكون متنقلا من حيز إلى حيز والأول هو الساكن، والثاني هو المتحرك. فثبت أن الجسم لو كان أزليا ، لكان في الأزل إما أن يكون متحركا، أو ساكنًا.
And this is the end of the discourse in explaining these premises that must be presented before commencing the proofs. The first proof: on establishing the temporal origination of bodies. It is that we say: If bodies were eternal, then in eternity they would be either moving or at rest. Both divisions are false, so the statement that they are eternal is false. So in establishing this proof we need to prove three premises. The first premise: on establishing the proof for the dichotomy. We say: The proof for it is that everything that occupies space must be specific to a particular location; what is meant is that it must be such that it can be pointed to as being 'here' or 'there'. When you have understood this, we say: In eternity, it is either remaining in one location or not like that, but rather moving from location to location. The first is the stationary, and the second is the moving. So it is established that if a body were eternal, then in eternity it would be either moving or at rest.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage marks the conclusion of the introductory sections and the beginning of the first actual proof of the work: proving that bodies (the physical world) are temporally originated (hadith). Razi outlines the proof in the form of a conditional: if bodies were eternal, they would be either in motion or at rest in eternity; both are impossible; therefore bodies cannot be eternal. He then states that to establish this argument, three premises are needed. The first premise is the justification of the dichotomy: every body (being spatial) must be either in a fixed location (rest) or changing location (motion). This is a necessary truth based on the fact that bodies occupy space and can be pointed to. Thus, the dichotomy is exhaustive and exclusive, setting up the refutation of both alternatives in subsequent premises.

Key Terms
أزلي
متحيز
حيز
ساكن
متحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes that motion or rest are inherently impossible for any existing thing.
Correction: The proof does not assume motion or rest are impossible in themselves; it will prove each impossible specifically for eternal existence in the subsequent premises.

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, what is the basis for the dichotomy of motion and rest for a body?
A) Its color
B) Its occupation of space
C) Its being composite
D) Its temporal nature

Key Takeaway
The first premise of Razi's proof is that every body, because it occupies space, must be either at rest or in motion — a dichotomy that sets up the refutation of both alternatives for eternal bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body, right now, is either at rest or in motion. This simple fact proves your body is not eternal—it was created. Does this truth sit comfortably with you, or does it challenge the way you live as if this world will never end? What would change if you truly believed that every moment is a gift from the Creator who brought your temporary body into existence?
?
Razi did not present this proof as mere intellectual exercise; he intended it to lead to certainty about the Creator. Have you let rational proofs translate into genuine faith, or are you content with knowing without acting? If this proof convinces you that the universe is created, what specific duty does that place on you today—and will you do it?
?
Consider that your own body is subject to the dichotomy of motion and rest every second. If you accept that it is temporal, then your ultimate goal cannot be permanent satisfaction in this world. Are you investing more in your temporary body or your eternal soul? What is one habit you can change right now to align with this truth?

Razi's First Proof: Impossibility of Eternal Motion
Razi proves that eternal motion is impossible by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that eternal motion is impossible due to the inherent priority in the definition of motion.

Core Idea
Motion by definition requires a prior state; eternity by definition has no prior — together they contradict.

Razi now presents the first proof within his second premise: that bodies cannot be in motion from eternity. He shows that the very nature of motion entails being preceded by something, while eternity excludes any such priority. This contradiction makes eternal motion impossible in itself.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the essence of motion
Read how Razi defines motion: it is a transition from one state to another. This is the core of the argument.
Focus on the Arabic: 'الحركة ماهيتها وحقيقتها أنها انتقال من حالة إلى حالة'.
2
Identify the necessary implication
Notice that every transition requires the prior existence of the state being left. Thus, motion necessarily involves being preceded by another (musabaqa bi-ghayr).
This is a logical necessity, not an empirical observation.
3
Contrast with the nature of eternity
Eternity (al-azal) means existence with no prior cause or state. Its essence negates being preceded.
Razi asserts that the two realities are mutually exclusive.
4
Conclude the impossibility
Since motion requires priority and eternity precludes it, the combination 'eternal motion' is a self-contradiction. It is impossible by its very essence (mumtani' li-dhatihi).
This is a concise apodictic proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في إقامة الدلالة على أنه يمتنع كون الأجسام في الأزل متحركة ويدل عليه وجوه الأول: أن الحركة ماهيتها وحقيقتها أنها انتقال من حالة إلى حالة، والانتقال من حالة إلى حالة لا بد وأن يكون مسبوقاً بحصول الحالة المنتقل عنها . فإذن حقيقة الحركة من حيث إنها تلك الحقيقة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير وحقيقة الأزل من حيث إنها هذه الحقيقة تنافي المسبوقية بالغير، فوجب أن يكون الجمع بين الحركة والأزل محالا ممتنعا لذاته.
The second premise: on establishing the demonstration that it is impossible for bodies to be in motion in eternity. And there are proofs for it. The first: that motion, its quiddity and reality, is that it is a transition from one state to another, and transition from state to state must be preceded by the occurrence of the state from which it transitions. So the reality of motion, inasmuch as it is that reality, necessitates being preceded by another; and the reality of eternity, inasmuch as it is that reality, precludes being preceded by another. Therefore, the combination of motion and eternity is necessarily impossible in itself (mumtani' li-dhatihi).
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument within the second premise of the main proof. Razi aims to show that bodies cannot be in motion from eternity. The argument is based on the very essence (mahiyya) of motion: motion is essentially a transition from one state to another, and every transition presupposes the prior existence of the state left behind. Thus, motion inherently requires being preceded (musabaqa) by something else (the initial state). Eternity (al-azal), by definition, means existence without any prior state or cause. Since motion entails priority and eternity precludes it, the two are mutually exclusive. Therefore, the concept of 'eternal motion' is a contradiction in terms—it is impossible by virtue of its own essence (mumtani' li-dhatihi). This is a concise apodictic proof.

Key Terms
ماهية الحركة
انتقال من حالة إلى حالة
مسبوقية بالغير
امتناع ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi denies all motion, including temporal motion, or that he denies change altogether.
Correction: The argument only denies motion that is without beginning (eternal); it does not deny motion that begins in time. Razi accepts temporal motion as possible.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say is the essence (haqiqa) of motion?
A) Change of place
B) Transition from state to state
C) Being in two places at once
D) Continuous existence

Key Takeaway
Eternal motion is impossible because the essence of motion requires being preceded by another state, while eternity by definition has no preceding state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi proves that motion cannot be eternal because it requires a prior state. Now think about your own life: every action you take—prayer, eating, even breathing—is it not preceded by a cause? Do you truly believe that each moment is dependent on Allah, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The argument shows a logical contradiction: eternity and motion cannot coexist. How does this affect your certainty that the universe was created? Have you ever truly internalized that you and everything around you are not eternal, and that your existence is a gift from Allah that can be taken away at any moment?
?
Consider the movements of your heart: your inclinations, your desires. They come and go; they are not eternal. Does this fact humble you? Do you recognize that your very will is created each moment by Allah, or do you claim autonomy over your own intentions?

Impossibility of Eternal Motion: The Regress Argument
Razi provides a second proof against eternal motion using an infinite regress of rotations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that an eternal series of rotations leads to a logical contradiction, requiring a beginning for all existences.

Core Idea
Eternal motion forces prior and posterior to coexist, which is impossible.

Razi now presents a more refined argument against the possibility of eternal motion. He considers an endless series of celestial rotations and shows that the non-existences preceding each rotation would all be simultaneous in eternity. This creates a dilemma: either some existence coexists with that totality of non-existences (contradiction) or no existence does, implying existence has a beginning. Follow the careful logical steps to see why eternal motion is rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the supposition
Suppose each rotation of the celestial sphere is preceded by another rotation, with no first rotation. This means every rotation has a non-existence (its absence) that itself has no beginning.
This is an actual infinite regress, not a potential one.
2
Collect all non-existences in eternity
Since each non-existence is without beginning, all these non-existences are together in eternity (they are all 'before' any existence in an unordered sense). Only the existences (rotations) have temporal order.
Think of eternity as a single timeless moment containing all these absences.
3
Apply the dilemma
Either some existence (a rotation) occurs together with that totality of non-existences in eternity, or none does. The first option leads to a contradiction because it would make the prior (non-existence) simultaneous with the posterior (existence).
This is the core of the argument.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the first option is impossible, we accept the second: no existence occurs in eternity. Therefore, existence must have a beginning and a first moment.
This proves that motion cannot be eternal; it must have started.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني : أنا إذا افترضنا أن كل دورة من دورات الفلك كانت مسبوقة بدورة أخرى، لا إلى أول فحينئذ تكون كل واحدة من تلك الدورات مسبوقة بعدم لا أول له، فتكون العدمات بأسرها مجتمعة في الأزل وإنما الترتيب في الوجودات، لا جميع العدمات السابقة على كل واحدة من هذه الوجودات في العدمات . فإذن كانت مجتمعة في الأزل. فإما أن يحصل مع مجموع تلك العدمات الحاصلة في الأزل شيء من الوجودات، أو لم يحصل. والأول باطل وإلا لزم أن يكون السابق مقارنا للمسبوق، وهو محال. وإذا بطل القسم الأول تعين القسم الثاني، وهو أنه لم يحصل في الأزل شيء من الوجودات. وذلك يقتضي أن يكون لمجموع الوجودات بداية وأول . وهو المطلوب.
The second proof: that if we suppose that each rotation of the celestial rotations was preceded by another rotation without beginning, then each one of those rotations is preceded by a non-existence that has no beginning, so all the non-existences are collected together in eternity—only order exists among the existences, not among all the non-existences preceding each one of these existences among the non-existences. So they were collected together in eternity. Then either something of the existences occurs together with that totality of non-existences occurring in eternity, or it does not occur. The first is false, otherwise it would necessitate that the prior be concurrent with the posterior, which is impossible. And when the first division is false, the second division is determined, namely that nothing of the existences occurred in eternity. That necessitates that the totality of existences has a beginning and a first—that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents a second, more complex argument against the possibility of eternal motion. Razi considers the case of celestial rotations (like the daily rotations of the spheres). If these rotations were eternal in the sense of an infinite past series (each preceded by another without a first), then consider the set of all non-existences (the absences) that precede each rotation. Since there is no first rotation, each rotation has a non-existence preceding it that itself has no beginning. All these non-existences are simultaneous in eternity because they are all 'before' any particular existence in an unordered sense; only the existences have temporal order. So in eternity, all these non-existences coexist. The question is: does any existence (i.e., any actual rotation) coexist with that totality of non-existences in eternity? If yes, then the prior (the non-existence) would be concurrent with the posterior (the existence)—a contradiction. If no, then no existence occurs in eternity, meaning that existences begin after eternity, i.e., they have a first beginning. This proves temporal origination.

Key Terms
دورات الفلك
عدم لا أول له
العدمات
السابق والمسبوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite regress of events is logically possible as long as each event has a cause.
Correction: Razi shows that an actual infinite regress of non-existences leads to a contradiction because all those non-existences become simultaneous in eternity, and then any existence would have to coexist with its own prior absence.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude must be the case if no existence occurs with the totality of non-existences in eternity?
A) The world is eternal
B) Motion is impossible
C) Existence has a beginning
D) Non-existence is eternal

Key Takeaway
An eternal series of motions leads to a contradiction because it forces the prior to coexist with the posterior; therefore, existence must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi’s argument forces you to confront the impossibility of an eternal past. Your own existence began at a specific moment. Do you live as if that moment is a mere fact, or does it make you acutely aware that every second of your life is a gift from the One who brought existence into being? What would change today if you truly believed your existence could end at any moment?
?
The argument shows that existence cannot be self-sufficient—it must have a beginning. In your daily life, do you act as if you are self-sufficient, relying on your own resources, or do you constantly acknowledge your dependence on Allah? Choose one concrete action today that reminds you of your dependence on the Creator.
?
If the entire universe had a beginning, then everything you have—your abilities, wealth, relationships—also had a beginning. Are you holding on to any of these as if they were permanent? How would your gratitude and generosity change if you fully accepted that all of this is temporary and given to you for a limited time?

The First Premise: Bodies in Eternity Must Be Moving or at Rest
Razi establishes the dichotomy that an eternal body must be either moving or at rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi establishes the exclusive dichotomy that an eternal body must be either moving or at rest, forming the first premise of his proof for the temporal origination of bodies.

Core Idea
An eternal body cannot avoid being either moving or at rest.

Imam Razi begins his first proof for the temporal origination of bodies by setting up a critical dichotomy. He argues that if bodies were eternal, they would have to be either moving or at rest in pre-eternity. This lesson unpacks that dichotomy, showing how it follows from the very nature of anything that occupies space. Understanding this foundational premise is essential to following the rest of Razi's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi states the overall proof first, then explains that it requires three premises, and finally gives the first premise in detail.
The Arabic passage includes the introduction to the entire proof; focus on the part that establishes the dichotomy.
2
Understand the key definition
Razi defines 'occupying space' (mutahayyiz) as anything that can be pointed to as being here or there. This is crucial because it implies a specific location, which leads to the dichotomy.
Memorize the terms: mutahayyiz, hayyiz, sākin, mutaharrik.
3
See how the dichotomy follows
Since an eternal body occupies space, it must be in a specific location in pre-eternity. It can either remain in that location (rest) or move from it (motion). No third option exists.
This dichotomy is self-evident once the definition of occupying space is accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الأول : في إثبات حدوث الأجسام. وهو أنا نقول: الأجسام لو كانت أزلية لكانت في الأزل، إما أن تكون متحركة، أو ساكنة والقسمان باطلان، فالقول بكونها أزلية باطل فنفتقر في تقرير هذا البرهان إلى إثبات مقدمات ثلاث : المقدمة الأولى : في إقامة الدليل على الحصر : فنقول : الدليل عليه : أن كل ما كان متحيزا فلا بد وأن يكون مختصا بحيز معين. والمراد منه : أنه لا بد وأن يكون أن يشار إليه بأنه ههنا أو هناك. بحيث يصح إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنه في الأزل إما أن يكون باقيًا في حيز واحد أو لا يكون كذلك، بل يكون متنقلا من حيز إلى حيز والأول هو الساكن، والثاني هو المتحرك. فثبت أن الجسم لو كان أزليّا ، لكان في الأزل إما أن يكون متحركا، أو ساكنًا.
The first proof: on establishing the temporal origination of bodies. It is that we say: If bodies were eternal, they would in pre-eternity be either moving or at rest, and both divisions are false, so the statement that they are eternal is false. So in establishing this proof we need to prove three premises: The first premise: on providing the proof for the exclusive dichotomy. We say: The proof for it is that everything that occupies space (mutahayyiz) must be specific to a particular space (hayyiz). And the intended meaning is that it must be such that it can be pointed to as being here or there. So that it is correct. If you understand this, we say: It in pre-eternity either remains in one space or it does not, rather it moves from space to space. The first is the stationary (sākin) and the second is the moving (mutaharrik). So it is established that if a body were eternal, it would in pre-eternity be either moving or at rest.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is the first premise of the first proof for the temporal origination of bodies (ḥudūth al-ajsām). Imam Razi begins by stating the overall argument: if bodies were eternal, they would in pre-eternity (al-azal) be either moving or at rest. Both possibilities are impossible, establishing that bodies cannot be eternal. This proof requires three premises, and the first is establishing the exclusive dichotomy that a body in pre-eternity must be either moving or at rest. Razi argues by definition: anything that occupies space (mutahayyiz) must be in a specific location (hayyiz), meaning it can be pointed to as 'here' or 'there'. Therefore, in pre-eternity it either stays in one place (rest) or moves from place to place (motion). No third option exists. Thus, the dichotomy is proven.

Key Terms

حيز
ساكن
متحرك
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'occupying space' implies a physical location only, but Razi means that anything that can be pointed to is necessarily subject to motion or rest.
Correction: Razi's definition of mutahayyiz is broad: anything that can be pointed to as 'here' or 'there' qualifies, and from that the dichotomy of motion or rest follows inevitably.

Checkpoint

A) Existing and non-existing
B) Moving and at rest
C) Finite and infinite
D) Created and uncreated

Key Takeaway
A body, if eternal, must be either moving or at rest in pre-eternity; no third state is possible, making the dichotomy a necessary starting point for proving temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that an eternal body must be either moving or at rest, both of which are impossible for something truly without beginning. Do you live as though the world and your own existence are self-sufficient and eternal, or do you acknowledge your utter dependence on a Creator who brought you into being? How would your daily life change if you truly internalized that you are not self-existent?
?
Consider that every object around you—including your own body—occupies space and is subject to motion or rest. If these bodies are not eternal but brought into existence, what does that imply about the One who brought them into existence? Are you living in a way that reflects gratitude for your contingent existence, or do you take it for granted?
?
Razi's first premise forces you to accept that any body existing without a beginning must be in one of two states, both of which are impossible. Have you ever honestly examined the implications of your own existence? If your body and everything you rely on are temporally originated, what does that demand of you in your relationship with your Creator?

Motion Cannot Be Eternal: The First Argument
Razi argues that motion's nature requires temporal priority, contradicting eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that motion and pre-eternity (al-azal) are inherently incompatible by their very essences.

Core Idea
Motion's essence requires a prior state; eternity excludes any priority.

Razi begins his proof that bodies cannot be moving in pre-eternity by analyzing the very definition of motion. He argues that motion is not a self-sufficient reality; it depends on a prior state from which it transitions. This dependence contradicts the definition of eternity, which is characterized by lack of any priority. Let's examine his logical argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Notice the key terms: حركة, ماهية, انتقال, مسبوقية, أزل, محال ذاتي. Let the author's rhythm come through.
2
Identify the definition of motion
Razi defines motion as a transition from one state to another. This is the foundational premise. Understand why he says transition requires a prior state.
The phrase 'من حالة إلى حالة' is crucial.
3
Contrast with the definition of pre-eternity
Pre-eternity (al-azal) is defined by not being preceded by anything. Razi asserts that this is incompatible with motion's need for a prior state.
The term 'مسبوقية' appears for both motion (it is preceded) and eternity (it is not).
4
Reach the conclusion
Since the two definitions are contradictory by their very natures (li-dhātihi), combining them is impossible. Razi calls this 'محال ممتنع لذاته' (inherently impossible).
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Note that this is a deductive argument: from definitions to an impossibility. It does not rely on scripture but on rational analysis of essences.
This is typical of kalam proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في إقامة الدلالة على أنه يمتنع كون الأجسام في الأزل متحركة ويدل عليه وجوه الأول: أن الحركة ماهيتها وحقيقتها أنها انتقال من حالة إلى حالة، والانتقال من حالة إلى حالة لا بد وأن يكون مسبوقاً بحصول الحالة المنتقل عنها . فإذن حقيقة الحركة من حيث إنها تلك الحقيقة تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير وحقيقة الأزل من حيث إنها هذه الحقيقة تنافي المسبوقية بالغير، فوجب أن يكون الجمع بين الحركة والأزل محالا ممتنعا لذاته.
The second premise: on providing the evidence that it is impossible for bodies to be moving in pre-eternity. And it is indicated by several aspects: The first: that motion—its essence (māhiyya) and reality—is that it is a transition from a state to a state, and transition from a state to a state must be preceded by the occurrence of the state from which the transition occurs. Therefore, the reality of motion, inasmuch as it is that reality, necessitates being preceded by something else, and the reality of pre-eternity, inasmuch as it is that reality, is incompatible with being preceded by something else. So it follows that combining motion and pre-eternity is inherently impossible and absurd.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is the second premise of the first proof, specifically the first of several arguments demonstrating that motion cannot occur in pre-eternity. Razi defines motion (ḥaraka) as a transition from one state to another, which necessarily requires a prior state from which the transition begins. This prior state means motion is always preceded by something else—it is not self-sufficient. Eternity (al-azal), on the other hand, is defined by not being preceded by anything. Therefore, the two are incompatible by their very essences (dhāt). The combination is 'inherently impossible' (muḥāl mumtaniʿ li-dhātihi). Thus, bodies cannot be moving in eternity.

Key Terms
حركة
ماهية
انتقال
مسبوقية
أزل
محال ذاتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion could be eternal if it is a continuous, uninterrupted process (e.g., a body always moving without starting).
Correction: Razi argues that even continuous motion requires a prior state at every instant. At any given moment, the motion is a transition from a previous state. Thus, the essence of motion always involves being preceded by that previous state, which conflicts with the definition of eternity as having no prior state.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say motion cannot exist in pre-eternity (al-azal)?
A) Because motion is a change that requires a prior state, while eternity cannot have a prior state
B) Because motion is a physical phenomenon that cannot exist without time
C) Because motion is created by God and cannot be eternal
D) Because the Quran says motion is temporary

Key Takeaway
Motion and pre-eternity are incompatible by their very definitions: motion requires a prior state, while eternity excludes all priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If motion cannot be eternal because it requires a prior state, what does that say about your spiritual habits? Are you moving toward Allah, or are you stuck in a cycle that has no real beginning or end? What prior state of heedlessness are you transitioning from, and what commitment will you make today to ensure that transition is real?
?
Razi says that combining motion and pre-eternity is 'impossible in itself'. Are there things in your life that you try to combine that are fundamentally incompatible—like love of this world and love of the next? Identify one such contradiction in your heart and decide which state you must leave behind.
?
The passage defines motion as a transition. If your life is a motion, what is the state you are leaving, and what is the state you are heading toward? Will you allow the prior state to define your future, or will you cut off the past and seek a fresh start that pleases Allah?

The Second Proof: Infinite Cycles and Impossible Coexistence
Razi proves that infinite cycles require all non-existences to coexist in pre-eternity, making existence impossible without a first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that infinite past cycles would require all non-existences to be simultaneous in pre-eternity, making existence impossible without a beginning.

Core Idea
Infinite cycles collapse into simultaneous non-existences — making a beginning necessary.

Razi now turns from general causal chains to the specific case of celestial cycles. He shows that if the universe had no beginning, then every cycle would be preceded by an infinite string of non-existences. But these non-existences would all have to coexist in pre-eternity, and existence could never arise alongside them. This forces the conclusion that the universe must have a starting point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure of the proof.
The passage uses a dichotomy: either existence occurs with the non-existences or it does not.
2
Understand the assumption
Razi assumes that every celestial cycle is preceded by another cycle, with no first cycle. This means an infinite regress of cycles.
3
Identify the key consequence
Each cycle is preceded by a non-existence. Since there are infinitely many cycles, all those non-existences are gathered in pre-eternity (al-azal). This is the crucial step.
The non-existences are not ordered; they all coexist in pre-eternity.
4
Follow the dichotomy
Razi then asks: either some existence occurs together with those gathered non-existences in pre-eternity, or no existence occurs. The first is impossible because it would make the prior (non-existence) simultaneous with the posterior (existence). Therefore, the second must hold.
This refutes the possibility of existence in pre-eternity.
5
Grasp the conclusion
If no existence occurred in pre-eternity alongside the infinite non-existences, then all existences must have a beginning. Thus, the universe is temporally originated.
This proves that bodies (including celestial spheres) are not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني : : أنا إذا افترضنا أن كل دورة من دورات الفلك كانت مسبوقة بدورة أخرى، لا إلى أول فحينئذ تكون كل واحدة من تلك الدورات مسبوقة بعدم لا أول له، فتكون العدمات بأسرها مجتمعة في الأزل وإنما الترتيب في الوجودات، لا جميع العدمات السابقة على كل واحدة من هذه الوجودات في العدمات . فإذن كانت مجتمعة في الأزل. فإما أن يحصل مع مجموع تلك العدمات الحاصلة في الأزل شيء من الوجودات، أو لم يحصل. والأول باطل وإلا لزم أن يكون السابق مقارنا للمسبوق، وهو محال. وإذا بطل القسم الأول تعين القسم الثاني، وهو أنه لم يحصل في الأزل شيء من الوجودات. وذلك يقتضي أن يكون لمجموع الوجودات بداية وأول . وهو المطلوب.
The second proof: If we assume that every cycle of the celestial spheres is preceded by another cycle, without a first, then each one of those cycles is preceded by a non-existence that has no beginning. Thus all the non-existences (ʿadamāt) as a whole are gathered in pre-eternity—rather the order is in the existences, not all of the prior non-existences preceding each one of these existences among the non-existences. So they are gathered in pre-eternity. Then either something of the existences occurs with the totality of those non-existences occurring in pre-eternity, or it does not. The first is false, otherwise it would require that the prior be simultaneous with the posterior, which is absurd. And if the first division is invalid, the second division is determined, which is that no existence occurred in pre-eternity. That necessitates that the totality of existences has a beginning and a first. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents the second independent proof for the temporal origination of bodies, focusing on celestial cycles. Razi argues that if celestial cycles (dawrāt al-falak) were infinite with no first cycle, then each cycle would be preceded by an infinite series of non-existences (ʿadamāt) that have no beginning. All these non-existences would be gathered together in pre-eternity (al-azal). Now, in that state of pure non-existence, either some existence occurs together with it or not. If existence occurs, that would mean the prior (the non-existence) coexists with the posterior (the existence), which is absurd. So, no existence can occur in pre-eternity alongside the infinite non-existences. Therefore, all existences must have a beginning and a first moment. This proves that bodies are temporally originated (ḥādith) and not eternal.

Key Terms
دورة
فلك
عدم
أزل
مسبوق
مقارن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think infinite regress is possible if we only consider existence, ignoring the non-existences.
Correction: Razi shows that when we consider the non-existences that precede each cycle, they all accumulate in pre-eternity, making it impossible for any existence to begin.

Checkpoint

A) All cycles would be equally old
B) All prior non-existences would be gathered in pre-eternity
C) The cycles would eventually reach a first cycle
D) The cycles would be finite

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress in cycles forces all prior non-existences to coincide, making the start of existence necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your existence is preceded by an infinite chain of non-existence, how can you truly claim your own existence has a foundation? Razi's argument forces you to admit a first moment—have you ever lived as if your actions have an ultimate beginning before Allah?
?
Are you comfortable with the idea that the universe cannot be eternal? How does this proof challenge any hidden assumption you hold that the world is self-sufficient or that your life is just a cycle without a Creator?
?
The proof shows that if cycles were infinite, all non-existences would coincide in pre-eternity. Consider your own sins and failures: if left unrepented, do you treat them as if they are beginningless cycles that can never be broken? How does Razi's logic expose the need for a decisive first step of repentance?

The Third Proof: Dichotomy of Motion in Eternity
Razi argues that motion must have a beginning via dichotomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that motion must have a beginning, based on the dichotomy of its existence in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Both possibilities of motion in eternity lead to a first beginning.

This passage presents Imam Razi's third proof for the temporal origination of the world. He uses a simple but powerful dichotomy: either motion existed in pre-eternity or it did not. By examining both cases, he shows that each inevitably leads to the conclusion that motion has a first beginning. This argument is foundational in proving that the universe is not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure of the dichotomy.
The passage is short but packed with logic.
2
Identify the two branches
Find the first branch: no motion in pre-eternity. Find the second branch: some motion in pre-eternity. Note how each branch is handled.
3
Understand the second branch's sub-dichotomy
Within the second branch, the motion is either the first or preceded. See how both sub-options lead to a beginning.
The second sub-option (preceded) leads to a contradiction.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Both branches end with a first motion, proving temporal origination. Recognize the strength of the argument.
This shows that motion cannot be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث : وهو أنه إما أن يقال : حصل في الأزل شيء من هذه الحركات أو لم يحصل. فإن لم يحصل في الأزل شيء من هذه الحركات والحوادث، وجب أن تكون لمجموع هذه الحركات والحوادث بداية وأول. وهو المطلوب. وإن حصل في الأزل شيء من هذه الحركات، فتلك الحركة الحاصلة في الأزل إن لم تكن مسبوقة بغيرها كانت تلك الحركة أول الحركات وهو المطلوب. وإن كانت مسبوقة بغيرها لزم أن يكون الأزل مسبوقاً بغيره. وهو محال.
The third proof: It is that either we say: something of these motions occurred in pre-eternity or it did not. If nothing of these motions and events occurred in pre-eternity, then the totality of these motions and events must have a beginning and a first. That is what is sought. And if something of these motions occurred in pre-eternity, then that motion occurring in pre-eternity—if it is not preceded by another, then that motion is the first motion, which is what is sought. And if it is preceded by another, then it would require that pre-eternity is preceded by something else, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, which is a simple argument from the dichotomy of motion existing in pre-eternity or not. Razi considers two possibilities: either some motion occurs in pre-eternity (al-azal) or no motion occurs. If no motion occurs, then all motions must have a first beginning, proving temporal origination. If some motion does occur in pre-eternity, that specific motion is either the first (not preceded) or it is preceded. If it is the first, then we have a first motion. If it is preceded, then pre-eternity itself would be preceded by something, which is contradictory to the definition of pre-eternity. Therefore, both possibilities lead to the conclusion that motion has a beginning.

Key Terms
حركة
حادث
أزل
بداية
أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a motion exists in pre-eternity, it could be part of an infinite chain.
Correction: Razi shows that any motion in pre-eternity either is the first or leads to a contradiction, so infinite chain is impossible.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what happens if a motion exists in pre-eternity and is preceded by another motion?
A) It becomes the first motion
B) It implies pre-eternity is preceded by something, which is absurd
C) It shows that motion is eternal
D) It proves the existence of God

Key Takeaway
The dichotomy shows that motion cannot be eternal, proving temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every motion and event has a beginning, what does that mean for your own existence and the moments of your life? Are you living as if your time is a gift with a clear start and end, or do you act as if it's eternal and endless? Commit today to truly internalize that your life has a beginning and will have an end, and let that urgency shape your priorities.
?
Razi shows that even the possibility of an infinite past leads to absurdity. Yet, you often act as if you have infinite tomorrows. What specific change in your daily routine would prove that you actually believe this truth? Write down one concrete action you will take this week to honor the fact that your time is finite and precious.
?
The proof forces you to acknowledge a first motion, a first cause. In your own life, have you truly submitted to the reality that your existence is not self-originating? What would it look like for you to live from that moment of submission today, not just intellectually but in your choices and dependencies? The Fourth Proof: Planetary Cycles Prove Finite Motions
Razi uses planetary cycle ratio to prove finite number of motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the ratio of planetary cycles to rationally prove that all celestial motions have a beginning.

Core Idea
Fewer cycles means finite — one finite motion implies all motions are finite.

Imam Razi presents his fourth proof for the temporal origination of the universe. Using an observable astronomical ratio, he argues that if one celestial motion has a finite number of cycles, then all motions must be finite. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of this concise yet powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical chain from observation to conclusion.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the empirical premise
Razi states that Saturn completes one cycle in the time the Sun completes thirty cycles. This is the observed ratio.
3
Understand the finiteness argument
Since Saturn's cycles are fewer than the Sun's, they are finite. Then he generalizes: if one motion is finite, all are finite because repeated finite multiples remain finite.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how the argument moves from observation to a universal conclusion. Is the generalization justified?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الرابع : أن في مدة دورة واحدة من أدوار (زحل) تتحرك الشمس ثلاثين دورة. فإذن عدد دورات (زحل) تكون أقل من دورات (الشمس) وكل ما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه. فإذن عدد دوراته متناه، فلحركة (زحل) بداية . وإذا كان كذلك، وجب أن يكون أيضًا لجميع الحركات بداية لأن ضعف المتناهي مرارًا متناهية، يكون متناهيا.
The fourth proof: In the period of one cycle of (Saturn)'s cycles, the sun moves thirty cycles. Therefore, the number of Saturn's cycles is less than the Sun's cycles. Everything that is less than something else is finite (mutanāh). Therefore, Saturn's number of cycles is finite, so Saturn's motion has a beginning. If that is the case, then all motions must also have a beginning, because the multiple of a finite number a finite number of times is finite.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, an argument from the observable ratio of planetary cycles. Razi notes that in the time it takes Saturn to complete one cycle, the Sun completes thirty cycles. Therefore, the number of Saturn's cycles must be less than the number of the Sun's cycles. Since something that is less than something else is finite (mutanāh), Saturn's cycles are finite, meaning Saturn's motion has a beginning. If one motion has a beginning, then all motions must have a beginning because multiplying a finite number a finite number of times still yields a finite number. Thus, all celestial motions are temporally originated.

Key Terms
دورة
زحل
شمس
متناه
بداية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ratio only shows relative finiteness (Saturn's cycles are fewer than Sun's) but does not prove absolute finiteness of either.
Correction: Razi argues that if Saturn's cycles are fewer than something else, they must be finite because an infinite cannot be fewer than another infinite. The principle 'what is less than something else is finite' establishes absolute finiteness.

Checkpoint
What empirical observation does Razi use in the fourth proof?
A) The moon orbits the earth once a month
B) Saturn completes one cycle for every thirty cycles of the Sun
C) The stars are fixed in their positions
D) The Earth is stationary

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth proof shows that an observable ratio between planetary cycles leads to the conclusion that celestial motions are finite and created.

إذا كانت حركات الكواكب الهائلة لها بداية، فماذا عن حياتك اليومية التي تعيشها كما لو أنها أبدية؟ هل تتصرف بناءً على أن لكل شيء بداية؟ ما الذي يمنعك من العيش بوعي أن أفعالك لها بداية ونهاية؟

رازي يستخدم رصدًا فلكيًا للوصول إلى حقيقة غيبية. هل هناك ملاحظات في حياتك تشير إلى أن كل شيء محدود، ولكنك تتجاهلها؟ كيف يمكنك أن تتعامل مع هذا البرهان كتذكير شخصي بأنك مخلوق محدود؟

The Fifth Proof: Finite Past via Mental Mapping
Razi uses imaginary mapping to prove that the past must be finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fifth proof that the series of past events must be finite, using an imaginary comparison of two infinite sets.

Core Idea
If the past were infinite, a larger infinite set would equal a smaller one, which is impossible.

Imam Razi now presents a subtle yet powerful argument for the finitude of past events. He asks us to imagine two sets of events: one from today back to pre-eternity and another from the time of the Flood back to pre-eternity. Through a mental mapping exercise, he shows that the only consistent conclusion is that both sets have a beginning. This proof avoids direct engagement with the infinite and uses logical impossibility instead.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two sets
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the two sets: Set A from today to pre-eternity, Set B from the Flood to pre-eternity. Note that Set A is larger by the interval from Flood to today.
The sets are imaginary, not actual historical lists.
2
Visualize the mental superposition
Imagine aligning the two sets at their finite ends (today and the Flood). Each event in one set is paired with an event in the other set that shares the same relative position from the starting point.
This is a thought experiment to compare lengths.
3
Consider the two possibilities
If the smaller set (B) does not end when we reach the other end, then it would be as long as the larger set, contradicting that one is larger. If it does end, then it is finite from that side, so it has a beginning.
Razi shows the first possibility is absurd, leaving the second.
4
Conclude finiteness of all past events
Since Set B is finite from the pre-eternity side, and Set A adds a finite extra amount (Flood to today), Set A is also finite. Therefore, all past events are finite, proving the universe had a beginning.
This proof relies on logical impossibility, not empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس : إذا فرضنا الحوادث الماضية من اليوم إلى الأزل جملة، ومن زمان الطوفان إلى الأزل جملة أخرى . فلا شك أن الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية، بما بين زمان الطوفان إلى هذا اليوم. فإذا طبقنا في الوهم الطرف المتناهي من الجملة الزائدة على الطرف المتناهي من الجملة الناقصة، حتى يقابل كل فرد من أفراد إحدى الجملتين بما يشابهه في المرتبة من الجملة الأخرى. فإن لم تنقض الجملة الناقصة عن الزائدة في الطرف الآخر، كان الشيء مع غيره كهو لا مع غيره. وهذا محال . وإن انقطعت الجملة الناقصة من ذلك الطرف، كانت متناهية من ذلك الطرف، وكانت متناهية من جانب الأزل. والزائد زاد عليها بمقدار متناه، والزائد على المتناهي بمقدار متناه يكون متناهيا . فالكل متناه في جانب الأزل.
The fifth proof: If we suppose the past events from today to pre-eternity as one set, and from the time of the Flood to pre-eternity as another set, there is no doubt that the first set is greater than the second set by the interval between the time of the Flood and today. Then if we mentally superimpose the finite end of the larger set onto the finite end of the smaller set, so that each individual of one set corresponds to its counterpart in rank from the other set, then if the smaller set does not fall short of the larger set at the other end, it would mean that a thing with another is like it without another, which is absurd. And if the smaller set ends at that end, then it is finite from that end, and it is finite from the side of pre-eternity. And what was added (to the larger set) is a finite amount, and that which is added to the finite by a finite amount is finite. So the whole is finite on the side of pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, a sophisticated argument using the comparison of two infinite series. Razi considers two sets of past events: one from today back to eternity, and another from the time of the Flood back to eternity. The first set is larger because it includes the extra time from the Flood to today. Now, imagine aligning the two sets at their finite ends (today and the Flood). If we try to map each event in the smaller set to a corresponding event in the larger set, one of two possibilities occurs: either the smaller set ends before the larger set (meaning it has a limit) or it does not end. If it does not end, then the smaller set would be equal to the larger set, which contradicts that one is larger. So the smaller set must end, meaning it is finite. Therefore, the larger set, which adds a finite amount to the finite smaller set, is also finite. Thus, all past events are finite, proving that the universe had a beginning.

Key Terms
حوادث
أزل
جملة
متناه
زيادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite sets cannot have different sizes; all infinite sets are equal in size.
Correction: While some infinite sets have the same cardinality, Razi's argument does not rely on cardinality but on logical impossibility: if one set is larger than another, the smaller must have an end, or else they would be equal.

Checkpoint
In the fifth proof, why must the smaller set (from the Flood to eternity) be finite?
A) Because it is a subset of a larger set
B) Because if it did not end, it would equal the larger set, which is impossible
C) Because the Flood was a historical event
D) Because Allah says so in the Quran

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof shows that the series of past events must be finite because comparing two different starting points leads to the unavoidable conclusion that the smaller set has a beginning, and thus the larger set does too.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the past is finite and the universe had a beginning, then you are a relatively recent creation. How should this affect your sense of purpose and urgency in your daily life? Where are you living as if you have endless time?
?
Razi's proof shows that an infinite chain of events is impossible. Is there any habit, sin, or excuse in your life that you treat as 'infinite'—something you think you can always deal with later? What would it look like to confront that today as if your time were finite?
?
The argument forces you to accept that there was a first moment. If God created time itself, then every moment is a gift. How much of your attention do you give to the Giver of each moment? What one change can you make today to live in more conscious awareness of the Creator who brought time into being?

The Sixth Proof: Finitude of the Past
Razi's sixth proof and conclusion of all proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument that the existence of today proves the past is finite, and how this supports the temporal origination of the universe.

Core Idea
An infinite past cannot be completed, so today could not exist.

This passage presents the final argument in a series of proofs for the temporal origination of the world. Razi uses a logical reductio: if the past were infinite, today could never arrive. Since today is here, the past must be finite. This proof concludes the case against the eternity of the universe.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the full argument.
Notice the conditional structure starting with 'law' (if).
2
Identify the conditional and its consequence
Find the if-statement: 'If past cycles were infinite...' and trace the logical consequence: today depends on completing the infinite.
3
Understand why the condition is impossible
Razi argues that completing what has no end is impossible. Ask yourself why that is logically impossible.
This is the key move in the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that since today exists, the past must be finite. Then Razi applies this to all bodies: they cannot be eternal.
This connects the physical world to a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان السادس : لو كانت الأدوار الماضية غير متناهية، لكان حدوث اليوم موقوفا على انقضاء ما لا نهاية له وانقضاء ما لا نهاية له ،محال فيلزم أن يكون حدوث اليوم موقوفًا على شرط محال، والموقوف على الشرط المحال، لا يوجد. فكان يلزم أن لا يوجد اليوم وحيث وجد علمنا أن الأمور المنقضية قبل هذا اليوم متناهية، فثبت بهذه البراهين أن القول يكون الأجسام متحركة في الأزل محال.
The sixth proof: If the past cycles were infinite, then the occurrence of today would be dependent upon the completion of what has no end, and the completion of what has no end is impossible. So it follows that the occurrence of today is dependent upon an impossible condition, and what is dependent upon an impossible condition does not exist. Therefore, it would follow that today does not exist. And since today does exist, we know that the things that have passed before today are finite. Thus it is established by these proofs that the statement that bodies are moving in pre-eternity is impossible.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth and final proof, followed by the conclusion of all proofs. Razi argues that if past cycles were infinite (ghayr mutanāhiya), then for today to occur, an infinite number of past events would have to be completed. But completing an infinite is impossible. Therefore, today's occurrence depends on an impossible condition, so it could not exist. Since today does exist, the past must be finite. Finally, Razi states that all these proofs establish that bodies cannot be moving in pre-eternity, meaning they are temporally originated.

Key Terms
أدوار ماضية
متناهية
انقضاء
شرط محال
حدوث اليوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that today could occur despite an infinite past if time is not understood as a succession of events.
Correction: Razi's argument does not depend on a particular theory of time; it relies on the logical principle that completing an infinite series is inherently impossible, regardless of how time is conceived.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what would be the consequence if past cycles were infinite?
A) Today would occur normally
B) Today would not exist
C) The cycles would repeat
D) The universe would be eternal

Key Takeaway
Razi's sixth proof shows that if the past were infinite, today could never exist; since today exists, the past must be finite, proving the world is created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you wake up each morning, do you take for granted that you are here, or do you realize that your existence is contingent on the impossible being avoided? How does this proof change your awareness of time and your accountability before the One who initiated it?
?
The proof shows that without a beginning, you could not exist today. Reflect on the fact that you are here not by chance but by necessity from the First Cause. What does that mean for how you live this finite life? Are you acting as if you have infinite tomorrows? How should this urgency reshape your priorities today?
?
If the past were infinite, today would be impossible. Yet you are reading this now. How does that logical certainty press you to submit to the One who made today possible? What are you doing today that acknowledges that your time is a gift from a Creator who acts in time? Will you live this day with gratitude and purpose, or will you waste it as if it were eternal?

Rational Judgments

Refutation of the objection that an eternal body need not be in motion or at rest
Razi refutes the objection by analyzing the concept of place (hayyiz).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that an eternal body need not be in motion or at rest by analyzing the concept of place (hayyiz) and demonstrating that it leads to impossible consequences.

Core Idea
The concept of 'place' is not self-evident; its analysis destroys the objection.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection-and-answer. The objector denies that an eternal body must be either moving or at rest, questioning the premise that every body occupies a place. Razi responds by subjecting the notion of 'place' (hayyiz) to a thorough conceptual analysis, showing that whether place is non-existent or existent, pointable or not, independent or dependent, it leads to impossibility. This refutation demonstrates the rigorous method of kalam in examining foundational concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Razi's response
Read the Arabic passage and translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: objection, then analysis of 'place'.
2
Understand the first division: place as non-existent or existent
Razi divides the possible meanings of place into non-existent and existent, and shows the first option is absurd.
Think about why a body cannot be in nothingness.
3
Analyze the existent place cases
Follow Razi's reasoning for each subtype: if place is existent but not pointable, if it is pointable independently (body), or dependently (accident).
Note the conclusions: interpenetration, infinite regress, circularity.
4
Conclude why the objection fails
Razi concludes that the concept of place is unintelligible, so the division based on it is invalid.
This reinforces the rational necessity of motion or rest for a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا نسلم أن الجسم لو كان أزليّاً لكان إمَّا متحركًا وإما ساكنا . قوله : والدليل عليه وهو أن كل جسم، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون حاصلا في حيز، وإذا كان كذلك، فإن بقي مستقرًا فيه، فهو الساكن، وإن لم يبق مستقرا فيه، فهو المتحرك). قلنا : هذا التقسيم بناء على قولكم : إن كل جسم فإنه لا بد وأن يكون حاصلا في الحيز، فما المراد من الحيز الذي جعلتموه ظرفًا للجسم. وتقديره أن المسمى بالحيز، إما أن يكون معدوما أو موجودًا ، فإن كان معدوماً كان نفيًا محضًا، وعدما صرفا . والقول يكون الجسم حاصلا في العدم المحض، والنفي الصرف غير معقول وأما إن كان المسمى بالحيز أمرًا موجودًا فذلك الموجود إما أن لا يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، أو يمكن فإن لم يمكن استحال حصول الجسم فيه . لأن الجسم الموجود يجب أن يكون بحيث يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية . فإذا كان المسمى بالحيز موجودًا ، ويمتنع أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، كان القول يجعل الحيز ظرفًا للجسم : جمعا بين النقيضين . وهو محال. وإما إن كان المسمى بالحيز أمرًا يشار إليه إشارة حسية، فكونه كذلك إما أن يكون بالاستقلال أو التبعية. فإن كان بالاستقلال كان المسمى بالحيز جسما . والقول يكون الجسم حاصلا في الحيز، يرجع حاصله إلى كون الجسم حاصلا في الجسم، ثم المراد من هذه الظرفية إن كان هو الحلول كان ذلك قولا بتداخل الأجسام. وهو محال. وإن كان المراد منه هو المماسة، لزم أن يكون جسم مماس جسم آخر. وذلك يوجب القول بوجود أجسام لا نهاية لها وأنتم تنكرون ذلك. وأما إن كان المسمى بالحيز أمرًا مشارًا إليه إشارة حسية على سبيل التبعية. فذلك هو العرض. فيرجع حاصل قولنا الجسم في الحيز أن الجسم حاصل في العرض، إلا أن ذلك أيضًا باطل من وجهين : المقدمة الثالثة أحدهما : أن العرض حاصل في الجسم، فلو كان الجسم حاصلا في العرض لزم الدور. والثاني : أنكم تقولون الجسم انتقل من هذا الحيز إلى آخر فلو كان المراد من الحيز هو العرض، لزم أن يكون المراد من قولنا : انتقل الجسم من حيز إلى حيز، هو أنه انتقل من عرض إلى عرض وكل ذلك محال فثبت أن القول بكون الجسم حاصلا في الحيز، أمر باطل محال . فكان التقسيم الذي فَرَّعْتُمُوه عليه أولى بالاستحالة والفساد.
If it is said: We do not accept that if the body were eternal it would be either moving or at rest. He (the objector) says: The evidence for it is that every body must occupy a place (hayyiz), and if that is so, then if it remains stable in it, it is at rest (sākin), and if it does not remain stable in it, it is moving (mutaharrik). We say: This division is based on your statement that every body must occupy a place (hayyiz). What is meant by the place (hayyiz) which you have made a container for the body? Its estimation is that what is called place (hayyiz) is either non-existent (ma'dūm) or existent (mawjūd). If it is non-existent, it is pure negation and absolute non-being (adam). And the statement that a body exists in pure non-being and absolute negation is unintelligible. As for if what is called place is an existent thing, then that existent is either such that it cannot be pointed to by sensory indication (ishāra ḥissiyya) or it can be. If it cannot, then it is impossible for the body to exist in it, because an existent body must be such that it can be pointed to by sensory indication. So if what is called place is existent but it is impossible to point to it sensibly, then the statement making the place a container for the body would be a combination of two contradictories, which is impossible. And if what is called place is a thing that can be pointed to by sensory indication, then its being so is either independently (istiqlāl) or dependently (taba'iyya). If it is independently, then what is called place is a body (jism). Then the statement that the body exists in the place reduces to the body existing in a body. Then if what is meant by this container is indwelling (ḥulūl), that is a statement of interpenetration of bodies (tadākhul al-ajsām), which is impossible. And if what is meant is contact (mumāssa), then it follows that one body contacts another body, and that necessitates the existence of infinite bodies, which you deny. And if what is called place is a thing that can be pointed to by sensory indication dependently, then that is an accident ('araḍ). So the content of our statement 'the body is in the place' reduces to the body existing in an accident. However, that too is invalid for two reasons: The third introduction: First: the accident exists in the body, so if the body existed in the accident, a circularity (dawr) would follow. Second: you say the body moves from one place to another; if what is meant by place is the accident, then what is meant by 'the body moved from place to place' would be that it moved from accident to accident, and all of that is impossible. So it is established that the statement that a body occupies a place is false and impossible. Therefore the division you built upon it is more deserving of impossibility and corruption.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector denies that an eternal body must be either moving or at rest, because the evidence for that—namely, that every body must occupy a place—is problematic. The objector implies that the concept of 'place' (hayyiz) is not clear. Razi responds by analyzing the possible meanings of 'place'. He divides it into non-existent and existent. If place is non-existent, it is absurd for a body to be in nothingness. If place is existent, it either cannot be pointed to (impossible, since a body must be pointable) or can be pointed to. If pointable, it is either an independent entity (a body) or a dependent entity (an accident). If it is a body, then 'body in place' means 'body in body', leading either to interpenetration (impossible) or contact implying infinite bodies (denied by the objector). If it is an accident, then 'body in place' means 'body in an accident', but an accident exists in a body, so this causes circularity (accident in body and body in accident). Also, movement from place to place would mean movement from accident to accident, which is nonsensical. Thus all options are impossible, so the original division of body into moving or at rest based on being in a place is invalid. Razi concludes that the objection's foundation is corrupt.

Key Terms
حيز
سكون
حركة
جسم
أزلي
ظرف
حلول
مماسة
عرض
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is simply denying the premise without justification.
Correction: Razi shows that the objection is based on an unclear concept of 'place', and he systematically demonstrates that any coherent definition leads to impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the first problem Razi raises with the concept of place?
A) If place is non-existent, it is unintelligible for a body to be in it.
B) If place is existent, it cannot be a body.
C) If place is existent, it must be an accident.
D) If place is non-existent, it is a dimension.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the concept of 'place' cannot be coherently defined, thus the objection based on it collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What concept in your own life—perhaps your idea of 'success,' 'purpose,' or 'identity'—do you assume is clear and unproblematic, yet might collapse if you analyzed it as ruthlessly as Razi analyzed 'place'? Are you willing to subject it to such scrutiny today?
?
Razi's argument shows that a seemingly simple starting point (every body must be in a place) leads to absurdity. When you build your beliefs on an unexamined foundation, you risk building on sand. What foundational belief are you holding that you have never questioned? Will you dig into it now, or wait until it crumbles?
