A Body at Its First Moment: Neither Moving nor at Rest
Proof that a body at origination is neither moving nor at rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why at the moment of its origination a body is neither moving nor at rest, and how this refutes the claim that there is no third possibility.

Core Idea
Motion and rest require a prior state; origination has none.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi argues against a commonly held assumption: that a body must always be either in motion or at rest. He concedes the concept of place (hayyiz) but shows that motion and rest are defined relative to a previous state. Therefore, a body at the first moment of its existence, having no prior state, falls into neither category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how the author structures his argument: concession, definition, conditional, conclusion.
The passage is a tightly reasoned kalam proof.
2
Analyze the definitions of motion and rest
Al-Razi defines motion as coming to be in a different place than before, and rest as coming to be in the same place as before. What do both definitions have in common?
They both refer to a prior state of the body.
3
Trace the conclusion
Since motion and rest require a prior state, at the first moment of existence there is no prior state. Therefore the body can be neither moving nor at rest. This refutes the claim that there is no intermediate.
The argument is simple but powerful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن القول بالحيز معقول . فلم قلتم : إن الجسم لا بد وأن يكون إما : ساكنا في الحيز، وإما متحركًا ؟ بيانه أن المتحرك هو الذي حصل في الحيز بعد أن كان في حيز آخر ، والساكن هو الذي حصل في الحيز، بعد أن كان في نفس ذلك الحيز. فإذن كونه ساكنا متحركًا مشروط بكونه موجودًا قبل ذلك . فإذن الجسم في أول حدوثه، يجب أن لا يكون ساكنا ولا متحركًا، وإذا ثبت هذا بطل قولكم إنه لا واسطة بين كون الجسم ساكنا أو متحركًا.
We grant that the statement about place (hayyiz) is intelligible. So why do you say that a body must necessarily be either at rest in the place or moving? Its explanation is that the moving thing is what comes to be in a place after having been in another place, and the resting thing is what comes to be in a place after having been in that same place. Therefore, its being resting or moving is conditioned by its existing prior to that. So at the first moment of its origination, the body must be neither resting nor moving. And if this is established, your statement that there is no intermediate between a body being resting or moving becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a response to the claim that a body must be either at rest or in motion. The author concedes the concept of place (hayyiz) but argues that motion and rest are defined relative to a prior state: motion is when a body is in a different place from before, rest when it is in the same place. Thus, both concepts presuppose existence prior to the current state. Therefore, at the very first moment of a body's existence, it has no prior state, so it is neither moving nor at rest. This refutes the claim that there is no third possibility (wāsiṭa) between motion and rest.

Key Terms
حركة (haraka)
سكون (sukūn)
حيز (hayyiz)
حدوث (huduth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body must always be either in motion or at rest at any given moment.
Correction: This is false for the first moment of a body's existence, because motion and rest are defined relative to a prior state, which does not exist at origination.

Checkpoint
Why is a body at the moment of its origination neither moving nor at rest?
A) Because motion and rest are impossible for a new body.
B) Because motion and rest are defined relative to a prior state, which does not exist at origination.
C) Because the body is not yet in a place.
D) Because motion and rest are purely accidental and not essential.

Key Takeaway
At the first moment of a body's existence, it cannot be classified as moving or at rest because both require a prior state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence began at a moment when you were neither moving nor at rest. How does that awareness change how you view your every subsequent action and stillness as a gift from Allah?
?
If at your first moment you had no prior state, what does that say about your absolute dependence on the One who gave you existence? Are you living as if you are self-sufficient?
?
When you consider that even being 'still' requires a prior state, how does that humble you in moments when you think you have achieved something by your own effort?

Refutation that Rest is a Positive Attribute
Two proofs that rest is not a positive attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rest is not a positive attribute, using the example of impossibility and possibility to show that change of states does not require a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Change of states does not prove a positive attribute.

Imam Razi continues his discussion on divine attributes by addressing the claim that rest (sukūn) is a positive attribute. He grants that motion in eternity is impossible but questions why rest cannot be eternal. The opponent argues that rest must be positive because a body changes from rest to motion. Razi counters with two arguments, the first of which is fully preserved, using the concepts of impossibility and possibility to demonstrate that change does not imply positivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the structure: Razi grants a premise, then refutes the opponent's proof.
The passage is cut off at the end; focus on the first proof.
2
Understand the opponent's claim and proof
Identify the opponent's argument: rest is a positive attribute because change from rest to motion implies one state is positive. Razi denies this.
The opponent uses the principle that change implies a positive attribute.
3
Analyze Razi's first proof
Study the analogy with impossibility (imtina') and possibility (imkan). Razi shows that these change yet are not positive, so the same applies to rest and motion.
Impossibility cannot be positive because it would make the impossible exist; possibility leads to regress.
4
Consider the incomplete second proof
Note that the second proof is cut off. It begins by arguing that change does not necessitate a new occurrence in the subject, using divine knowledge as an example.
The rest of the argument is not available in this source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متحركًا في الأزل. فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ساكنا ؟ قوله : (لأن السكون أمر ثبوتي والثابت الأزلي يمتنع زواله) قلنا : لا نسلم أن السكون أمر ثبوتي. وقوله : (الدليل عليه أن الجسم يتحرك بعد أن كان ساكنا، وبالعكس. وذلك يوجب كون إحدى الحالتين أمرًا ثبوتيًا). نقول : لا نسلم أن تبدل إحدى الحالتين بالأخرى، يوجب كون إحدى الحالتين ثبوتية والذي يدل عليه وجوه : • أحدها: أن الحوادث عندكم ممتنعة الحدوث في الأزل، ثم انقلبت جائزة الحدوث فيما لا يزال فههنا تبدل الامتناع بالصحة، مع أنه لا يصح أن يكون الامتناع أمرًا ثبوتيًا. وتكون الصحة أمرًا ثبوتيًا. أمَّا أن الامتناع لا يكون أمرًا ثبوتيًا فلأن الامتناع لو كان أمرًا ثبوتيّا، لكان الموصوف به ثبوتيًا، فيلزم أن يكون ممتنع الثبوت ثابتًا، وهو محال . وأما أن الإمكان لا يمكن أن يكون أمرًا ثبوتيًا. فلذلك لأنه لو كان أمرًا ثبوتيًا لا متنع كونه واجبًا لذاته، بل يكون ممكنا لذاته . فيكون إمكانه زائدا عليه. ويلزم التسلسل. والوجه الثاني : في بيان أن تبدل إحدى الحالتين بالأخرى، لا يقتضي كون إحدى الحالتين أمرًا ثبوتيًا، ولا تجدد أمر في المحل : ذلك لأنه قبل حصول الحادث المعين، لا يصدق أن الله تعالى عالم بحصوله في هذا الوقت لأنه لو صدق عليه أنه عالم بحصوله في ذلك الوقت مع أنه غير حاصل في ذلك الوقت، لكان ذلك جهلاً. وهو على الله
We grant that the body is impossible to be moving in eternity. So why is it not permissible for it to be at rest? He says: 'Because rest is a positive attribute (amr thubuti), and an eternal positive attribute cannot cease.' We say: We do not grant that rest is a positive attribute. And he says: 'The proof for it is that the body moves after having been at rest, and vice versa; that necessitates that one of the two states is a positive attribute.' We say: We do not grant that the change of one state into another necessitates that one of the two states is positive. What indicates this are aspects: One of them: that contingent events (hawadith), according to you, are impossible in eternity, then they become possible in the future (ma la yazal); here there is a change from impossibility to possibility, while impossibility cannot be a positive attribute, and possibility is a positive attribute. As for impossibility not being a positive attribute, because if impossibility were a positive attribute, then that which is described by it would be positive, necessitating that the impossible of existence be existent, which is absurd. And as for possibility not being able to be a positive attribute, because if it were a positive attribute, it would be impossible for it to be necessary in itself; rather it would be possible in itself, so its possibility would be additional to it, leading to infinite regress. The second aspect: in explaining that the change of one state into another does not necessitate that one of the two states is a positive attribute, nor does it necessitate a new occurrence in the subject: that is because before the occurrence of a particular contingent event, it is not true that God is knowing of its occurrence at that time, because if it were true that He is knowing of its occurrence at that time while it is not occurring at that time, that would be ignorance. And upon God
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that rest (sukūn) is a positive attribute (amr thubuti), thereby allowing that a body could be at rest in eternity. The author grants that motion in eternity is impossible (as conceded from earlier discussion) but asks why rest cannot occur. The opponent argues that rest is a positive attribute, and an eternal positive attribute cannot cease (otherwise it would change, implying imperfection). The author denies that rest is a positive attribute. The opponent offers a proof: because a body can change from rest to motion, one of the two states must be positive (since change implies coming into existence of something). The author responds with two proofs (wajh). The first proof uses the example of impossibility (imtina') and possibility (imkan) in the context of eternal events: according to the opponent, events are impossible in eternity but become possible in the future—this is a change from impossibility to possibility, yet impossibility is not a positive attribute (if it were, the impossible would exist), and possibility is not a positive attribute either (if it were, it would lead to regress). Therefore, change of states does not require a positive attribute. The second proof, which is cut off, argues that the change of states does not necessitate a new occurrence in the subject, using the example of God's knowledge: before an event occurs, God is not said to know it as occurring at that time (because that would be ignorance); the implication is that change in the object does not entail a change in the subject's attribute. The argument is incomplete but the first proof is fully presented.

Key Terms
سكون (sukūn)
أمر ثبوتي (amr thubuti)
تبدل (tabaddul)
امتناع (imtina')
إمكان (imkan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a state changes, the original state must have been a real positive entity rather than a mere negation or absence.
Correction: Change does not imply positivity; impossibility changes to possibility, yet neither is a positive attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof given to show that change of states does not require one state to be a positive attribute?
A) The change from impossibility to possibility in the case of events in eternity shows that neither is a positive attribute.
B) Rest and motion are both positive but one is negated by the other.
C) God's knowledge prevents change.
D) The body has no prior state.

Key Takeaway
Rest is not a positive attribute; change can occur between non-positive states, as shown by the analogy with impossibility and possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that observable changes in your life—like a change from ignorance to knowledge—require that you had a real 'quality' of ignorance. But the lesson shows that change can happen between non-positive states. Where in your own understanding of yourself have you attached too much reality to temporary states, as if they were fixed attributes? Will you begin to see them as mere negations or possibilities that Allah can transform without them being real entities in you?
?
Consider the example of God's knowledge: before an event, it is not true that God knows it at that specific time. How does this shape your confidence that God's knowledge is complete and unchanging, even when your own life seems full of change? Are you willing to trust that Allah's knowledge of your future does not depend on a positive attribute that changes?
?
This passage refutes the idea that rest is a positive attribute. Reflect on a 'rest' in your spiritual life—a period where you felt stagnant or inactive. Did you view that rest as a real, fixed defect in yourself? How can you now see it as simply the absence of motion, not a permanent identity, so that you can move forward without being burdened by a false sense of an unchanging negative state? Objection and Answer: Defining Motion and Rest
Razi answers an objection about infinite divisibility and defines motion and rest using necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi grounds the definitions of motion and rest in necessary knowledge, avoiding the need for a deeper metaphysical definition of place.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are defined by simple sense perception, not complex philosophy.

Razi presents an objection to a previous introduction that assumed two sides in every extended thing, leading to infinite divisibility. He then answers by appealing to what everyone knows necessarily: that every extended object is perceived as 'here' or 'there'. From this simple fact, he derives the definitions of motion and rest, showing that deeper speculation about the nature of place is unnecessary for the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
First, read the opening objection: 'If two sides were assumed...' Notice how Razi summarizes the problem and declares it invalid due to proofs for the atom.
The objection is part of the standard kalam method of stating a problem before answering.
2
Identify the key concept
In the answer, Razi introduces 'necessary knowledge' (علم ضروري) as the foundation. Everyone necessarily knows that an extended thing can be pointed to as here or there. This is self-evident.
Necessary knowledge does not require proof; it is immediate.
3
Examine the definitions
Razi defines rest as the continuity of being 'here or there', and motion as its change. See how these follow directly from the necessary knowledge of being here/there.
4
Note the limitation
Razi explicitly avoids discussing the reality of place. Understand why this is sufficient for his argument: the definitions do not require a philosophical theory of space.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider how this approach avoids speculative metaphysics by relying on self-evident truths. This is a hallmark of Razi's method in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة قلنا : لو افترض في كل متحيز جانبان لزم كون الجسم قابلا لانقسامات لا نهاية لها وذلك محال بالدلائل الدالة على إثبات الجوهر الفرد ولما بطل ذلك فسدت هذه المقدمة، وبالله التوفيق. الجواب : أما الاستفسار عن الحيز فنقول : إن كل أحد يعلم بالضرورة أن كل متحيز فإنه يصح أن يشار إليه بالحس، بأنه هنا أو هناك، وهذا القدر معلوم بالضرورة، ويكفينا بناء الفرض على هذا المقدار ؛ وذلك لأن كونه هنا أو هناك إما أن يبقى مستمرا أو لا يبقى فإن بقي مستمرا فهو الساكن وإن لم يبق فهو المتحرك، وهذا القدر وافٍ بتقرير هذه المقدمة. وأما الخوض في بيان أن حقيقة المكان ما هم ؟ فلا حاجة بنا في المسألة إليه.
The Third Introduction. We said: If two sides were assumed in every extended thing (mutahayyiz), then the body would be divisible without end, and that is impossible according to the proofs indicating the establishment of the atom (jawhar fard). When that was invalidated, this introduction becomes invalid. And with Allah is success. The answer: As for the inquiry about place (hayyiz), we say: Everyone knows necessarily that every extended thing (mutahayyiz) can be pointed to sensibly as being here or there, and this much is known necessarily, and it suffices us to build the assumption on this extent. That is because its being here or there either remains continuously or does not remain; if it remains continuous, it is at rest (sākin), and if it does not remain, it is in motion (mutaharrik). This much suffices for establishing this introduction. As for delving into explaining what the reality of place is, we have no need for it in this issue.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the definition of place and the nature of motion and rest. Razi begins by stating the problematic assumption: if every extended thing had two sides, bodies would be infinitely divisible, which is refuted by proofs for the atom. Then he provides the answer: the necessary, self-evident knowledge that every extended object can be perceived as 'here' or 'there' is sufficient. From this, he derives the definitions: if the state of being 'here or there' remains continuous, it is rest; if it changes, it is motion. He dismisses the need for a deeper philosophical definition of place itself, as it is not necessary for the current argument. The argument relies on immediate sense perception and common understanding, not speculative metaphysics.

Key Terms
متحيز
حيز
جوهر فرد
ساكن
متحرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi is denying any definition of place or that he is rejecting the need for a definition of motion and rest.
Correction: Razi is not denying a definition of place; he is saying that a deeper, philosophical definition is unnecessary for the argument. The definitions of motion and rest are based on simple, necessary knowledge of being here/there.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is sufficient to define motion and rest?
A) A precise definition of place
B) The necessary knowledge of being here or there
C) The concept of the atom
D) Infinite divisibility

Key Takeaway
Razi defines motion and rest by the persistence or change of the 'here/there' designation, relying on necessary sensory knowledge rather than speculative philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you tend to overcomplicate your faith by seeking deep philosophical proofs for every matter, while ignoring the simple, self-evident truths from revelation and nature? How can you train yourself to trust what is clear and necessary today?

?
How often do you rely on the necessary knowledge of Allah's existence and oneness without requiring elaborate demonstrations, and when do you indulge in unnecessary speculation? What step will you take this week to ground yourself in what is self-evident?

Deflecting the Objection: Body at First Origination
Razi responds to the objection that a body at initial creation is neither moving nor at rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi deflects an objection about the body's initial state by clarifying the temporal scope of the discussion.

Core Idea
The body's initial moment is not the focus; its state after enduring matters for motion and rest.

Razi faces an objection that at the very first moment of existence, a body is neither moving nor at rest. This could undermine arguments about motion and rest being necessary attributes of bodies. In response, Razi redirects attention to the body's state of permanence (al-bāqī), the period after creation when the body endures. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi responds by redefining the subject of discussion.
The objector focuses on the initial moment; Razi shifts to the enduring state.
2
Grasp the key term 'al-bāqī'
Recognize that the discussion is about the body after it persists, not at its origin. The term 'al-bāqī' (الباقي) refers to the body's continued existence after creation.
This distinction is crucial for the argument's validity.
3
Connect to the six proofs
Note that Razi references earlier proofs that the body cannot be moving in pre-eternity (al-azal). This implies that motion is temporal and begins at creation.
The six proofs are not detailed here, but they support the necessity of motion or rest in the enduring state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: الجسم في أول حدوثه ليس بمتحرك ولا ساكن قلنا : كلامنا في الجسم الباقي، ولا شك أن الجسم حال بقائه لا بد وأن يكون إما متحركًا وإما ساكنا . ثم إنا ذكرنا الدلائل الستة على أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون متحركا في الأزل.
His statement: 'The body at its first origination is neither moving nor at rest.' We said: Our discussion is about the body in its state of permanence (al-bāqī), and there is no doubt that the body in its state of permanence must be either moving or at rest. Then we mentioned the six proofs that the body is impossible to be moving in pre-eternity (al-azal).
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This is an objection and answer concerning the initial state of a body. The objector claims that a body at the moment of its coming into existence is neither moving nor at rest. Razi replies that his discussion is about the body after it has come into being and continues to exist (al-bāqī). In that state, it necessarily is either moving or at rest. He then references an earlier set of six proofs establishing that the body cannot have been moving in pre-eternity, implying that motion began at creation. Thus, the objection is deflected by clarifying the temporal scope of the argument.

Key Terms
أول حدوثه
الباقي
متحرك
ساكن
الأزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body at its first moment of creation is neither moving nor at rest, so the categories of motion and rest do not apply to bodies at creation.
Correction: Razi explains that the discussion concerns the body after it endures (al-bāqī), at which point it must be either moving or at rest. The initial instant is not the focus.

Key Takeaway
Razi deflects the objection by shifting focus from the body's first moment to its state of enduring existence (al-bāqī), where motion or rest is necessary.

?

Which direction are you moving right now? What is one concrete step you can take to ensure you are not drifting into rest (neglect) but moving closer to Him?
?
The objector used a subtle technicality to avoid the necessary conclusion. Where in your life do you use exceptions or technicalities to shy away from the full implications of Islam? Will you commit today to stop hiding behind such loopholes and embrace the complete call of the truth?

The Eternity of Motion's Possibility Refuted
Razi refutes the claim that motion's possibility has no beginning, thus motion could be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the lack of beginning to motion's possibility does not entail that motion is eternal, and how Razi refutes this objection using the condition of being preceded by non-existence.

Core Idea
Possibility without beginning does not mean actual eternity.

The objection claims that since the possibility of motion has no starting point, motion could be eternal. Razi responds by clarifying a crucial distinction: a thing considered under the condition of being preceded by non-existence can have no beginning to its possibility, yet it remains contingent and not eternal. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument: first the objector's claim, then Razi's response.
2
Identify the key condition
Notice the phrase 'taken with the condition of being preceded by non-existence'. This condition is central to Razi's refutation.
The condition (شرط) is that the thing is considered as preceded by non-existence.
3
Understand the refutation
Razi argues that even though the possibility of such a thing has no beginning, that does not make the thing itself eternal. The implication from no beginning of possibility to actual eternity is invalid.
This refutation relies on the distinction between possibility and actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : إنه لا بداية لصحة وجود الحركة فيلزم صحة كون الحركة أزلية قلنا : قد ذكرنا أن الشيء أخذ بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالعدم، فهو مع هذا الشرط لا أول لصحته، ثم إنه لم يلزم منه صحة كون هذا الشيء بهذا الشرط أزليا، فكذا ههنا.
His statement: 'There is no beginning for the validity of the existence of motion, so it follows that motion is valid to be eternal.' We said: We have already mentioned that when a thing is taken with the condition that it is preceded by non-existence, then with this condition there is no beginning for its validity, but from this it does not follow the validity of this thing with this condition being eternal. So likewise here.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
In this objection and answer, the objector argues that since the possibility of motion has no starting point, motion could be eternal. Razi responds by drawing on a distinction previously made: a thing considered as preceded by non-existence has no limit to its possibility, but that does not mean it can be eternal. In other words, the possibility is endless in the sense that there is no first moment when it becomes possible, but the actual existence of the thing is still contingent and not eternal. Thus, the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
لا بداية
صحة وجود
أزلي
مسبوق بالعدم
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something's possibility has no beginning, then that thing must be eternal.
Correction: Razi shows that a thing considered under the condition of being preceded by non‑existence can have no beginning to its possibility yet not be eternal; actual eternity does not follow from mere possibility without beginning.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the claim that motion's possibility has no beginning, so motion could be eternal?
A) He accepts the argument
B) He distinguishes between possibility and actuality, noting that a thing conditioned by being preceded by non-existence has no beginning to its possibility but is not eternal
C) He says motion is impossible
D) He says the possibility is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The fact that motion's possibility has no beginning does not make motion eternal; it remains contingent and preceded by non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something was possible for you, it would necessarily happen? How does Razi's distinction between possibility and actuality under a condition challenge your certainty about your own plans?
?
Consider your own existence: you are preceded by non-existence. Does knowing that your possibility had no beginning but your actual existence had a beginning change how you view the gift of your life? What condition defines your existence?
?
The objector thought endless possibility guarantees endless reality. Where in your life do you confuse what could be with what must be? Are you treating your potential as if it is already actual, and forgetting the condition that makes you contingent?

Why Change Demands a Positive State
Razi argues that when a thing changes, one of its states must be positive, not merely non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi insists that any change implies a positive new state and how he dismisses counterexamples as self-evident.

Core Idea
Change always requires a positive reality, not mere removal of absence.

In this passage, Razi confronts an objection to his principle that change must involve a positive new state. The objector asks: why can't it be merely a non-existent state? Razi answers succinctly, showing that even the removal of non-existence is a positive act. He then dismisses counterexamples as attacks on self-evident premises, reinforcing the foundational nature of his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's question and Razi's two-part response.
2
Analyze the logical move
Identify the two cases Razi considers: if the original state is positive, the conclusion is immediate; if it is non-existent, the new state removes non-existence and is therefore positive.
Focus on the phrase 'رافع العدم ثبوت'.
3
Understand the dismissal
Razi dismisses counterexamples as attacks on a self-evident premise. Ask yourself why he considers this premise self-evident.
This step connects to the big picture of foundational principles in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «لِمَ قلتم بأنه لما صدق على الشيء أنه لم يكن كذا ثم صار كذا، فإنه يقتضي كون إحدى الحالتين أمرًا ثبوتياً؟». قلنا : إحدى الحالتين إن كانت ثبوتية فهو المقصود، وإن كانت عدمية فالحالة الثابتة رافعة لها ورافع العدم ثبوت فالحالة الثابتة يجب أن تكون ثبوتية. وأما النقوض التي ذكرتموها فهي نقوض على مقدمة بديهية، فلا تستحق الجواب.
His statement: 'Why did you say that when it is true of a thing that it was not such-and-such and then became such-and-such, it necessitates that one of the two states is a positive matter?' We said: If one of the two states is positive, that is what is intended; if it is non-existent, then the fixed state is removing it, and the remover of non-existence is positive, so the fixed state must be positive. As for the counterexamples you mentioned, they are counterexamples to a self-evident premise, so they do not deserve an answer.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage handles an objection questioning the need for a positive state when something changes. Razi's answer is logical: either the state is positive (which is what he wants) or it is non-existent. In the latter case, the enduring state that arises removes the non-existence, and since the remover of non-existence is itself positive, the new state must be positive. He dismisses counterexamples as attacks on a self-evident premise, indicating that the principle that change implies a positive new state is intuitively clear.

Key Terms
ثبوتي
عدمي
حالة
رافع العدم
مقدمة بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think change can occur without a positive new state, e.g., via mere removal of a privation.
Correction: Razi argues that even the removal of non-existence is itself a positive act, so the new state is necessarily positive.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, when a thing changes from not being something to being it, the new state must be:
A) Non-existent
B) Positive (thubūtī)
C) Neither positive nor non-existent
D) Eternal

Key Takeaway
For any change, the resultant state is positive because it removes non-existence, and the remover of non-existence is positive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you change a bad habit, do you focus on 'removing' it or on building a positive alternative? How does Razi's argument expose the inadequacy of thinking change is just elimination? What positive state will you actively adopt today?
?
Consider a time you blamed your environment or others for your flaws, as if your deficiency was just an absence. How does this lesson indict that excuse? What non-existent state are you passively leaving unremoved?
?
If every meaningful change requires a positive step, what positive step have you been avoiding in your relationship with Allah because it feels easier to call it 'removing distractions'? What will you commit to do this week?

Razi's Defense of the Definition of Motion
Razi defends motion as immediate second contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defines motion as the atom's immediate occurrence in the second place at the instant the first place is lost, refuting the idea of a transitional state.

Core Idea
Motion is not a transition; it is immediate contact with the second place when the first contact ceases.

In this passage, Razi responds to an objection against the standard Ash'ari definition of motion. The objector argues that motion is a state prior to being in the second place. Razi defends the definition by analyzing the instant of change. Let's see how he uses the concept of 'now' (al-ān) to rebut the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the objection claims motion is a transitional state preceding the second place.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says motion is not mere occurrence in the second place but a state prior to that occurrence, i.e., transitioning from first to second.
3
Razi's logic using 'now'
Razi argues that when the first place is lost, there is a first instant of that non-existence. At that same instant, the body must be in contact with another point along the path. Thus, motion is simply the second contact at that instant.
The key concept is 'al-ān' (the now) – the first moment of absence of the first contact.
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that motion has no meaning except the occurrence of the second contact at the first moment of the first contact's non-existence. This refutes the need for a transitional state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : "الحركة ليست عبارة عن حصول الجوهر في الحيز الثاني، بل عن حالة متقدمة على هذا الحصول، وهو انتقاله من الحيز الأول إلى الحيز الثاني " . قلنا : إن الحصول في الحيز الأول لما عدم بقي "الآن" الذي هو أول زمان ذلك العدم ولا بد وأن يكون الجسم قد حصل في حد آخر من حدود المسافة، فإذن لا معنى للحركة إلا حصول المماسة الثانية في "الآن" أول زمان عدم المماسة هو الأولى.
His statement: 'Motion is not the occurrence of the atom in the second place, but rather a state prior to this occurrence, namely its transitioning from the first place to the second place.' We said: When the occurrence in the first place is negated, the 'now' (al-ān) which is the first time of that non-existence remains, and the body must have occurred at another limit of the distance. So then motion has no meaning except the occurrence of the second contact (al-mumāssa al-thāniya) in the 'now' which is the first time of the non-existence of the first contact.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This objection and answer address the definition of motion. The objector claims motion is not merely the atom being in the second place, but a transitional state preceding it. Razi replies that once the atom is no longer in the first place, there is a first instant (now) when that non-existence begins; at that same instant, the atom must be in contact with another point along the path. Therefore, motion is simply the body's contact with the second point at the first moment of losing contact with the first. This counters the idea of an intermediate transitional state.

Key Terms
حركة
جوهر
حيز
الآن
مماسة
وقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is a gradual transition or intermediate state between places.
Correction: Motion is the immediate occurrence of the atom in the second place at the very instant the first place is lost, with no intermediate state.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is motion?
A) A gradual transition between places
B) The occurrence of contact with the second place at the instant the first place is lost
C) The state of rest
D) The atom's potential to move

Key Takeaway
Razi defines motion as the atom's contact with the second place at the first instant of losing the first contact, rejecting any intermediate transitional state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you ever find yourself waiting for a 'transition' period to pass before fully embracing your new circumstances? Razi insists that change happens immediately: the moment the first place is lost, you are already in contact with the next. Are you living in the illusion of a transition, or are you fully present in the new reality you are already in? What first contact must you let go of today, and what second contact are you already touching?
?
Think of a habit or situation you feel you are 'in the process of changing.' Razi says that the moment you are no longer in the old state, you are immediately in a new one. Is there a part of your life where you are falsely believing you are still 'in transition' when you have already crossed into a new phase? What would it mean to accept that you are already in the new place?
?
The passage speaks of the 'now' as the first time of non-existence of the first contact. Are there areas of your faith where you are holding onto a past state that no longer exists, afraid to embrace the immediate contact with something new? What is the 'now' calling you to accept without delay?

The Necessity of Two Sides in Extended Things
Razi defends the necessary knowledge of two sides in extended things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the existence of two distinct sides in every extended thing is a necessary truth that cannot be denied.

Core Idea
Every extended thing has two distinct sides—this is undeniable.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses an objection that assuming two sides in an extended object leads to infinite divisibility. He counters by pointing to a self-evident fact: every extended thing has a side that faces one direction and another side that faces a different direction. This is known necessarily and cannot be rejected. Let's explore this key point in the debate over the nature of physical bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection claims that assuming two sides implies infinite divisibility, and Razi's response appeals to necessary knowledge.
The objection is presented as a quotation within Razi's text.
2
Identify the necessary truth
Razi asserts that it is a necessary truth that every extended thing has a side that faces one direction different from the side facing another direction (e.g., up vs down). This is the core of his defense.
This necessary truth is considered self-evident and not subject to denial.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on why Razi's claim blocks the objection. If the existence of distinct sides is a necessary truth, then the assumption of two sides is not merely hypothetical but real, so it does not entail an absurd infinite regress.
The objection assumed two sides as a hypothetical, but Razi shows it is an actual property.
4
Apply to the larger debate
Consider how this argument fits within the broader discussion of the nature of space and atoms in Islamic theology (kalam). Razi's view supports the idea that extended things have intrinsic directions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله لو افترض في كل متحيز جانبان للزم أن يكون كل متحيز قابلا للقسمة، لا إلى نهاية. السماء قلنا : إن من المعلوم بالضرورة أن كل متحيز فإن الجانب الذي منه يلي غير الجانب الذي يلي الأرض وما كان معلوم الثبوت بالضرورة لا يمكن إنكاره وبالله التوفيق.
His statement: 'If two sides are assumed in every extended thing, it would necessitate that every extended thing is divisible without end.' [The sky?] We said: It is known necessarily that every extended thing has a side by which it faces [one direction] different from the side by which it faces the earth, and whatever is known to be necessarily true cannot be denied. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This is a final objection and answer about the assumption of two sides. The objector claims that assuming two sides implies infinite divisibility. Razi counters by pointing to the self-evident fact that every extended object has a side that faces one direction and another side that faces another (like up and down). This is a necessary truth that cannot be denied, so the assumption of distinct sides is valid and does not lead to infinite divisibility. The objection is thus dismissed.

Key Terms

معلوم بالضرورة

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Razi shows that the existence of two sides is a necessary truth, not a mere assumption, and thus the objection does not apply.

Checkpoint

B) By stating that it is a necessary truth that every extended thing has distinct directions
C) By denying that it leads to infinite divisibility

Key Takeaway
The existence of two distinct sides in every extended thing is a necessary truth that cannot be denied, thereby defusing the objection of infinite divisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Existence of Necessity: Two Proofs from Al-Razi
Al-Razi gives two proofs that necessity is not non-existent and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's two rational proofs that necessity (al-wujub) is an existent attribute, not a non-existent one, and how he refutes all objections in one stroke.

Core Idea
Necessity must be existent because its opposite is non-existent.

Al-Razi tackles a subtle but crucial point: is the attribute of necessity (wujub) something that exists in the external world, or merely a mental construct? If it were non-existent, then nothing could truly be described as necessary, which would undermine the very concept of a Necessary Being. In this passage, he presents two crisp logical proofs and a unified answer to potential objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the structure: 'First', 'Second', 'As for the objections'.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof argues that necessity affirms existence; if it were non-existent, one contradictory would cause the affirmation of the other, which is impossible.
Contradictories cannot be causally related.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof uses the relationship between necessity and non-necessity. Non-necessity applies to non-existent things, so non-necessity is non-existent; thus necessity must be existent as one of the two contradictories.
Focus on the chain: non-necessity is predicated of non-existent → non-necessity is non-existent → necessity is existent.
4
Examine the objection and refutation
The objection says if necessity were purely non-existent, nothing would be described as necessary, negating the Necessary Being. Al-Razi refutes this by turning it against the objector: that very consequence shows necessity must be existent.
The refutation is a single face that answers all objections.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for theology: if necessity were non-existent, the concept of Wajib al-Wujud (Necessary Being) would be empty. Al-Razi safeguards the reality of the divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن الوجوب تأكيد الوجود، فلو كان الوجوب عدميًا لكان أحد النقيضين سببًا لتأكد الآخر، وإنه محال.
الثاني : أن الوجوب يناقض اللاوجوب، والداخل تحت اللا وجوب إما الممتنع وإما الممكن الخاص والممتنع معدوم، والممكن الخاص يجوز أن يكون معدوماً، فإذن اللا وجوب محمول على المعدوم فيكون معدوماً، وإن كان اللاوجوب معدومًا كان الوجوب موجودًا ضرورة أن أحد النقيضين لا بد وأن يكون موجوداً.
وأما المعارضات فإنها بأسرها معارضة بوجه واحد وهو أن الوجوب لو كان عدميًا محضا في الخارج لم يكن الشيء في الخارج موصوفًا بأنه واجب، فهذا يقتضي نفي واجب الوجود لذاته، وإنه محال.
First: That necessity is an affirmation of existence, so if necessity were non-existent, one of the two contradictories would be a cause for the affirmation of the other, and that is impossible.
Second: That necessity contradicts non-necessity, and that which falls under non-necessity is either the impossible or the particular possible; the impossible is non-existent, and the particular possible may be non-existent. So non-necessity is predicated of the non-existent and thus is non-existent. And if non-necessity is non-existent, then necessity must exist, since one of the two contradictories must necessarily exist.
As for the objections, they are all opposed by a single face: that if necessity were purely non-existent in the external, nothing in the external would be described as necessary. This would necessitate the negation of the Necessary Being by virtue of itself, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs that necessity (al-wujub) is not a non-existent attribute but rather an existing one, and then answers all objections with a single refutation. The first proof: necessity affirms existence; if it were non-existent, one contradictory would cause the affirmation of the other, which is impossible. The second proof: necessity contradicts non-necessity. Non-necessity applies to the impossible or the possible, both of which are non-existent. So non-necessity is non-existent, thus necessity must be existent. The objector says: if necessity is non-existent, nothing external would have the attribute of necessity, negating the Necessary Being. But the author responds that the objection fails because necessity is not a separate entity but a pure relation or judgment? Actually the passage says all objections are answered by one face: that if necessity were purely non-existent externally, nothing would be described as necessary, which would negate the Necessary Being. That is impossible. So this supports that necessity is existent.

Key Terms
al-wujub
al-adami
al-mumtani'
al-mumkin al-khas
al-mu'aradat

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity is only a mental concept, not something that exists externally.
Correction: Al-Razi proves that necessity must be existent because its contradictory (non-necessity) is predicated of non-existent things, so non-necessity is non-existent; hence necessity must be existent as one of the two contradictories must exist.

Checkpoint
How does the second proof show wujub is existent?
A) By showing non-necessity applies only to the impossible
B) By showing non-necessity is predicated of non-existent and thus non-necessity is non-existent, so necessity must exist
C) By using the concept of affirmation
D) By refuting objections

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi establishes that necessity is an existent attribute through two logical arguments, preserving the reality of the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If necessity were merely a mental concept and not an existent reality, what would that do to your certainty about Allah's existence? Are there any obligations (like prayer or honesty) you treat as 'necessary' in theory but deny in practice? How does this lesson force you to confront that inconsistency?
?
Al-Razi argues that denying necessity as existent leads to denying the Necessary Being. When have you subtly denied an attribute of Allah because it felt too abstract or distant? Will you now affirm that attribute as real and let it shape your life?
?
The refutation says that if necessity were non-existent, nothing could be described as necessary. What do you call 'necessary' in your daily life—work, family, faith—but often treat as optional? How does knowing necessity is an existent reality change your sense of accountability to Allah's commands?

The Fifth Proof: Impossibility of an Eternal Body
Imam Razi presents the fifth proof for the origination of bodies through two possibilities of an eternal body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's fifth proof for the temporal origination of bodies, which refutes the possibility of an eternal body through a dilemma involving the relationship between eternity and bodyhood.

Core Idea
An eternal body leads to contradiction whether eternity is identical to bodyhood or additional to it.

Imam Razi continues his series of proofs for the temporal nature of bodies. Here he presents a clever dilemma: if a body were eternal, what would that eternity be? Either it is the very fact that it is a body, or it is something extra. Both options lead to absurdities, showing that no body can be eternal. Let's follow this logical argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Let the logical flow settle in your mind.
The proof is a dilemma with two branches.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The first alternative: eternity is identical to bodyhood. The second: eternity is additional to bodyhood. Notice how these are the only possibilities for a relationship between a thing and its attribute.
Think: if a body is eternal, what does 'eternal' mean? Is it the same as its being a body or not?
3
Understand why the first alternative fails
If eternity were identical to bodyhood, then knowing something as a body would mean knowing it as eternal. But we know bodies are bodies without knowing if they are eternal, so identity is false.
This refutation relies on the principle that if two concepts are identical, knowledge of one is knowledge of the other.
4
Understand why the second alternative fails
If eternity is additional, then that additional attribute itself must be either eternal or originated. Both lead to contradictions: if eternal, infinite regress; if originated, the eternal would have a beginning.
This alternative is more subtle. Reflect on the infinite regress argument and the contradiction of an eternal thing having a beginning.
5
Examine the objection and response
An objection might be raised: why can't the eternal be defined as existence plus prior non-existence? The response clarifies that the originated has prior non-existence, while the eternal has no prior non-existence, so the distinction holds.
This response deepens the understanding of the terms 'originated' and 'eternal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الخامس في حدوث الأجسام : لو كان الجسم قديما لكان قدمه إما أن يكون عين كونه جسما وإما أن يكون مغايرا لكونه جسماً، والقسمان باطلان، فيبطل القول بكون الجسم قديما إنما قلنا: إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قدم الجسم عين كونه جسما لأنه لو كان كذلك لكان العلم يكونه جسما علما بكونه قديماً، فكما أن العلم بكونه جسمًا ضروريًا لزم أن يكون العلم بكونه قديماً ضرورياً، ولما بطل هذا فسد هذا القسم، وإنما قلنا: إنه لا يجوز أن يكون قدم الجسم زائدا على كونه جسماً؛ لأن ذلك الزائد إن كان قديما لزم أن يكون قدمه زائدا عليه، ولزم التسلسل، وإن كان حادثا فكل حادث فله أول وكل قديم فلا أول له فلو كان قدم القديم عبارة عن ذلك الحادث لزم أن يكون ذلك الشيء له أول وأن لا يكون له أول، وهو محال. فإن عارضونا بكونه حادثا قلنا : الحادث عبارة عن مجموع الوجود الحاصل في الحال والعدم السابق ولا يبعد حصول العلم بالوجود الحاصل مع الجهل بالعدم السابق، بخلاف القديم فإنه لا معنى له إلا نفس وجوده فظهر الفرق، وهذا وجه جليل، وفيه مباحث دقيقة. وليكن هذا آخر كلامنا في شرح دلائل حدوث الأجسام والله أعلم.
The fifth proof concerning the occurrence of bodies: If the body were eternal, then its eternity is either identical to its being a body or additional to its being a body. Both alternatives are false, so the statement that the body is eternal is false. We say: It is not permissible for the eternity of the body to be identical to its being a body, because if that were the case, then knowledge of it being a body would be knowledge of its being eternal. Just as knowledge of it being a body is necessary, so knowledge of it being eternal would be necessary. When this is false, this alternative is invalid. And we say: It is not permissible for the eternity of the body to be additional to its being a body, because that additional attribute, if eternal, would entail that its eternity is additional to it, leading to infinite regress. And if it is originated, every originated thing has a beginning, and every eternal thing has no beginning. So if the eternity of the eternal were that originated thing, it would require that thing to have a beginning and not to have a beginning, which is impossible. If they object by saying it is originated, we say: The originated thing is the sum of existence occurring in the present and prior non-existence, and it is not unlikely to have knowledge of existence while ignorant of prior non-existence, unlike the eternal which has no meaning except its very existence. Thus the difference appears. This is a lofty point, containing subtle investigations. Let this be the end of our speech on explaining proofs of the occurrence of bodies, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof (burhan) for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies. It is a classification argument: either the eternity (qidam) of a body is the same as its being a body (jism), or it is an additional attribute. Both are refuted. If identical, then knowing it as a body would necessitate knowing it as eternal, but the former is intuitive while the latter is not, so the identity is false. If additional, then that additional attribute itself must be eternal or originated. If eternal, infinite regress. If originated, then it has a beginning, but the eternal thing has no beginning, contradiction. The author also addresses a potential objection about the definition of 'originated' and clarifies that the eternal is just its existence without prior non-existence. The proof is considered strong and subtle.

Key Terms
جسم (body)
قدم (eternity)
حدوث (origination)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity could be an additional attribute of a body without affecting its nature.
Correction: The proof shows that if eternity is additional, it leads to either infinite regress or contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the first alternative considered for the eternity of a body?
A) Eternity is identical to bodyhood
B) Eternity is additional to bodyhood
C) Eternity is separate from bodyhood
D) Eternity is unknowable

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's fifth proof demonstrates that the concept of an eternal body is impossible because it leads to logical contradictions whether eternity is identical to or separate from bodyhood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the logical impossibility of an eternal body, what does that tell you about your own existence and your relationship to the Creator? Are you living as if this world is eternal, or as one who knows it is originated and will return?
?
The proof shows that the world has a beginning. How does this truth change your daily priorities? What would you do differently today if you truly believed that everything around you is contingent and will end?
?
Imam Razi argues that knowledge of a body does not entail knowledge of its eternity. Do you know the world as temporary, or do you act as if it is permanent? What would it take for you to truly internalize that nothing here is eternal except Allah?

The Eternal Cause and the Need for a Specifier
Razi presents the initial premise and the first reductio of the great proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal cause alone cannot explain the world's specific time of creation, and how the first part of Razi's 'great proof' leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
An eternal cause cannot determine a temporal effect without a specifying factor.

Razi begins his great proof by examining the relationship between an eternal cause and its temporal effect. He sets up a dilemma: if the cause is sufficient, the effect should be eternal; if not, the effect's existence at a particular time requires a reason. This lesson unpacks the first part of the argument, showing the impossibility of a specification without a preponderator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
This is a logical proof, so pay attention to the conditionals.
2
Understand the initial premise
Identify the two possibilities: either the cause necessitates the effect or not. What follows from each?
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
The author supposes all causal factors present and considers the effect's existence. The specification of time leads to a contradiction.
Notice the terms: 'murajjih' (preponderator) and 'khalf' (contradiction).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The first part concludes that arbitrary preference without a preponderator is impossible.
This is a key principle in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة
حصولها حصول الأثر أو لا يجب، فإن وجب لزم من دوامه دوام الأثر، وإن لم يجب كان وجوده مع تلك الآثار جائزا.
فلنفرض ذاته مع مجموع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، تارة مع وجود تلك الآثار وأخرى مع عدمها، فاختصاص ذلك الوقت بالوجود دون الوقت الآخر إما أن يتوقف على اختصاصه بأمر ما لأجله كان هو أولى بوجود ذلك الأثر، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، فإن كان الأول كان ذلك المخصص معتبرًا في المؤثرية، وهو ما كان حاصلا قبل ذلك الوقت فإذن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، وكنا قد فرضناه حاصلاً، هذا خلف، وإن كان الثاني كان ذلك ترجيحا لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح أصلا، وهو محال، هذا إذا قلنا بأن كل ما كان ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية كان حاصلا في الأزل، أما إذا قلنا بأن كل ما كان حاصلا في الأزل فحدوثه في لا يزال بعد أن لم يكن، إما أن يفتقر إلى مؤثر أو لا يفتقر، فإن لم يفتقر فقد حدث الممكن لا عن مرجح وإن افتقر نقلنا الكلام إلى كيفية إحداث تلك الأمور، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال.
هذا هو العمدة الكبرى للقوم
The Third Premise: Its obtaining necessitates the obtaining of the effect or it does not. If it necessitates, then from its perpetuity follows the perpetuity of the effect; if it does not necessitate, then its existence with those effects is possible. So let us suppose its essence together with the totality of the matters considered in causality (al-muʾaththiriyyah), sometimes with the existence of those effects and sometimes without them. Then the specification of that time for existence to the exclusion of another time either depends on its specification by something by virtue of which it is more deserving of the existence of that effect, or it is not so. If the first, then that specifying factor is considered in causality, and it was existing before that time. Then everything indispensable for causality was existing in eternity (al-azal), and we had already supposed it to be existing. This is a contradiction (khalf). If the second, then that is a preponderance of one of the two sides of an equally possible thing over the other without any preponderator (murajjih) at all, and that is impossible (muḥāl). This is if we say that everything indispensable for causality was existing in eternity. But if we say that everything that was existing in eternity, whose coming to be in the future after non-existence either requires an effecter or does not. If it does not require, then the possible has occurred without a preponderator; and if it requires, we transfer the discussion to how those things are brought about, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, and that is impossible. This is the great pillar (al-ʿumdah al-kubrā) for the [mutakallimūn].
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents what Razi calls 'the great pillar' (al-ʿumdah al-kubrā) of the mutakallimūn's proof for the temporal creation of the world. It is a proof by reductio ad absurdum, focusing on the problem of temporal specification: why was the world created at this particular time rather than earlier or later? The argument begins by considering the relationship between the cause (implied to be God's power and will) and the effect (the world). If the cause is eternal and sufficient, the effect should be eternal; but if the cause is not sufficient, then the effect can exist with it or not. The mutakallimūn suppose the essence of God along with all factors necessary for causality. They consider two cases: either the effect exists or not. The fact that the world exists at a specific time requires a specifying factor that distinguishes that time. If that factor is something additional, it must have existed before that time—but all necessary factors were already supposed to exist from eternity, so this is contradictory. If there is no such factor, then the choice of one time over another is an arbitrary preference without a preponderator, which is impossible. The argument then considers a second scenario: if everything existing from eternity is subject to temporal origination, either it requires an effecter or not. If not, a possible being occurs without a cause; if it does, then we ask about the cause of that cause, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Thus, the world must have a determinant for its temporal specification, proving that it is not eternal but created in time.

Key Terms
المؤثرية (al-muʾaththiriyyah)
المرجح (al-murajjih)
الترجيح بلا مرجح (tarjīḥ bi-lā murajjih)
الخلف (al-khalf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this proof attempts to prove God's existence.
Correction: It actually aims to prove the world's temporality by showing that its specification to a particular time requires a determinant.

Checkpoint
According to the first part of Razi's argument, if the cause necessitates the effect, what follows?
A) The effect is possible
B) The effect is eternal
C) The effect requires a specifier
D) The effect is impossible

Key Takeaway
An eternal cause cannot produce a temporal effect without a specifier; assuming a specifier leads to contradiction, and assuming none leads to an unacceptable arbitrary preference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on natural causes to accomplish your goals, do you ever pause to consider that the ultimate cause is God, and that He alone determines the moment and manner of every effect? How would your daily planning change if you truly internalized that no effect occurs without His specifying will?
?
The argument shows that arbitrary preference is impossible for God. Yet, in your own life, you often choose between options without a clear reason. Does this discrepancy trouble you? Are you willing to examine your heart for the specifiers—the motives, desires, or fears—that drive your choices, and see if they align with what God wants?
?
Razi's proof forces you to confront the fact that if God's essence necessitated the world, the world would be eternal. But you believe the world is created in time. So, what is the specifier in God's eternal will that made Him create the world at a particular moment? Can you live with the mystery that God's wisdom is beyond your full comprehension, yet trust that His choice is never arbitrary?

The Second Scenario: Infinite Regress of Causes
Razi presents the second scenario leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's argument addresses the possibility that all eternal factors are themselves temporally originated, leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If everything from eternity is itself created, either it requires a cause (regress) or not (arbitrary).

Having shown the problems with assuming an eternal specifier, Razi considers a different scenario: what if everything that exists from eternity is itself temporally originated? This lesson examines the second horn of his argument, which leads to either an uncaused event or an infinite chain of causes, both impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the conditional 'if we say that everything existing from eternity...' and follow the two possibilities.
This part uses the term 'tasalsul' (infinite regress).
2
Understand the two branches
Either the originated thing needs a cause or not. Identify the consequences of each.
3
Consider the infinite regress objection
If it needs a cause, then we ask about that cause's cause, leading to an endless chain.
This is a standard argument against an infinite causal series.
4
Synthesize with lesson 1
Both parts together constitute the full 'great pillar' proof.
The conclusion is that the world must have a temporal origin.

Source Text

فلنفرض ذاته مع مجموع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية، تارة مع وجود تلك الآثار وأخرى مع عدمها، فاختصاص ذلك الوقت بالوجود دون الوقت الآخر إما أن يتوقف على اختصاصه بأمر ما لأجله كان هو أولى بوجود ذلك الأثر، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك، فإن كان الأول كان ذلك المخصص معتبرًا في المؤثرية، وهو ما كان حاصلا قبل ذلك الوقت فإذن كل ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية ما كان حاصلا في الأزل، وكنا قد فرضناه حاصلاً، هذا خلف، وإن كان الثاني كان ذلك ترجيحا لأحد طرفي الممكن المتساوي على الآخر من غير مرجح أصلا، وهو محال، هذا إذا قلنا بأن كل ما كان ما لا بد منه في المؤثرية كان حاصلا في الأزل، أما إذا قلنا بأن كل ما كان حاصلا في الأزل فحدوثه في لا يزال بعد أن لم يكن، إما أن يفتقر إلى مؤثر أو لا يفتقر، فإن لم يفتقر فقد حدث الممكن لا عن مرجح وإن افتقر نقلنا الكلام إلى كيفية إحداث تلك الأمور، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال.

Possibility is Not an Existing Attribute
Razi concludes that imkan cannot be an existing attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that possibility (imkan) is not a real, existent attribute but a conceptual rational judgment.

Core Idea
Possibility does not exist as an attribute; it is merely a rational notion.

After presenting multiple proofs, Imam al-Razi delivers a decisive conclusion: possibility cannot be conceived as an existing attribute. This single sentence settles a key debate in kalam about the ontological status of imkan. The phrase 'with God is success' reminds us of divine guidance in reaching truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation. Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يعقل أن يكون صفة موجودة' — it asserts that imkan cannot be rationally considered an existing attribute.
This is a conclusion, so it references earlier proofs that are not provided here.
2
Understand the conclusion in context
Realize that Razi is summarizing a longer argument. The conclusion implies that imkan is not a positive attribute inhering in things, but a mental concept used to describe the state of being possible.
3
Reflect on the key term 'imkan'
Consider how this conclusion affects your understanding of possibility in relation to Allah's attributes and creation. Imkan is a rational judgment, not an existent entity.

Source Text

فثبت بهذه الدلائل أن الإمكان لا يعقل أن يكون صفة موجودة، وبالله التوفيق.
So it is established by these proofs that possibility cannot be conceived as an existing attribute, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This is a concluding statement. After presenting multiple proofs, the author affirms that possibility (imkan) is not a real, existent attribute. It is a conceptual notion, not something that exists in the external world. The phrase 'with God is success' expresses reliance on divine guidance.

The Issue of Existence and Essence: Two Positions
Razi states the issue and the two positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical positions on whether existence is identical to essence or additional to it.

Core Idea
Existence and essence: one or two? A foundational debate in Islamic theology.

Razi begins by linking the issue of the non-existent to a deeper metaphysical question: is existence the same as essence or distinct? He then presents the two main positions, setting the stage for the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Razi frames the issue as dependent on the existence-essence relationship.
The phrase 'متفرعة على مسألة أخرى' shows the logical dependency.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the two views: al-Ash'ari's identification of existence with essence, and the majority view that they are distinct.
The majority includes many theologians (متكلمين) and most philosophers (حكماء).
3
Understand the significance
Consider why this distinction matters. If existence is the same as essence, then essences inherently exist; if additional, existence is an attribute.
This shapes later arguments about God's attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في أن المعدوم ليس بشيء وهذه المسألة متفرعة على مسألة أخرى وهي أن الوجود هل هو مغاير للماهية أم لا؟ فذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري" إلى أن وجود كل شيء نفس ماهيته وذهب كثير من المتكلمين وجمهور الحكماء إلى أن وجود الشيء وصف مغاير لماهيته. واحتج من قال بأن الوجود زائد على الماهية بوجوه : الوجه الأول : أن الوجود وصف مشترك فيه بين الموجودات وخصوصیات الماهيات غير مشترك فيها بين الموجودات فالوجود مغاير للماهية. وإنما قلنا : إن الوجود وصف مشترك فيه بين الموجودات بوجوه : الأول : أن صريح العقل حاكم بأنه لا واسطة بين طرفي النقيضين، ولولا أن مفهوم الوجود أمر واحد واقع في مقابلة النفي، وإلا لبطل ذلك. فإن قيل : إن عنيتم بقولكم إنه لا واسطة بين النقيضين أنه لا واسطة بين تحقق تلك الحقيقة وبين لا تحققها فهذا مسلم، ولكن هذا لا يقتضي كون الوجود قدرًا مشتركا بين كل الموجودات، فإن تحقق كل حقيقة عبارة عن خصوصية تلك الحقيقة، وتعين تلك الماهية وإن عنيتم به أن ههنا مفهوما مشتركًا واحدًا بين الموجودات هو المسمى بالموجودية، فهذا هو المصادرة على المطلوب. قلنا : لا شك في أن العقل يتصور من معنى الثبوت مفهوما ومن معنى السلب مفهوماً، ويجزم بأنهما لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، وهو عند تصور مفهوم الثبوت ومفهوم السلب لا يفتقر إلى الإشارة إلى ماهية معينة وذلك يوجب كون مفهوم الثبوت أمرًا واحدًا.
The Second Issue: That the non-existent is not a thing. This issue is branched from another issue, which is whether existence is distinct from essence or not. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held that the existence of every thing is its very essence, while many theologians (mutakallimūn) and the majority of philosophers (ḥukamā') held that the existence of a thing is an attribute distinct from its essence. Whoever said that existence is additional to essence argued by several proofs: The First Proof: That existence is a shared attribute among existents, while the particularities of essences are not shared among existents, so existence is distinct from essence. We say that existence is a shared attribute among existents by [several] aspects: The first: that the clear intellect judges that there is no intermediate between the two contradictories, and were it not that the concept of existence is one thing standing in opposition to negation, that would be invalid. If it is said: If you mean by your statement that there is no intermediate between contradictories that there is no intermediate between the actualization of that reality and its non-actualization, that is conceded, but that does not necessitate existence being a common factor among all existents, for the actualization of each reality is an expression of the particularity of that reality and the determination of that essence; and if you mean by it that there is here one common concept among existents called 'existentness' (al-mawjūdiyya), that is begging the question. We reply: There is no doubt that the intellect conceives from the meaning of affirmation (thubūt) a concept and from the meaning of negation (salb) a concept, and it judges decisively that they cannot be combined nor both removed, and when conceiving the concept of affirmation and the concept of negation it does not need to refer to any specific essence, and that necessitates that the concept of affirmation is one thing.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the second issue and the first proof for the claim that existence is additional to essence. Razi begins by stating the issue: whether the non-existent is a thing, which depends on whether existence is distinct from essence. He notes the two positions: al-Ash'ari identifies existence with essence, while most theologians and philosophers distinguish them. Then he presents the first proof from the pro-additionalists. The proof is that existence is shared among all existents, whereas the specific natures of essences are not shared, so existence must be distinct. To establish that existence is shared, he gives a first aspect based on the law of non-contradiction: the intellect conceives of affirmation and negation as two mutually exclusive and exhaustive concepts, and this concept of affirmation does not depend on any particular essence, implying it is one universal concept. He then includes an objection: the opponent might say that this argument either assumes what it wants to prove (if they mean a common concept) or merely restates the fact that each essence has its own actualization. The response is that the intellect's concept of affirmation is independent of any specific essence, proving its unity.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's view is the same as the philosophers' view.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes them: al-Ash'ari identifies existence with essence, while the majority (including philosophers) hold that existence is additional.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari regarding existence and essence?
A) Existence is additional to essence.
B) Existence is the same as essence.
C) Existence is a separate attribute of God only.
D) He did not address this issue.

Key Takeaway
The two main positions on existence and essence set the foundation for the first proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not the same as your essence, then who you are (your essence) is something that could have remained unrealized. Does that thought make you more grateful to the One who gave you existence? In what specific way will you express that gratitude today?
?
The debate you just read is abstract, but it touches the very ground of your being. Do you live as if your existence is merely an accident—something that could be swapped or lost—or as though it is inseparable from who you are? How would your daily choices change if you truly felt that your existence is a gift distinct from your essence, sustained moment by moment by the Creator?
