The First Proof: Existence as a Shared Attribute
Razi presents the first proof and the objection-reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for existence being additional to essence and how Razi responds to an objection.

Core Idea
Existence is a shared concept, proving it is distinct from the particular essences.

In this passage, Razi presents the first argument from the pro-additionalists, based on the shared nature of existence. He then anticipates an objection and provides a response using the law of non-contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and reply
Read the entire passage. Note the structure: a claim, an aspect, an objection, and a reply.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Understand the shared attribute argument
The proof states: existence is shared among existents, but specific essences are not shared. Therefore existence must be distinct from essence.
The argument uses the fact that 'existent' applies to many diverse things.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector argues that the 'shared concept' might be ambiguous or that the argument assumes what it wants to prove.
The objection has two interpretations: one trivial, one question-begging.
4
Follow the reply
Razi replies by invoking the intellect's judgment on affirmation and negation, showing the concept of affirmation is independent of any specific essence.
The reply uses the law of non-contradiction to prove the unity of the concept of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية في أن المعدوم ليس بشيء وهذه المسألة متفرعة على مسألة أخرى وهي أن الوجود هل هو مغاير للماهية أم لا؟ فذهب أبو الحسن الأشعري" إلى أن وجود كل شيء نفس ماهيته وذهب كثير من المتكلمين وجمهور الحكماء إلى أن وجود الشيء وصف مغاير لماهيته. واحتج من قال بأن الوجود زائد على الماهية بوجوه : الوجه الأول : أن الوجود وصف مشترك فيه بين الموجودات وخصوصیات الماهيات غير مشترك فيها بين الموجودات فالوجود مغاير للماهية. وإنما قلنا : إن الوجود وصف مشترك فيه بين الموجودات بوجوه : الأول : أن صريح العقل حاكم بأنه لا واسطة بين طرفي النقيضين، ولولا أن مفهوم الوجود أمر واحد واقع في مقابلة النفي، وإلا لبطل ذلك. فإن قيل : إن عنيتم بقولكم إنه لا واسطة بين النقيضين أنه لا واسطة بين تحقق تلك الحقيقة وبين لا تحققها فهذا مسلم، ولكن هذا لا يقتضي كون الوجود قدرًا مشتركا بين كل الموجودات، فإن تحقق كل حقيقة عبارة عن خصوصية تلك الحقيقة، وتعين تلك الماهية وإن عنيتم به أن ههنا مفهوما مشتركًا واحدًا بين الموجودات هو المسمى بالموجودية، فهذا هو المصادرة على المطلوب. قلنا : لا شك في أن العقل يتصور من معنى الثبوت مفهوما ومن معنى السلب مفهوماً، ويجزم بأنهما لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، وهو عند تصور مفهوم الثبوت ومفهوم السلب لا يفتقر إلى الإشارة إلى ماهية معينة وذلك يوجب كون مفهوم الثبوت أمرًا واحدًا.
The Second Issue: That the non-existent is not a thing. This issue is branched from another issue, which is whether existence is distinct from essence or not. Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held that the existence of every thing is its very essence, while many theologians (mutakallimūn) and the majority of philosophers (ḥukamā') held that the existence of a thing is an attribute distinct from its essence. Whoever said that existence is additional to essence argued by several proofs: The First Proof: That existence is a shared attribute among existents, while the particularities of essences are not shared among existents, so existence is distinct from essence. We say that existence is a shared attribute among existents by [several] aspects: The first: that the clear intellect judges that there is no intermediate between the two contradictories, and were it not that the concept of existence is one thing standing in opposition to negation, that would be invalid. If it is said: If you mean by your statement that there is no intermediate between contradictories that there is no intermediate between the actualization of that reality and its non-actualization, that is conceded, but that does not necessitate existence being a common factor among all existents, for the actualization of each reality is an expression of the particularity of that reality and the determination of that essence; and if you mean by it that there is here one common concept among existents called 'existentness' (al-mawjūdiyya), that is begging the question. We reply: There is no doubt that the intellect conceives from the meaning of affirmation (thubūt) a concept and from the meaning of negation (salb) a concept, and it judges decisively that they cannot be combined nor both removed, and when conceiving the concept of affirmation and the concept of negation it does not need to refer to any specific essence, and that necessitates that the concept of affirmation is one thing.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the second issue and the first proof for the claim that existence is additional to essence. Razi begins by stating the issue: whether the non-existent is a thing, which depends on whether existence is distinct from essence. He notes the two positions: al-Ash'ari identifies existence with essence, while most theologians and philosophers distinguish them. Then he presents the first proof from the pro-additionalists. The proof is that existence is shared among all existents, whereas the specific natures of essences are not shared, so existence must be distinct. To establish that existence is shared, he gives a first aspect based on the law of non-contradiction: the intellect conceives of affirmation and negation as two mutually exclusive and exhaustive concepts, and this concept of affirmation does not depend on any particular essence, implying it is one universal concept. He then includes an objection: the opponent might say that this argument either assumes what it wants to prove (if they mean a common concept) or merely restates the fact that each essence has its own actualization. The response is that the intellect's concept of affirmation is independent of any specific essence, proving its unity.

السلب
النقيضان
الوجود
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a denial of the law of non-contradiction.
Correction: The objection accepts the law but questions whether it proves a single shared concept of existence; it accuses the proof of equivocation or circularity.

A) Only the existence of God.

Second Argument: Division of Existent into Necessary and Possible
Razi uses logical division to prove existence is a single concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the logical division of existents into necessary and possible demonstrates that existence is a single common concept.

Core Idea
Logical division requires a shared concept; existence is that shared term.

Imam Razi presents a concise argument from logic. If we can divide all existents into two categories—the Necessary Being and possible beings—then the term 'existent' must have a meaning that applies equally to both. This argument shows that existence is a universal concept, not tied to individual essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the logical structure emerge naturally.
Notice the key terms: الموجود, الواجب لذاته, الممكن لذاته.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage has three parts: a premise (we can divide existents), a logical principle (the object of division is shared), and a conclusion (the concept 'existent' is common). Underline these in the translation.
3
Reflect on the logical principle
Why must the divided term be common to both divisions? Consider a simple division like dividing animals into humans and non-humans: 'animal' is the shared concept. The same applies here.
This is a key principle in logic: the genus is divided into species.
4
Apply the conclusion
Conclude that existence is a single concept that applies to both Allah (the Necessary) and creatures (the possible). But note: it is only conceptual commonality, not existential.
Avoid the misconception that this implies God and creation share the same kind of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : في بيان أن المفهوم من الموجودية أمر واحد، أنه يمكننا تقسيم الموجود إلى الواجب لذاته وإلى الممكن لذاته، فمورد التقسيم مشترك بين القسمين، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون المفهوم من الموجود قدرًا مشتركا بين القسمين.
The Second Proof: In demonstrating that the concept of existentness (al-mawjūdiyya) is one thing: that we can divide the existent into that which is necessary by itself and that which is possible by itself, and the object of division is shared between the two divisions, and this necessitates that the concept of existent is a common factor between the two divisions.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is the second proof for existence being a shared concept and thus distinct from essence. Razi argues that we can divide existents into the Necessary Being (God) and possible beings. In any logical division, the term being divided must have a single meaning that applies to both parts. Since 'existent' is the term divided, its concept must be common to both the necessary and the possible. If existence were not a single concept but tied to each essence, such a division would not be possible. Therefore, existence is a shared attribute.

Key Terms
الموجود
الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته
التقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that Allah and creatures share the same kind of existence (i.e., existence is identical in both).
Correction: The argument only proves that the concept of existence is common at the conceptual level; it does not entail that the mode or reality of existence is the same. Allah's existence is necessary, while creatures' existence is contingent—they differ in essence.

Checkpoint

A) That existence is impossible.
B) That the concept of existence is shared between necessary and possible beings.
C) That necessary and possible have nothing in common.
D) That existence is an accident of essences.

Key Takeaway
The logical division of existence into necessary and possible proves that 'existence' is a single concept shared by all existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Razi argues that division requires a shared concept. You divide people into 'believers' and 'disbelievers', but do you remember that both share the same concept of existence and thus the same capacity to know their Creator? Who is one person you have dismissed as 'different' that you now need to see as sharing the same existential reality as you? What will you do about it this week?
?
You are a possible being—your existence is not necessary by itself. Yet you spend your time as if you will always exist. If the concept of existence is truly shared, then your existence today is utterly dependent on the One whose existence is necessary. What would it look like to live one hour today fully conscious that you exist only because He keeps you in existence?

Belief Stability Argument for Existence
Razi's third proof that existence is one and additional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the stability of belief in existence to argue that existence is a concept additional to essence.

Core Idea
Belief in existence stays constant when our belief about an essence's details changes.

Razi presents an intuitive argument based on our own beliefs. When we consider something as existent, that belief does not waver even when we change our mind about what kind of thing it is – substance or accident, extended or not, color or taste. This stability suggests that existence is a single, independent concept, separate from the specific nature of the thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the examples Razi uses: substance/accident, extended/non-extended, color/taste.
2
Identify the key premise
Razi observes that belief in existence remains unchanged even when we change our beliefs about the particularities of the thing.
This is the core observation that drives the argument.
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Razi argues that if existence were tied to specific essences, then changing essence-beliefs would change existence-belief. But that does not happen.
The conditional is: if existence were not one concept, belief would change; it doesn't, so it is one.
4
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, the concept of existence is one thing in all cases, implying it is additional to essence.
This supports the broader theological point that existence is an attribute distinct from essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : إنا إذا اعتقدنا أن أمرًا من الأمور موجود فسواء اعتقدنا أن ذلك الأمر جوهر أو عرض، وبتقدير أن يكون جوهرًا فهو متحيز أو غير متحيز، وبتقدير أن يكون عرضا فهو إما لون أو طعم، فإن اعتقدنا في كونه موجودا يكون باقيا غير متغير، ولولا أن المفهوم من كونه موجودًا أمر واحد في الكل لوجب أن يتغير ذلك الاعتقاد عند تغير اعتقاد الخصوصيات كما إذا اعتقدنا فيه أنه جوهر، ثم اعتقدنا فيه عرض، فإنه يتغير الاعتقاد الأول.
The Third Proof: When we believe that some thing is existent, whether we believe that thing is a substance or an accident, and if it is a substance, then it is either extended or non-extended, and if it is an accident, then it is either a color or a taste, our belief concerning it that it is existent remains constant and unchanging. And were it not that the concept of its being existent is one thing in all cases, that belief would necessarily change when our belief about the particularities changes, as when we believe it is a substance, then believe it is an accident, the first belief changes.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is the third proof that the concept of existence is one and therefore additional. Razi uses an intuitive argument: when we believe something to be existent, that belief remains stable even when we change our beliefs about its specific nature (whether it is a substance or accident, extended or not, colored or tasty). If the concept of existence were tied to each specific essence, then changing our belief about the essence would necessarily change our belief about its existence. But we observe that the existence-belief persists unchanged. Therefore, existence is a single concept independent of the particularities, implying it is additional to essence.

Key Terms

الجوهر
العرض
الخصوصيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about actual attributes of objects changing rather than our beliefs about them.
Correction: Razi's argument focuses on our conceptual beliefs: the belief in existence remains steady even when we change our mind about what kind of thing it is. It is not about objective changes in the thing itself.

Checkpoint

A) Because existence is impossible.
B) Because existence is a distinct concept separate from particular essences.
C) Because essences change constantly.
D) Because belief is not reliable.

Key Takeaway
The stability of belief in existence across changes in particular attributions shows that existence is a single concept separate from essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Razi argues that your belief in something's existence remains steady even when you change your mind about its details. Where in your life have you held onto a false belief about someone's essence (e.g., thinking they are 'bad') because you confused their actions with their existence? How can you repent from that injustice?

The Self-Refutation of Denying Shared Existence
Razi presents a reductio argument and concludes the first four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi's fourth argument uses a reductio to prove that existence is shared among essences, and how he concludes that existence is one thing distinct from each essence.

Core Idea
Denying that existence is shared forces you to admit it is shared.

This lesson presents the fourth and final direct proof of the series: a clever reductio argument that turns the denial of shared existence against itself. Razi shows that anyone who denies that existence is a single concept shared among essences must, to avoid an infinite regress, acknowledge that it is indeed shared. Then he weaves together all four proofs to establish the key distinction between existence and essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the structure: a conditional statement ('if someone says...'), a consequence, and a concluding synthesis.
The passage is dense; reading it twice will help.
2
Identify the reductio structure
The argument assumes the opponent's position (existence is not shared) and shows it leads to an absurd requirement (separate proof for each instance). Therefore the original denial is false.
This is an indirect proof, also called proof by contradiction.
3
Understand the conclusion's two premises
The conclusion has two premises: (1) existence is one concept in all existents (established by the four proofs), and (2) each essence's particularity is known by necessity to be unshared. Together they prove existence is distinct from essence.
This conclusion is the goal of the entire sequence of proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أن من قال : الوجود غير مشترك فيه بين الماهيات يلزمه الاعتراف بأن الوجود مشترك فيه بين الماهيات وإلا لكنا نفتقر في كل واحد واحد من مسمى الوجود بأنه غير مشترك فيه بين الماهيات إلى دليل منفصل، ولما كان الحكم على الوجود بأنه غير مشترك فيه، كل وجود علمنا أن الوجود من حيث أنه وجود مفهوم واحد. فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الوجود أمر واحد في جميع الموجودات، وأما أن خصوصية ماهية كل واحد من الماهيات غير مشترك فيها بين الماهيات فمعلوم بالضرورة، فثبت بمجموع هاتين المقدمتين أن وجود كل شيء مغاير لماهيته.
The Fourth Proof: That whoever says existence is not shared among essences is compelled to admit that existence is shared among essences; otherwise we would need a separate proof for each and every instance of what is called existence that it is not shared among essences. And since we judge existence as being not shared, [we realize that] every existence we know that existence as such is one concept. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that existence is one thing in all existents. And as for the fact that the particularity of the essence of each one of the essences is not shared among the essences, that is known by necessity. So it is established by the combination of these two premises that the existence of every thing is distinct from its essence.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof for existence being a single concept, followed by a conclusion that synthesizes all four proofs. The fourth proof is a reductio: if someone denies that existence is shared among essences, then they must admit it is shared to avoid an infinite regress of proofs. Because to prove that each instance of existence is not shared would require a separate proof for each, which is impossible. Moreover, when we judge that existence is not shared, we already have a unitary concept of existence that we are applying. Therefore, existence is one concept. Then Razi concludes from all four proofs that existence is one thing in all existents, and it is self-evident that particular essences are not shared. Therefore, existence is additional to essence.

Key Terms
الوجود
الماهية
الاشتراك
الدليل المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It leads to the conclusion that existence does not exist.
B) It would require a separate proof for each thing's existence not being shared.
C) It proves that existence is a particular of each essence.
D) It shows that existence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Razi's fourth proof shows that denying shared existence is self-refuting, and the combined four proofs establish that existence is one thing distinct from each essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the countless things around you—trees, animals, people, stars—do you intuitively feel that they all share something called 'existence'? If you were to deny that, as some might, would you be able to maintain that denial without falling into logical contradiction? What does this say about how your own mind unavoidably grasps reality?
?
The argument shows that even to deny a shared truth about existence, you must already possess a unified concept of it. Is there any area of your life where you reject a truth that you actually rely on every moment? Could the very act of denying something about God, your own soul, or moral responsibility betray that you already know it deep down? What would it take to stop denying and start admitting?
?
Razi concludes that existence is one thing in all existents, while each thing's essence is unique. Do you live as though your existence is just 'your own' or do you recognize it as a gift shared with all creation? How would your interactions with others change if you truly believed that the same existence that makes you real also makes them real—and that both are distinct from what you essentially are? What one concrete step can you take today to honour that shared existence?

The Modality Argument for Existence Distinct from Essence
Razi presents a second proof based on the modality of possible beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the modality of possible beings demonstrates that existence is additional to essence.

Core Idea
A possible essence is neither existence nor non-existence; it underlies both.

Imam Razi now presents a second, independent proof that existence is not identical to essence. He uses a simple logical observation: if we consider a possible thing as existing, it cannot be non-existent; if as non-existent, it cannot exist. Since the thing itself is neither necessarily existent nor necessarily non-existent, its essence must be something other than either attribute. This argument highlights the conceptual priority of essence over existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi sets up the argument by assuming a possible being and then considering it under two conditions.
The passage includes an alternative formulation; focus first on the main argument.
2
Understand the two hypothetical considerations
Identify the two scenarios: (1) the possible being considered with existence – it cannot accept non-existence, so it becomes necessary. (2) Considered with non-existence – it cannot accept existence, so it becomes impossible.
This shows that the essence itself, when taken without either attribute, remains possible.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
If essence were identical to existence, it would always be necessary; if identical to non-existence, always impossible. Since it is neither, essence must be distinct from both. This establishes the proof.
The alternative formulation reinforces the same point: possibility means the essence can be described as existent or non-existent.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters for kalam: it shows that existence is an accident added to essence, which is a key premise in arguments for God's existence and attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية على أن الوجود مغاير للماهية هي أنه لا شك أن في الموجودات ما هو ممكن لذاته، فنقول : ذلك الموجود الذي هو ممكن لذاته إن أخذناه مع اعتبار الوجود كان غير قابل للعدم ؛ لأن الشيء حال كونه موجودًا لا يقبل العدم، وما لا يقبل العدم لا يكون ممكن الوجود والعدم، وإن أخذناه مع اعتبار العدم كان غير قابل للوجود ؛ لأن الشيء حال كونه معدوما لا يقبل الوجود، وما لا يقبل الوجود لا يكون ممكن الوجود والعدم، فلو لم تكن ماهيته مغايرة للوجود والعدم لما كانت الماهية ممكنة أصلاً، ولما كانت ممكنة، علمنا أنها مغايرة للوجود والعدم. ويمكن تقرير هذه الحجة بعبارة أخرى وهي أن قولنا في الشيء إنه ممكن الوجود والعدم أنه لا يمتنع أن يحكم على تلك الماهية بكونها موجودة وبكونها
The Second Proof that existence is distinct from essence is that there is no doubt that among existents there is what is possible by itself. We say: That existent which is possible by itself, if we take it together with the consideration of existence, it is not receptive of non-existence, because a thing while it is existent does not accept non-existence, and what does not accept non-existence cannot be possible in respect of existence and non-existence. And if we take it together with the consideration of non-existence, it is not receptive of existence, because a thing while it is non-existent does not accept existence, and what does not accept existence cannot be possible in respect of existence and non-existence. So if its essence were not distinct from existence and non-existence, the essence would not be possible at all. And since it is possible, we know that it is distinct from existence and non-existence. This proof can be formulated in another way: that our statement about a thing that it is possible in respect of existence and non-existence means that it is not impossible to judge of that essence that it is existent and that it is [non-existent]...
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is an entirely separate second proof for the thesis that existence is additional to essence, based on the modality of possible beings. Razi begins by noting that some existents are possible in themselves (contingent). He then considers a possible being under two hypothetical conditions: if we consider it together with existence, it becomes necessary (cannot accept non-existence); if we consider it together with non-existence, it becomes impossible (cannot accept existence). Since the essence itself is neither necessary nor impossible but possible, it must be distinct from both existence and non-existence. If the essence were identical with existence, it could never be non-existent; if identical with non-existence, it could never exist. Thus essence is a third thing underlying both modalities. Razi also mentions an alternative formulation: the judgment that a thing is possible means that its essence can be described as existent or as non-existent without contradiction, implying essence is distinct from both attributes.

Key Terms
الممكن لذاته
الوجود
العدم
الماهية
الوجوب
الامتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that existence is a real attribute separate from essence, making essence something that can exist or not.
Correction: The argument only shows that essence is conceptually distinct from existence and non-existence; it does not prove that existence is a real accident inhering in essence. Razi's aim is to show the logical priority of essence, not to establish a separate ontological status.

Checkpoint
What happens to a possible essence when considered together with existence, according to Razi's second proof?
A) It becomes necessary and cannot accept non-existence.
B) It becomes impossible.
C) It remains possible.
D) It becomes non-existent.

Key Takeaway
A possible being's essence is distinct from both existence and non-existence because, considered under either attribute, it loses its possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you ever pause to realize that you are not defined by whether you exist or not—your essence goes deeper? How would your life change if you truly lived from that essence rather than from your circumstances?
?
This argument shows that your possibility is a gift: you are not necessary, so every moment you exist is a continuous act of being given existence by the Necessary Being. Have you truly felt the weight of being constantly sustained, or do you take your existence for granted?
?
If your essence is distinct from existence and non-existence, then your worth is not tied to your worldly existence or achievements. What would it mean for you to anchor your identity in that unchangeable essence rather than in things that can cease to exist?

The Distinction Between Conception and Assent Proves Existence Is Additional
Razi presents the fourth proof using the distinction between conception and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intuitive difference between merely conceiving a thing and judging it to exist demonstrates that existence is an attribute added to the essence, not identical to it.

Core Idea
Our ability to distinguish 'blackness' from 'blackness exists' proves existence is additional.

Imam Razi now presents a fourth proof that relies on a basic cognitive distinction: the difference between forming a concept (tasawwur) and making a judgment (tasdiq). By reflecting on this distinction, we see that existence cannot be identical to the essence. The passage uses a simple but powerful example that is accessible to anyone's immediate intuition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once. Let the argument sink in before analyzing.
Notice the example of 'blackness' and 'blackness exists'.
2
Identify the two cognitive acts
The passage distinguishes between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Conception is simply having a mental image; assent is affirming a judgment about something.
This distinction is foundational in Islamic logic and epistemology.
3
Analyze the example of 'blackness'
When you say only 'blackness', you have a conception but no judgment. When you say 'blackness exists', you add the judgment of existence. Rational people immediately recognize that saying 'blackness' alone neither affirms nor denies anything.
This shows that existence adds something beyond the mere concept.
4
Draw the theological conclusion
If existence were identical to the essence, then saying 'blackness' would already imply existence, making 'blackness exists' redundant. Since it is not redundant, existence must be an additional attribute.
This proof is meant to show that existence is not part of the essence but an extra property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة على أن الوجود زائد على الماهية : هي أنا في بداهة العقول ندرك الفرق بين التصور والتصديق بين قولنا : السواد موجود معلوم بالبداهة، ولذلك فإن من قال السواد وسكت حكم كل عاقل بأنه ما نفى وما أثبت وما ذكر كلاما.
The fourth argument that existence is additional to the essence is that we perceive, in the primary intuitions of intellects, the difference between conception and assent, as in our saying: 'Blackness exists' – it is known by self-evidence. For that reason, if someone says 'Blackness' and stops, every rational person judges that he neither negated nor affirmed anything, and he did not utter a complete statement.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof that existence is additional to essence. It relies on the intuitive distinction between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). The example is the phrase 'Blackness exists'. When we simply say 'Blackness' without any predicate, we have only a conception; we have not affirmed or negated anything. But when we say 'Blackness exists', we have made a judgment (assent) that existence is predicated of blackness. This judgment adds something—namely, the attribution of existence—to the mere essence of blackness. If existence were identical to the essence or part of it, then just saying 'Blackness' would already include existence, and the judgment 'Blackness exists' would be redundant or meaningless. Since every rational person recognizes a difference between merely conceiving blackness and judging that it exists, this proves that existence is additional to the essence.

Key Terms
وجود (wujud)
ماهية (mahiyya)
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
بداهة (badaha)
سواد (sawad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that saying 'Blackness exists' is merely stating a fact without adding any new component, so existence is not separate from the concept.
Correction: The proof shows that the act of assenting to existence is distinct from simply conceiving the essence. Rational people recognize that 'Blackness' alone does not include existence, so existence must be an additional attribute.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what does a rational person understand when someone says only 'Blackness' without adding 'exists'?
A) He has negated blackness.
B) He has affirmed existence for blackness.
C) He has neither negated nor affirmed anything.
D) He has made an incomplete judgment about blackness.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive difference between conceiving an essence and judging it to exist proves that existence is an additional attribute beyond the essence.

The Self-Evident Distinction
Razi presents the fourth proof based on the self-evident distinction between conception and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intuitive difference between saying a thing's name and asserting its existence proves that existence is not identical to essence.

Core Idea
The mere mention of a thing makes no claim; asserting its existence does—this proves existence is distinct.

Imam Razi builds his fourth proof on a simple observation: everyone recognizes the difference between just conceiving of a thing and making a judgment about its existence. He argues that if 'existence' were identical to each essence, the two expressions would be indistinguishable. This lesson walks through the core of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement carefully
Note how Razi grounds his argument in self-evident knowledge ('badahah'). He states that we perceive the difference between conception and assent by primal intuition.
2
Examine the example of 'blackness'
See why 'blackness' alone is incomplete—no claim is made—while 'blackness exists' is a truth-claim that requires evidence. This illustrates the distinction.
3
Grasp the logical conclusion
If existence were identical to essence, the difference between 'blackness' and 'blackness exists' would vanish, contradicting self-evidence. Therefore, existence is additional.
This is the core inference of the proof.
4
Prepare for the objection
Understand that the proof leads directly to an objection about verbal difference, which will be addressed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة على أن الوجود زائد على الماهية : هي أنا في بداهة العقول ندرك الفرق بين التصور والتصديق بين قولنا : السواد موجود معلوم بالبداهة، ولذلك فإن من قال السواد وسكت حكم كل عاقل بأنه ما تفى وما أثبت وما ذكر كلاما مفيداً، وإذا قال: السواد موجود أو غير موجود فقد نفى أو أثبت، وادعى ويطالب على صحة ما ذكره بالحجة، ولو كان كونه موجودًا هو نفس کونه سوادًا لما حصل الفرق المذكور المعلوم بالبداهة. اللفظ لا بحسب المعنى. فإن قيل : هذا الفرق الذي ذكرتم واقع بحسب فالجواب : أنا نعلم بالبداهة أن أن من للعالم صانعاً، ثم أقام الدليل على دعواه لم يكن مطلوبه بهذه الحجة نفس اللفظ بل المعنى، فسقط ما ذكروه من السؤال.
The fourth proof that existence is additional to quiddity: We perceive in the primality of minds the difference between conception and assent, between our saying 'blackness exists' known by self-evidence. Therefore, whoever says 'blackness' and stops, every rational person judges that he has neither negated nor affirmed anything and has not uttered a useful sentence. But when he says 'blackness exists' or 'does not exist', he has negated or affirmed, made a claim, and is required to prove the correctness of what he said with evidence. If its being existent were the very same as its being blackness, this difference which is known by self-evidence would not occur. (This difference is) in the wording, not according to meaning. If it is said: This difference you mention occurs only verbally. The answer: We know by self-evidence that if someone says 'the world has a Maker', then offers proof for his claim, what he intended by that proof is not the mere wording but the meaning. Thus what they mentioned of the question collapses.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument) followed by an objection and answer. Razi presents the fourth proof: We intuitively know the difference between conception (mere apprehension of 'blackness') and assent (judging that 'blackness exists'). When someone simply says 'blackness', he has made no claim; but when he says 'blackness exists' or 'does not exist', he has made a truth claim that requires evidence. If existence were identical to the essence of blackness, the phrase 'blackness exists' would be redundant and no different from 'blackness' alone, contradicting this evident distinction. Therefore existence must be additional to essence. An objector then says this difference is merely verbal, not conceptual. Razi refutes: If someone says 'the world has a Maker' and then proves it, his proof is about the meaning, not the words. Similarly, the difference between 'blackness' and 'blackness exists' is a difference in meaning, not just wording. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur)
تصديق (tasdiq)
بداهة (badahah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'existence' and 'essence' are identical concepts, such that saying 'X exists' adds no new meaning.
Correction: The self-evident difference between simple conception and assertive judgment shows that existence is a distinct attribute added to the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the key self-evident observation that forms the basis of the fourth proof?
A) Everyone knows that blackness is a color.
B) There is a clear distinction between saying 'blackness' and 'blackness exists'.
C) Existence is a predicate that adds nothing.
D) Conception and assent are identical for simple terms.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive difference between conceiving a thing and judging it to exist proves that existence is an additional attribute, not identical to essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a concept you are absolutely certain of—like your own existence. When you say 'I exist', do you feel that existence adds something beyond the concept of 'you'? If existence were identical to your essence, could you ever doubt your own existence? What does that tell you about the reality of the extra attribute?
?
When you recite the Shahada, you say 'Ashhadu an la ilaha illa Allah'. How does the act of bearing witness (assent) differ from merely thinking about the words? In light of Razi's argument, what does this distinction demand from your heart, not just your tongue?
?
Consider a time you were uncertain whether something existed (e.g., a rumor or a promise). Did you notice a shift in your mind when you moved from mere conception of the thing to affirming its existence? How does that everyday experience prove the lesson's point, and what does it reveal about your intellectual honesty when you claim something exists?

The Objection and Its Refutation
Razi refutes the objection that the conception-assent distinction is merely verbal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that the difference between conception and assent is merely verbal, not conceptual.

Core Idea
The objection is refuted by the self-evident fact that proof aims at meaning, not words.

After presenting the proof from the distinction between conception and assent, Razi anticipates an objection: perhaps the difference is only in wording, not in meaning. He responds by appealing to another self-evident truth: when someone asserts a claim like 'the world has a Creator' and then proves it, the proof is about the meaning, not the mere words. This lesson examines the objection and its decisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection statement
Notice how the objector tries to reduce the distinction to a linguistic formality, claiming the difference is only in wording.
The phrase 'اللفظ لا بحسب المعنى' may be part of the objection.
2
Examine Razi's response
He uses the example of a proof for the world's Creator to show that meaning is what matters, not words.
3
Understand the collapse of the objection
If the difference were only verbal, then proofs would be about words, which is absurd. Thus the objection fails.
4
Connect back to the fourth proof
See how the refutation strengthens the original argument that existence is additional to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة على أن الوجود زائد على الماهية : هي أنا في بداهة العقول ندرك الفرق بين التصور والتصديق بين قولنا : السواد موجود معلوم بالبداهة، ولذلك فإن من قال السواد وسكت حكم كل عاقل بأنه ما تفى وما أثبت وما ذكر كلاما مفيداً، وإذا قال: السواد موجود أو غير موجود فقد نفى أو أثبت، وادعى ويطالب على صحة ما ذكره بالحجة، ولو كان كونه موجودًا هو نفس کونه سوادًا لما حصل الفرق المذكور المعلوم بالبداهة. اللفظ لا بحسب المعنى. فإن قيل : هذا الفرق الذي ذكرتم واقع بحسب فالجواب : أنا نعلم بالبداهة أن أن من للعالم صانعاً، ثم أقام الدليل على دعواه لم يكن مطلوبه بهذه الحجة نفس اللفظ بل المعنى، فسقط ما ذكروه من السؤال.
The fourth proof that existence is additional to quiddity: We perceive in the primality of minds the difference between conception and assent, between our saying 'blackness exists' known by self-evidence. Therefore, whoever says 'blackness' and stops, every rational person judges that he has neither negated nor affirmed anything and has not uttered a useful sentence. But when he says 'blackness exists' or 'does not exist', he has negated or affirmed, made a claim, and is required to prove the correctness of what he said with evidence. If its being existent were the very same as its being blackness, this difference which is known by self-evidence would not occur. (This difference is) in the wording, not according to meaning. If it is said: This difference you mention occurs only verbally. The answer: We know by self-evidence that if someone says 'the world has a Maker', then offers proof for his claim, what he intended by that proof is not the mere wording but the meaning. Thus what they mentioned of the question collapses.

Commentary
This passage is a proof (argument) followed by an objection and answer. Razi presents the fourth proof: We intuitively know the difference between conception (mere apprehension of 'blackness') and assent (judging that 'blackness exists'). When someone simply says 'blackness', he has made no claim; but when he says 'blackness exists' or 'does not exist', he has made a truth claim that requires evidence. Therefore existence must be additional to essence. An objector then says this difference is merely verbal, not conceptual. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the distinction between conception and assent is only a matter of wording, not a real difference in meaning.
Correction: Razi shows that in making and proving claims, we are concerned with meaning, not mere words, so the distinction is conceptual.

Checkpoint
How does Razi refute the objection that the difference is merely verbal?
A) He denies that there is any difference at all.
B) He argues that proof is always about meaning, not words.
C) He says the objector cannot understand self-evident truths.
D) He changes the subject to another example.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the conception-assent distinction is merely verbal is refuted by the self-evident fact that rational proof targets meaning, not wording.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make or hear an argument for God's existence, do you focus more on the exact words or the underlying meaning? How does this lesson challenge you to examine your own approach?

?

Existence is Additional to Essence: The Intuitive Proof
Razi argues existence is additional to essence via intuitive distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's intuitive argument that existence is additional to essence based on the obvious difference between affirming and denying existence of the same thing.

Core Idea
The intuitive difference between saying 'X exists' and 'X does not exist' proves existence is not the same as essence.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a compelling argument for the real distinction between existence and essence. He begins with a simple observation: when we say 'blackness is existent' versus 'blackness is non-existent,' these statements clearly differ in meaning. This difference, known by intuitive reason (badīha), would not arise if existence were identical to essence. Even an objection that this difference is merely verbal is refuted by Razi, showing that the argument is about meaning, not just words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from argument to objection to refutation.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Understand the initial argument
Identify Razi's core claim: if existence were identical to essence, then statements affirming and denying existence of the same essence would not differ. Since they clearly differ (by intuition), existence must be additional.
The key phrase is 'المعلوم بالبداهة' – known by intuition.
3
Analyze the objection and refutation
The objection claims the difference is merely verbal. Razi refutes it by analogy: if someone proves the world has a Creator, they are proving a meaning, not just words. Similarly, the difference between 'is existent' and 'is non-existent' is a real difference in meaning.
Notice how Razi uses an analogy to shift the burden of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مفيداً، وإذا قال: السواد موجود أو غير موجود فقد نفى أو أثبت، وادعى ويطالب على صحة ما ذكره بالحجة، ولو كان كونه موجودًا هو نفس کونه سوادًا لما حصل الفرق المذكور المعلوم بالبداهة. اللفظ لا بحسب المعنى. فإن قيل : هذا الفرق الذي ذكرتم واقع بحسب فالجواب : أنا نعلم بالبداهة أن أن من للعالم صانعاً، ثم أقام الدليل على دعواه لم يكن مطلوبه بهذه الحجة نفس اللفظ بل المعنى، فسقط ما ذكروه من السؤال.
Beneficially, and when he says: blackness is existent or non-existent, he has negated or affirmed, and he claims and is required to provide evidence for the correctness of what he mentioned. And if its being existent were identical to its being blackness, the mentioned difference known by intuition would not occur. The wording, not according to meaning. If it is said: This difference you mentioned occurs according to [wording]. The answer: We know by intuition that [the world has a Maker], then he provided evidence for his claim; his demand by this evidence was not the wording itself but the meaning. So what they mentioned of the objection falls away.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a proof that existence (wujūd) is additional (zāʾid) to essence (māhīya). Razi begins by noting that when one says 'blackness is existent' or 'blackness is non-existent', each statement carries informative content that requires evidence. If existence were identical to blackness itself, the two statements would be equivalent and the clear distinction we intuit between them would disappear. This difference is known by intuition (bi al- badīha). The objection claims this difference is merely verbal (bi ḥasab al-lafẓ), not real. Razi refutes this by analogy: if someone says 'the world has a Creator' and then proves it, the proof concerns meaning, not the wording of the statement. Similarly, the distinction between 'black is existent' and 'black is non-existent' is a real difference in meaning, not just words. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
وجود (wujūd)
ماهية (māhīya)
بداهة (badīha)
لفظ (lafẓ)
معنى (maʿnā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference between 'x is existent' and 'x is non-existent' is a mere verbal difference, not a real conceptual difference.
Correction: Razi shows that the difference is in meaning (maʿnā), not just words, by analogy with proving the world has a Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's initial argument in this passage?
A) To prove that existence is identical to essence.
B) To prove that existence is additional to essence by showing the obvious difference between affirming and denying existence of the same essence.
C) To prove that blackness is the only real essence.
D) To reject the need for evidence in statements about existence.

Key Takeaway
Razi's intuitive proof shows that the clear difference between affirming and denying existence for the same essence demonstrates that existence is not identical to essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I exist,' do you truly feel the weight of that statement as a separate reality added to your human essence, or do you take it for granted? In what practical ways does your awareness of your own contingent existence shape your gratitude to Allah?
?
If someone denied the self-evident difference between your existence and non-existence, would you be able to defend it without relying on theological jargon? Razi used an analogy to the proof of the Creator. Consider: how often do you defend your most basic beliefs about Allah's existence with the same clarity and conviction?
?
The objection Razi refutes is that the distinction between existence and non-existence is 'just words.' Are there areas in your faith where you have reduced Allah's attributes or your own accountability to mere verbal formulas, forgetting the real meaning behind them? What would it take for you to treat the difference between 'Allah exists' and 'Allah does not exist' as a life-or-death reality?

Objection and Answer on Existence Being Additional
Razi presents an objection and his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection against existence being additional to essence and how Imam Razi answers it by identifying the essence as the subject of existence.

Core Idea
The objection collapses once we realize that essence is the subject of existence without being pre-existent.

One of the strongest objections against the Ash'ari view that existence is additional to essence is that it leads to absurd consequences. In this passage, Imam Razi presents that objection and then provides a precise answer that hinges on understanding the status of the essence itself. Let's work through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice that this is an objection-and-answer unit.
The Arabic is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the objection's two horns
The objector presents a dilemma: if existence depends on the essence being existent, circularity/regress; if not, an existent attribute subsists in pure non-existence. Identify both branches.
3
Grasp the wall illustration
The objector uses the example of a wall: we perceive only attributes; if an attribute can subsist in non-existence, the wall itself could be non-existent. This shows the absurdity.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze Razi's answer
Razi's answer: the locus of existence is the essence considered abstractly, which is distinct from existence and non-existence. Thus, existence does not subsist in non-existence.
The essence is a 'third thing', not non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج من قال الوجود غير زائد على الماهية بأن قال : لو كان الوجود زائدا على الماهية لكان قيام الوجود بالماهية وإن توقف على كون الماهية موجودة لزم إما كون الشيء مشروطا بنفسه، أو وقوع التسلسل، وإن لم يتوقف عليه فحينئذ يلزم قيام الصفة الثبوتية بالعدم المحض وذلك محال ؛ لأن المحسوس عندنا من الذوات ليس إلا الصفات، وإذا جوزنا قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، فالجدار الذي نشاهده لا نشاهد منه إلا لونه وكثافته وثقله، فإذا لم يبعد قيام الصفة الموجودة بالمحل المعدوم، لم يبعد أن يكون الموصوف بالكثافة والثقل واللون والمخصوص معدوما محضاً، وذلك يقتضي وقوع الشك في أن ذات الجدار وذات الإنسان هل موجودة أم لا؟ ومعلوم أن ذلك فاسد. والجواب عنه : أن محل الوجود هو الماهية ثم إن الماهية من حيث هي هي ماهية مغايرة للوجود والعدم، فلا يلزم من ذلك قيام الموجود بالمعدوم.
And those who said existence is not additional to essence argued by saying: If existence were additional to essence, then the subsistence of existence in the essence—if it depends on the essence being existent—entails either the thing being conditioned by itself or the occurrence of infinite regress; and if it does not depend on it, then a positive attribute would subsist in pure non-existence, and that is impossible. Because what is perceived by us of entities is nothing but attributes. And if we allow the subsistence of an existent in a non-existent subject, then the wall we observe—we observe from it only its color, density, and weight. So if it is not remote for an existent attribute to subsist in a non-existent subject, it is not remote for the thing described by density, weight, color, and particularity to be pure non-existence. That would lead to doubt about whether the essence of the wall and the essence of the human are existent or not. And it is known that this is invalid. The answer is: The locus of existence is the essence; then the essence, considered as essence, is distinct from existence and non-existence, so the subsistence of an existent in a non-existent does not follow.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objectors (those who deny that existence is additional) argue: if existence were additional to essence, then either (a) existence's subsistence in essence depends on that essence being existent, leading to circularity or infinite regress, or (b) it does not depend, implying an existent attribute subsists in pure non-existence, which is absurd. They illustrate by observing that we only perceive attributes like color, density, weight; if an attribute can subsist in a non-existent subject, then the wall itself could be non-existent while its attributes exist, leading to skepticism. Razi answers by clarifying: the locus (maḥall) of existence is the essence considered abstractly, which is distinct from both existence and non-existence. Thus, existence does not require the essence to be pre-existent, nor does it entail subsistence in pure non-existence because the essence is not non-existent in itself; it is a third thing.

Key Terms
وجود (wujūd)
ماهية (māhīya)
صفة ثبوتية (ṣifa thubūtīya)
عدم (ʿadam)
محل (maḥall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection proves that existence cannot be additional to essence because it leads to absurd consequences.
Correction: The objection only shows that existence cannot subsist in a non-existent subject, but the essence is not non-existent; it is distinct from both existence and non-existence. Therefore, the objection fails.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise in the objection against existence being additional?
A) That existence must be identical to essence because otherwise attributes would be separate.
B) That if existence is additional, it would either cause circularity or require an existent to subsist in non-existence.
C) That the essence can never be the subject of existence.
D) That the wall's attributes cannot exist if the wall is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the objection against existence being additional fails because the essence, as the subject of existence, is distinct from both existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objectors argued that if existence is additional, we could doubt the existence of everything around us. Have you ever felt a similar doubt about the reality of creation? How does this lesson reinforce that Allah's act of giving existence is real and certain? Will you today affirm in your heart that every existent depends entirely on Him?
?
Razi answers that the essence, in itself, is neither existent nor non-existent. How does this truth about your own essence humble you? You exist only by an added gift from Allah. Are you living as if your existence is self-grounded or as a constant reception? Write down one concrete way you will remind yourself today of your dependence on Allah for existence.

The First Proof: Realization Implies Existence
Razi's first proof that the non-existent is not a thing, using the concept of realization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first proof that if the non-existent were a thing, it would be existent, which is contradictory.

Core Idea
If essences were real in non-existence, they would be existent—an impossibility.

Razi begins his refutation by focusing on the concept of 'realization' (taḥaqquq). If the non-existent were a thing, it would have some reality. But reality in the external world is exactly what we mean by existence. This proof shows the absurdity of claiming that non-existent essences have any being.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice how Razi uses the terms 'realization' and 'establishment' as synonyms for existence.
2
Identify the main claims
The argument has two key premises: (1) if essences were realized without existence, their realization would be additional to their essence; (2) realization is exactly existence. The conclusion is a contradiction.
3
Examine the blackness example
Razi provides an intuitive example: conceptualizing 'blackness established in non-existence' is different from affirming it. This shows that establishment is something additional.
This reinforces the idea that realization is not inherent to the essence.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the hypothesis leads to an impossibility, the claim that the non-existent is a thing is false.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا في بيان أن المعدوم ليس بشيء وجوه : البرهان الأول : أن هذه الماهيات لو كانت متحققة في الخارج حال عرائها عن الوجود لكانت في كونها متحققة خارج الذهن ومباينة لخصوصياتها المعينة، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة فيلزم أن يكون تحقق كل ماهية تقررها زائدا على خصوصيتها، ولا معنى للوجود إلا هذا التحقق والتقرر فيلزم كونها موجودة حال كونها معدومة، وذلك محال. ومما يدل على أن كونها مختلفة خارج الذهن أمر زائد على ماهيتها المخصوصة: أنا إذا قلنا : السواد متقرر حال العدم فنحن ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين ذلك التصور وهذا التصديق، ولولا أن تقررها خارج الذهن حال عدمها أمر زائد على ماهيتها وإلا لما بقي فرق بين التصور والتصديق وذلك محال على ما قررناه فثبت أن الماهيات لو كانت متقررة حال عدمها، لكانت موجودة حال عدمها ولما كان محالا علمنا أن القول بأن الماهيات متقررة حال عدمها محال. البرهان الثاني : أن الذوات الثابتة في العدم إما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بثبوت الذوات المعدومة، أما أن كونها متناهية باطل فبالاتفاق، وأما أن كونها غير متناهية باطل فذلك لأن مجموع الذوات المعدومة حين ما لم يخرج منها شيء إلى الوجود كانت أكثر مما يبقى منها في العدم بعد خروج بعضها إلى الوجود وإلا لكان الشيء مع غيره كهو لا مع غيره، وذلك محال، وإذا كان كذلك كانت الذوات التي بقيت الآن في العدم أقل من مجموع الذوات التي كانت معدومة عند ما لم يخرج شيء منها إلى الوجود، وما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه، فالذوات التي بقيت الآن في العدم متناهية، والتي خرج منها إلى الوجود متناهية ومجموع المتناهي مع المتناهي يكون متناهياً، فثبت أن القول بكون الذوات غير متناهية محال. البرهان الثالث : هذه الماهيات من حيث إنها هي ممكنة لذواتها، وكل ممكن محدث فهذه الماهيات من حيث هي هي محدثة فيلزم أن تكون هذه الماهيات مسبوقة بالنفي المحض والعدم الصرف، وهو المطلوب.
And we have proofs that the non-existent is not a thing: The first proof: If these essences were realized in the external world while stripped of existence, they would be, in their being realized externally and distinct from their specific particularities, that by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So the realization of each essence—its establishment—must be additional to its particularity. And there is no meaning of existence except this realization and establishment. So they would be existent while being non-existent, and that is impossible. And among the indications that their being different externally is additional to their specific essence is: When we say 'blackness is established in the state of non-existence' we perceive an intuitive distinction between that conceptualization and this affirmation. And were it not that its establishment externally in the state of non-existence is additional to its essence, there would be no difference between conceptualization and affirmation—and that is impossible, as we have established. So it is established that if essences were established in the state of non-existence, they would be existent in the state of non-existence. And since that is impossible, we know that the statement that essences are established in the state of non-existence is impossible. The second proof: The entities subsisting in non-existence are either finite or infinite, and both divisions are false. So the claim of the subsistence of non-existent entities is false. As for their being finite, it is false by agreement. As for their being infinite, it is false because: the totality of non-existent entities at the time when none of them had emerged into existence was greater than what remains in non-existence after some emerge into existence—otherwise the thing with others would be like it without others, which is impossible. If that is so, then the entities that now remain in non-existence are less than the totality of entities that were non-existent when none had emerged into existence. And whatever is less than something else is finite. So the entities now remaining in non-existence are finite, and those that emerged into existence are finite, and the sum of the finite with the finite is finite. So it is established that the claim that entities are infinite is impossible. The third proof: These essences, considered in themselves, are possible in themselves, and every possible is originated. So these essences, considered in themselves, are originated. Consequently, they must be preceded by pure negation and absolute non-existence, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage contains three proof's (barāhīn) that the possible non-existent (maʿdūm) is not a thing or entity. The first proof argues from the nature of realization (taḥaqquq) and establishment (taqarrur): if essences were realized externally without existence, their realization would be additional to their specific nature. But realization is exactly existence, so they would be existent and non-existent simultaneously, which is absurd. The intuitive distinction between simply conceptualizing 'blackness established in non-existence' and affirming that it is actually established externally shows that establishment adds something, namely existence. The second proof uses finitude: non-existent essences, if real, must be either finite or infinite. Finite is rejected by agreement (all consider the set of possible non-existent things potentially infinite?). Infinite is rejected by a cardinality argument: the set of all possible non-existent things before any existed is larger than the set after some exist (since some are now existent). But since the remaining set is smaller, it is finite, and the set that came into existence is also finite, so the total is finite, contradicting the assumption of infinity. Thus the hypothesis fails. The third proof is concise: all possible essences are contingent (mumkin) and thus originated (muḥdath). As originated, they are preceded by pure non-existence, so in themselves they are nothing before existence. This establishes the desired conclusion: the non-existent is not a thing.

Key Terms
برهان
ماهية
تحقق
تقرر
معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the non-existent can have some form of reality without existing.
Correction: Razi proves that any reality implies existence, so non-existence cannot have any reality.

Checkpoint
What does Razi's first proof conclude about the claim that essences are established in non-existence?
A) It is possible if we distinguish between realization and existence.
B) It is impossible because it would require essences to be both existent and non-existent.
C) It is possible only for blackness.
D) It is impossible because essences are finite.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof demonstrates that claiming the non-existent is a thing leads to the contradiction of simultaneous existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi proves that the non-existent is nothing at all. Everything you own, every person you love, and your own self were once non-existent—absolute nothing. Does your heart truly believe that only Allah has eternal existence, or do you cling to things as if they have permanence?
?
If existence is the same as realization, then your very being is a continuous gift from Allah. Do you live each moment conscious that you are nothing without Him sustaining you, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient?
?
The proof shows that claiming reality for things in non-existence leads to contradiction. Are you holding onto any 'future plans' or 'hopes' as if they are already real, trusting in them more than in Allah's decree? Will you repent of that reliance and affirm that only Allah gives existence?

The Second Proof: Finitude Argument
Razi's second proof that the non-existent is not a thing, based on finitude of the set of non-existent entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that the set of non-existent entities cannot be infinite, so the non-existent cannot be things.

Core Idea
If non-existent entities were real, they would be finite—contradicting the infinite number of possibles.

Razi's second proof uses a quantitative argument. If non-existent entities were real, they must be either finite or infinite. The finite option is rejected by consensus, and the infinite option leads to a logical contradiction about cardinalities. Thus, the hypothesis fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the structure: a dichotomy between finite and infinite.
Note that 'by agreement' refers to the consensus that the number of non-existent entities is not finite.
2
Understand the infinite argument
Razi argues that if the set were infinite, then the subset remaining after some exist would be smaller, but any smaller set is finite—contradicting the assumption of infinity.
This is a reductio on the infinite nature.
3
Follow the cardinality reasoning
The totality before any existence is greater than the remainder after some exist. Since the remainder is less, it is finite. Then the sum of finite parts is finite.
4
Conclusion
Both options are impossible, so the original claim that non-existent entities are real is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And we have proofs that the non-existent is not a thing: The first proof: If these essences were realized in the external world while stripped of existence, they would be, in their being realized externally and distinct from their specific particularities, that by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So it is established that if essences were established in the state of non-existence, they would be existent in the state of non-existence. The second proof: The entities subsisting in non-existence are either finite or infinite, and both divisions are false. So the claim of the subsistence of non-existent entities is false. Why does Razi reject the possibility that non-existent entities are infinite?

?

The Third Proof: From Possibility to Origination
Razi's third proof that the non-existent is not a thing, based on the contingency and origination of possible essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that because possible essences are originated, they must be preceded by absolute non-existence, meaning they are not things in non-existence.

Core Idea
Possible essences are originated and thus preceded by pure non-existence.

Razi's third proof is concise and relies on the nature of possibility. Every possible thing (mumkin) is originated (muḥdath), meaning it comes into existence after not existing. Therefore, considered in themselves, possible essences are not things; they are preceded by absolute non-existence. This directly refutes the claim that the non-existent is a thing.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the short Arabic passage and translation. Note the logical sequence: possible in itself -> originated -> preceded by non-existence.
This proof assumes the well-known kalam principle that everything possible is originated.
2
Understand the premises
The first premise: these essences are possible in themselves. The second: every possible is originated. So the essences are originated.
3
Derive the conclusion
If originated, they must be preceded by pure negation and absolute non-existence. Thus, in the state of non-existence, they are nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا في بيان أن المعدوم ليس بشيء وجوه : البرهان الأول : أن هذه الماهيات لو كانت متحققة في الخارج حال عرائها عن الوجود لكانت في كونها متحققة خارج الذهن ومباينة لخصوصياتها المعينة، وما به المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة فيلزم أن يكون تحقق كل ماهية تقررها زائدا على خصوصيتها، ولا معنى للوجود إلا هذا التحقق والتقرر فيلزم كونها موجودة حال كونها معدومة، وذلك محال. ومما يدل على أن كونها مختلفة خارج الذهن أمر زائد على ماهيتها المخصوصة: أنا إذا قلنا : السواد متقرر حال العدم فنحن ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين ذلك التصور وهذا التصديق، ولولا أن تقررها خارج الذهن حال عدمها أمر زائد على ماهيتها وإلا لما بقي فرق بين التصور والتصديق وذلك محال على ما قررناه فثبت أن الماهيات لو كانت متقررة حال عدمها، لكانت موجودة حال عدمها ولما كان محالا علمنا أن القول بأن الماهيات متقررة حال عدمها محال. البرهان الثاني : أن الذوات الثابتة في العدم إما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بثبوت الذوات المعدومة، أما أن كونها متناهية باطل فبالاتفاق، وأما أن كونها غير متناهية باطل فذلك لأن مجموع الذوات المعدومة حين ما لم يخرج منها شيء إلى الوجود كانت أكثر مما يبقى منها في العدم بعد خروج بعضها إلى الوجود وإلا لكان الشيء مع غيره كهو لا مع غيره، وذلك محال، وإذا كان كذلك كانت الذوات التي بقيت الآن في العدم أقل من مجموع الذوات التي كانت معدومة عند ما لم يخرج شيء منها إلى الوجود، وما كان أقل من غيره فهو متناه، فالذوات التي بقيت الآن في العدم متناهية، والتي خرج منها إلى الوجود متناهية ومجموع المتناهي مع المتناهي يكون متناهياً، فثبت أن القول بكون الذوات غير متناهية محال. البرهان الثالث : هذه الماهيات من حيث إنها هي ممكنة لذواتها، وكل ممكن محدث فهذه الماهيات من حيث هي هي محدثة فيلزم أن تكون هذه الماهيات مسبوقة بالنفي المحض والعدم الصرف، وهو المطلوب.
And we have proofs that the non-existent is not a thing: The first proof: If these essences were realized in the external world while stripped of existence, they would be, in their being realized externally and distinct from their specific particularities, that by which they share is other than that by which they differ. So the realization of each essence—its establishment—must be additional to its particularity. And there is no meaning of existence except this realization and establishment. So they would be existent while being non-existent, and that is impossible. And among the indications that their being different externally is additional to their specific essence is: When we say 'blackness is established in the state of non-existence' we perceive an intuitive distinction between that conceptualization and this affirmation. And were it not that its establishment externally in the state of non-existence is additional to its essence, there would be no difference between conceptualization and affirmation—and that is impossible, as we have established. So it is established that if essences were established in the state of non-existence, they would be existent in the state of non-existence. And since that is impossible, we know that the statement that essences are established in the state of non-existence is impossible. The second proof: The entities subsisting in non-existence are either finite or infinite, and both divisions are false. So the claim of the subsistence of non-existent entities is false. As for their being finite, it is false by agreement. As for their being infinite, it is false because: the totality of non-existent entities at the time when none of them had emerged into existence was greater than what remains in non-existence after some emerge into existence—otherwise the thing with others would be like it without others, which is impossible. If that is so, then the entities that now remain in non-existence are less than the totality of entities that were non-existent when none had emerged into existence. And whatever is less than something else is finite. So the entities now remaining in non-existence are finite, and those that emerged into existence are finite, and the sum of the finite with the finite is finite. So it is established that the claim that entities are infinite is impossible. The third proof: These essences, considered in themselves, are possible in themselves, and every possible is originated. So these essences, considered in themselves, are originated. Consequently, they must be preceded by pure negation and absolute non-existence, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage contains three proof's (barāhīn) that the possible non-existent (maʿdūm) is not a thing or entity. The first proof argues from the nature of realization (taḥaqquq) and establishment (taqarrur): if essences were realized externally without existence, their realization would be additional to their specific nature. But realization is exactly existence, so they would be existent and non-existent simultaneously, which is absurd. The intuitive distinction between simply conceptualizing 'blackness established in non-existence' and affirming that it is actually established externally shows that establishment adds something, namely existence. The second proof uses finitude: non-existent essences, if real, must be either finite or infinite. Finite is rejected by agreement (all consider the set of possible non-existent things potentially infinite?). Infinite is rejected by a cardinality argument: the set of all possible non-existent things before any existed is larger than the set after some exist (since some are now existent). But since the remaining set is smaller, it is finite, and the set that came into existence is also finite, so the total is finite, contradicting the assumption of infinity. Thus the hypothesis fails. The third proof is concise: all possible essences are contingent (mumkin) and thus originated (muḥdath). As originated, they are preceded by pure non-existence, so in themselves they are nothing before existence. This establishes the desired conclusion: the non-existent is not a thing.

Key Terms

محدث
عدم
نفي محض
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That possible essences have some kind of eternal reality in the mind of God or in themselves.
Correction: Razi states that they are originated and preceded by pure non-existence, so they have no reality before existence.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of Razi's third proof?
A) That possible essences are eternal.
B) That possible essences are originated and thus preceded by absolute non-existence.
C) That possible essences are infinite.
D) That possible essences are realized in non-existence.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof shows that possible essences are originated, so they are nothing before existence—hence the non-existent is not a thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a possible being. If God had not willed you into existence, you would be pure non-existence. How does that reality change the way you live today?
?
Razi says possible essences are preceded by pure negation and absolute non-existence. You were once nothing. Does your daily life reflect that you owe everything to the Necessary Being?
?
If the non-existent is not a thing, then your existence is a gift from nothing. What are you holding onto as if it were yours by right?
