Proof That Non-Existents Are Distinct and Have Quiddities
Razi argues for the distinction of non-existents through possibility and impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why non-existent things must have distinct essences (quiddities) even though they do not exist in the external world, and how Razi argues for this using the distinction between possible and impossible.

Core Idea
Non-existents are not nothing—they have determinate essences that make them distinct.

Razi addresses a fundamental question: Do things that do not exist still have some kind of reality? In this passage, he argues that even non-existent entities are distinct from each other in their very essences. He uses the fact that God cannot create the impossible but can create the possible to show that these categories are inherently different, and then refutes the idea that their distinction might only be mental. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the initial argument
Notice how Razi begins by stating that the possible and impossible are distinct in themselves. He proves this by pointing out that God can create possible things but cannot create impossible things. If they were not distinct, this difference in divine power would not hold.
This is the core intuition: difference in capability implies difference in the objects.
3
Consider the mental existence objection
An objection is raised: perhaps these non-existent things exist in the mind, so their distinction is only mental. Razi examines two possibilities: either the known objects themselves exist in the mind, or only the knowledge of them exists. He shows the first leads to absurdity and the second is irrelevant.
Focus on why the second option is admitted but does not affect the argument.
4
Follow the conclusion
After refuting the objection, Razi concludes that non-existents are distinct in themselves. He then argues that distinction requires each entity to have a specific quiddity (reality), so non-existents must have quiddities.
This final step establishes that non-existents are not mere nothing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذين القسمين متميز عن الآخر في نفسه وحقيقته، ولذلك كان القادر المختار لا يمكنه إيجاد الممتنعات ويمكنه إيجاد الممكنات فلولا امتياز الممكن عن الممتنع في نفسه وإلا لما ذلك. صح لا يقال: هذه الأمور وإن كانت معدومة في الخارج إلا أنها موجودة في الذهن؛ فلهذا صح وقوع الامتياز فيها؛ لأنا نقول: إنكم إما أن تقولوا: هذه المعلومات موجودة في الذهن أو تقولوا العلم بها موجود في الذهن، والأول باطل ; لأنا إنما نعلم الشمس والقمر، فلو كانت المعلومات موجودة في الذهن لزم فيمن تصور شموسًا كثيرة وأقمارًا كثيرة وبحرا من زئبق وجبلاً من زبرجد أن توجد في ذهنه هذه الأشياء، والقول بفساده معلوم بالضرورة، وأما الثاني، وهو أن الحاضر في الذهن هو العلم بهذه الأشياء فهذا مسلم إلا أن بحثنا عن المعلوم لا عن العلم، فهذه المعلومات لما لم تكن موجودة في الذهن علمنا أنها في أنفسها وحقائقها متميزة، سواء وجدت في الذهن أو لم توجد، وذلك هو المطلوب. فثبت بهذه البراهين الأربعة تميز بعض المعدومات عن البعض حال كونها معدومة. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : امتياز أحد الأمرين عن الآخر يتوقف على كون كل واحد منهما في نفسه حقيقة معينة وماهية معينة، فإن تميز البعض عن البعض حكم من أحكام تلك الحقيقة وصفة من أوصافها وثبوت الصفة بدوم تقرير الموصوف محال، فثبت أن المعدومات متميزة متقررة، وثبت أن المتميز لا يتحقق إلا عند كون الحقائق والماهيات متقررة، وهذا يوجب القطع بكون المعدومات ذوات وماهيات وحقائق، وذلك هو المطلوب.
These two divisions are distinct from each other in themselves and their essences, and therefore the powerful capable agent cannot bring into existence the impossible things and can bring into existence the possible things. Were it not for the distinction of the possible from the impossible in themselves, that would not be. Correct. It cannot be said: These things, even though nonexistent externally, yet they exist in the mind; thus distinction occurs in them. For we say: Either you say that these known things exist in the mind, or you say that knowledge of them exists in the mind. The first is false; for we only know the sun and the moon. If the known existed in the mind, then whoever imagines many suns and many moons and a sea of mercury and a mountain of emerald would have these things existing in his mind, and the claim of its falsity is known necessarily. As for the second, namely that what is present in the mind is knowledge of these things, this is conceded, but our inquiry is about the known, not about knowledge. Since these known things do not exist in the mind, we know that they are in themselves and their essences distinct, whether they exist in the mind or not. That is what was sought. So it is established by these four proofs the distinction of some non-existents from others while they are nonexistent. And when this is established, we say: The distinction of one thing from another depends on each of them being in itself a specific reality and a specific quiddity. For the distinction of some from others is a judgment of those realities and an attribute of their attributes, and the subsistence of an attribute without the existence of the described subject is impossible. So it is established that non-existents are distinct and determinate, and it is established that the distinct does not come about except when realities and quiddities are determinate. This necessitates the certainty that non-existents are selves, quiddities, and realities. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a proof that non-existent things are distinct from each other and have quiddities. The author begins by stating that the two categories (possible and impossible) are distinct in themselves. This is proven by the fact that the powerful agent (God) cannot create the impossible but can create the possible. If the possible and impossible were not distinct in themselves, this difference in capability would not hold. An objection is then raised: perhaps these non-existent things exist in the mind, allowing distinction. The author refutes this by considering two options: if the known objects themselves existed in the mind, then imagining multiple impossible objects would require them to exist in the mind, which is absurd. If only knowledge exists in the mind, that is conceded, but the discussion is about the known objects, not knowledge. Since the known objects do not exist in the mind, their distinction must be in their own essences. The author then concludes that non-existents are distinct even while nonexistent, and having established that, he argues that distinction requires each entity to have a specific reality or quiddity. Since distinction is an attribute of that reality, and attributes cannot subsist without a subject, the non-existents must be determinate quiddities. Thus, non-existents are things (selves, quiddities, realities).

حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existents are nothing at all; they have no reality or distinction.
Correction: The argument shows that non-existents have determinate essences (quiddities) that distinguish them, even though they do not exist in the external world or in the mind.

Checkpoint
Why cannot the mental existence objection succeed according to the author?
A) Because known objects exist in the mind.
B) Because only knowledge exists in the mind, not the known objects.
C) Because the mind cannot contain multiple objects.
D) Because the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Non-existent things have real, determinate essences that distinguish them, even when they are not present in the mind or external world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every possible act you could do has a determinate reality in God's knowledge, what does that mean for how seriously you consider your choices before acting?
?
The passage argues that even non-existents have fixed quiddities. Does your daily life reflect a belief that God knows every possibility of your character—your potential virtues and flaws—even before they occur? What would it take to live more consistently with that conviction?
?
You often assume some things in your life are impossible for you to change. Based on this lesson, is that impossibility truly objective (like God cannot create a contradiction) or is it a false belief? Name one thing you have dismissed as impossible that you should re-evaluate in light of God's power and your potential.

The Second Proof: Non-Existent is Something (Based on Quiddity)
Razi presents a proof, objection, and response regarding the non-existent being something.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's second proof that the non-existent is something, based on the quiddity of the possible, and the refutation of a misinterpretation of possibility.

Core Idea
The quiddity of a possible thing must be something even when non-existent.

This passage presents the second argument of the Mu'tazila for their thesis that the non-existent is a thing (shay'). The argument builds on the nature of possible existents and the quiddity (mahiyya) that can be conceived with or without existence. Razi then includes a key objection and a sophisticated response based on the structure of propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. The proof starts from the undisputed existence of possible things and defines possibility as admitting existence and non-existence.
Notice that the proof relies on the quiddity being capable of being established with non-existence.
2
Identify the objection
The objection attempts to reinterpret 'possible' in a way that avoids the conclusion. Try to understand the alternative interpretation offered.
3
Analyze the response
The response uses the concept of a proposition (qadiyya) with subject and predicate. It shows that the objection leads to a contradiction.
This is a logical argument that requires careful attention to the structure of propositions.
4
Understand the conclusion
The response concludes that the correct interpretation of possibility requires the quiddity to be something even when non-existent.
This is the core claim of the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية للمعتزلة على أن المعدوم شيء وهي أنه لا نزاع في وجود موجودات ممكنة الوجود لذواتها ولا معنى للمكن إلا الذي يصح عليه الوجود والعدم وكل ما كان كذلك كانت ماهيته ممكنة التقرر مع الوجود ومع العدم تارة أخرى، وإذا عقل تقرر ماهية مع العدم ثبت أن المعدوم شيء. لا يقال : المراد من قولنا : إنه يمكن أن يكون معدوما هو أنه لا يمتنع بقاء ماهية متقررة متحققة ولا يمتنع أيضًا بطلان تلك الماهية وخروجها عن كونها ماهيية وحقيقة، فهذا هو المراد من الإمكان، ومعلوم أن هذا القدر لا يدل على أن المعدوم شيء ; لأنا نقول : إذا قلنا : هذه الماهية يمكن بطلانها وزوالها، فلا شك أن هذه قضية موضوعها قولنا هذه الماهية ومحمولها : قولنا يمكن بطلانها وزوالها، ولا شك أن ماهية القضية مركبة من الموضوع والمحمول والنسبة الخاصة، ولا شك أن المركب لا يوجد إلا عند وجود جميع مفرداته فإذن هذه القضية لا توجد ولا تتقرر إلا إذا حصل موضوعها مقارنا لمحمولها، فإذا قلنا : الموضوع نفس تلك الماهية، وجعلنا المحمول إمكان بطلان الماهية كان معنى هذا الكلام : أن هذه الماهية يمكن تقررها حال بطلانها، ومعلوم أن ذلك محال، فثبت أن كون الممكن يستحيل أن يكون مفسرًا بهذا المعنى بل لا بد وأن يكون مفسرًا بأنه يجوز كون الماهية موصوفة بالوجود ويجوز كونها خالية عن الوجود ومهما كان الأمر كذلك ثبت أن المعدوم شيء.
The second argument of the Mu'tazila that the non-existent is something is that there is no dispute about the existence of existents that are possible in existence in themselves, and there is no meaning of 'possible' except that which admits existence and non-existence. And everything that is like that, its quiddity is possible to be established with existence and with non-existence at another time. And if the establishment of a quiddity with non-existence is conceived, it is established that the non-existent is something. It cannot be said: The meaning of our saying that it can be non-existent is that it is not impossible for a determinate, realized quiddity to remain, and it is also not impossible for that quiddity to be annihilated and to exit from being a quiddity and a reality. This is the meaning of possibility, and it is clear that this much does not indicate that the non-existent is something. For we say: If we say, 'This quiddity can be annihilated and removed,' then there is no doubt that this is a proposition whose subject is our statement 'this quiddity' and whose predicate is our statement 'can be annihilated and removed.' And there is no doubt that the quiddity of a proposition is composed of the subject, the predicate, and the specific relation. And there is no doubt that the compound does not exist except when all its parts exist. So this proposition does not exist and is not established unless its subject occurs together with its predicate. If we say that the subject is that very quiddity, and we make the predicate the possibility of the annihilation of the quiddity, then the meaning of this statement is that this quiddity can be established at the time of its annihilation, and it is known that that is impossible. So it is established that the possible cannot be interpreted in this sense; rather it must be interpreted as that the quiddity can be described with existence and can be free from existence. And whenever this is the case, it is established that the non-existent is something.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument of the Mu'tazila for the view that the non-existent (ma'dum) is something (shay'). The argument proceeds from the nature of possible existents. First, there is agreement that possible things exist. The possible is defined as that which can be described with existence and non-existence. Therefore, the quiddity (mahiyya) of such a thing must be capable of being established both with existence and with non-existence. If we can conceive of a quiddity being established while it is non-existent, then the non-existent is something. An objection is then raised: one might claim that 'possible existence' simply means that a quiddity that already exists in some sense can be annihilated or remain, not that it can exist before it exists. The author (representing the Mu'tazila) refutes this by analyzing the proposition 'This quiddity can be annihilated.' This is a proposition with a subject and predicate. For such a proposition to be meaningful, the subject must be present at the same time as the predicate. If the subject is the quiddity itself and the predicate is the possibility of its annihilation, then the meaning would be that the quiddity is established at the time of its annihilation, which is contradictory. Therefore, the correct interpretation of possibility is that the quiddity can be characterized by existence and can lack existence, which implies that the quiddity itself is something even when non-existent. Thus, the non-existent is something.

Key Terms
ممكن
ماهية
عدم
قضية
موضوع
محمول
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility only refers to the capacity for a quiddity to persist or be annihilated, not that the quiddity itself is something while non-existent.
Correction: The response shows that this interpretation leads to a contradiction in propositions, so the correct interpretation is that the quiddity can be characterized by existence or non-existence, making it something even when non-existent.

Checkpoint
What does the response use to refute the objection?
A) The definition of existence
B) The structure of a proposition with subject and predicate
C) The concept of quiddity
D) The distinction between mental and external existence

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's second proof argues that the quiddity of a possible thing must be something even when non-existent, because possibility implies the quiddity can be established with non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own essence (quiddity) is something even when you no longer exist, how does that change your perspective on the permanence of your identity? What does it mean for your accountability after death?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that possibility requires the quiddity to be capable of being with non-existence. If everything about you—your thoughts, actions, and existence—is merely possible, how should that affect your attachment to worldly achievements? Will you live differently knowing that your essence remains something in God's knowledge even after you pass away?
?
Consider a sin you might commit in the future: its quiddity, according to this proof, is something even before you commit it. Does that make you more mindful of your choices? How does the reality of the non-existent being something affect your sense of moral responsibility for future actions?

The Mu'tazila's Proof: Possibility as a Real Attribute
Razi presents the Mu'tazila's third proof that possibility is a real attribute requiring a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use the concepts of impossibility and possibility to argue that the non-existent is a something (shay').

Core Idea
Possibility is a real attribute that proves the quiddity exists before creation.

This lesson presents the third argument of the Mu'tazila for the claim that non-existent things have a kind of reality. Using the logic of contradictories, Razi shows that possibility must be a positive attribute inhering in a subject — the quiddity — even before the thing exists. Follow the steps carefully to see how this leads to the conclusion that the non-existent is something.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument in Arabic and English
Read the passage once to get the flow, then study the translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: first a general principle, then a proof by elimination, then application to originated things.
Pay attention to the terms امتناع and إمكان.
2
Understand the key distinction: impossibility vs. possibility
Impossibility cannot be an existing attribute because it would force the impossible to exist. Therefore, possibility must be a real attribute. Grasp this crucial step.
This uses the principle of contradictories.
3
Apply to originated things
Every originated thing is possible before existence. Since possibility is a real attribute, there must be a subject to receive it, namely the quiddity. This establishes the non-existent as a something.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: what does this imply about God's knowledge of things before creation? The quiddity is known and has a sort of reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة أن كل موجود فهو إما أن يكون ممتنع الوجود لذاته، وإما أن يكون متصور الوجود لذاته سواء كان ذلك الذي هو متصور الوجود، واجب الوجود أو غير واجب الوجود، فإذن كونه ممتنع الوجود وكونه متصور الوجود أمران متقابلان لا واسطة بينهما، فنقول : امتناع صفة موجودة إذ لو كان الامتناع صفة موجودة لكان الممتنع الموصوف بهذا الامتناع موجوداً ضرورة الوجود لا يمكن أن يكون امتناع قيام الصفة الموجودة بالنفي المحض والعدم الصرف وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون ممتنع الجود موجوداً، وهو محال فثبت أن امتناع الوجود ليس صفة موجودة، فوجب أن يكون تصور الوجود صفة موجودة ضرورة أنه لا بد في أحد النقيضين من أن يكون أمرًا ثبوتيًا. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا شك أن كل محدث فإنه قبل حدوثه ممكن الحدوث لذاته، إذ لو لم يكن ممكنا لذاته، لكان إما لذاته فيكون موجوداً قبل أن كان وهو محال أو ممتنعا لذاته، فيلزم انقلاب الماهية من الامتناع الذاتي إلى الإمكان الذاتي، وهما محالان، فثبت أن كل محدث فهو قبل حدوثه ممكن لذاته، وثبت أن الإمكان صفة ثابتة وثبت أن الصفة الثابتة تستدعي موصوفًا ثابتاً، فإذن لا بد وأن تكون الماهية متقررة قبل حدوثها، حتى تكون موصوفة بهذا الإمكان، وذلك يوجب القول بكون المعدوم شيئًا.
The third argument: Every existent is either impossible in existence in itself or conceivable in existence in itself, whether what is conceivable in existence is necessary of existence or not necessary of existence. So then being impossible of existence and being conceivable of existence are two opposites with no middle between them. We say: Impossibility is an existing attribute? If impossibility were an existing attribute, then the impossible thing described by this impossibility would exist; for it is necessary that the existence of an attribute cannot be established by pure negation and pure non-existence. Then it would follow that the impossible would exist, which is absurd. So it is established that impossibility of existence is not an existing attribute. Then it must be that conceivability of existence is an existing attribute, necessarily because one of the two contradictories must be a positive thing. When this is established, we say: There is no doubt that every originated thing, before its origination, is possible of origination in itself. For if it were not possible in itself, it would either be necessary in itself, in which case it would exist before it existed, which is absurd, or impossible in itself, which would entail the quiddity changing from intrinsic impossibility to intrinsic possibility, both of which are absurd. So it is established that every originated thing is, before its origination, possible in itself. And it is established that possibility is a stable attribute, and it is established that a stable attribute requires a stable subject. So then the quiddity must be determinate before its origination, so that it is described by this possibility. And that necessitates the view that the non-existent is something.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument of the Mu'tazila for the claim that the non-existent (ma'dum) is something (shay'). The argument uses the concepts of impossibility (imtina') and possibility (imkan) as real attributes. First, the author establishes that every existent is either impossible or possible (conceivable) in itself. These are contradictory opposites, so one must be a positive attribute. He reasons that impossibility cannot be an existing attribute because if it were, the thing described as impossible would have to exist to receive that attribute, but impossibility is supposed to prevent existence. Thus, impossibility is not a real attribute. Therefore, the opposite, conceivability or possibility, must be a real positive attribute. Next, the author applies this to originated things (hawadith). Before they come into existence, they are possible. They cannot be necessary (for then they would exist eternally) nor impossible (for then they could never come to exist). So they must be possible in themselves. Since possibility is a real attribute, it must inhere in a subject (mawsoof). That subject is the quiddity (mahiyya) of the thing, which must be determinate even before the thing exists. Thus, the quiddity is something, and therefore the non-existent (the thing before it exists) is something.

Key Terms
امتناع
إمكان
ماهية
صفة
موصوف
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility is merely a negation and not a positive attribute, but possibility is also just a negation of impossibility.
Correction: The argument shows that impossibility cannot be a positive attribute, but by the law of contradictories, possibility must be positive. Possibility is a real attribute that inheres in a subject, unlike impossibility which is purely negative.

Checkpoint
Why must possibility (imkan) be a real (positive) attribute according to the argument?
A) Because it is the opposite of impossibility, which is also real.
B) Because impossibility cannot be an existing attribute, so its opposite must be positive.
C) Because all originated things are possible.
D) Because the quiddity must exist before origination.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that possibility must be a real attribute, and since originated things are possible before existence, their quiddities must exist as 'something' even when they are non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence was merely 'possible' before God brought you into being, what does that tell you about the nature of your security, your plans, and your attachments? Do you live each day as if you are sustained by His will alone?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that even non-existence has a reality in God's knowledge. Think of something you have lost or something you deeply desire but do not yet have. Does knowing that it remains 'something' in God's knowledge change how you hold onto it or wait for it?
?
Possibility is not just a thought; it is a real attribute requiring a real subject. Your own possibilities—your future, your potential—are real in God's sight. Are you willing to surrender your imagined control and trust that your possibilities are only real because He sustains them moment by moment?

Rational Judgments

First Example: Knowledge of Impossibilities
Razi gives the first example: knowledge of impossibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that we can mentally perceive impossibilities without any corresponding reality in non-existence.

Core Idea
Mental perception does not imply external reality.

Razi responds to the claim that what we perceive mentally must have some reality. He presents the first example: our knowledge of impossibilities. We judge impossible things impossible, and we conceive them. Yet everyone agrees impossibilities have no essences. This shows mental perception can occur without any corresponding reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on impossibilities
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice how Razi describes our knowledge of impossibilities.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the three elements: our judgment that something is impossible, our conception of it, and the distinction between different impossibilities.
Razi says we judge, conceive, and distinguish.
3
Note the agreement
Observe that Razi points out there is agreement among scholars that impossibilities are not essences or realities.
This agreement is crucial for refutation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this example shows that mental perception does not require reality in non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الأولى أن نقول : إنا نجد من أنفسنا شعورًا وإدراكًا في صور كثيرة مع حصول الاتفاق بيننا وبينكم على أنها ليست ماهيات ولا حقائق، بل هي نفي محض وعدم صرف.
الصورة الأولى : العلم بالممتنعات؛ وذلك لأنا نحكم بأن شريك الله ممتنع ونقيم الدلائل على ذلك، ولو أنا تصورنا شريك الإله وإلا لاستحال منا أن تحكم عليه بالامتناع، لأن التصديق بدون التصور محال، ولأنا نقول: شريك الإله محال، والجمع بين الوجود والعدم محال، وحصول الجسم الواحد في آن واحد في مكانين محال، ونميز بين كل واحد من هذه التصديقات وبين الآخر، والشعور الذهني والامتياز العقلي حاصل في هذه الصور، مع حصول الاتفاق بين جميع العلماء على أن هذه الممتنعات ليست ذوات ولا حقائق ولا ماهيات.
الصورة الثانية : العلم بالمركبات، كما إذا تصورنا بحرًا من زئبق، وجبلاً من وزبرجد، فإن البحر والجبل عبارة عن جواهر موصوفة بالتركيب والتأليف، وموصوفة بألوان مختلفة وأشكال مخصوصة وعندكم الثابت في العدم إنما هو ذوات الجواهر والأعراض ، فإما أن يكون الجوهر موصوفًا بالعرض فذلك غير ثابت في العدم البتة والجبل إنما يكون جبلا لكون الجواهر موصوفة بالأعراض والتركيبات، فإذن المشعور الذهني حاصل في هذه الصورة مع أن الشعور به غير حاصل البتة، لا في العدم ولا في الوجود.
الصورة الثالثة : العلم بالنسب والإضافات مثل علمنا بحصول هذا الجسم في هذا الحيز دون ذلك الحيز وطلوع الشمس غدًا من المشرق لا من المغرب، ولا معنى لطلوع الشمس من المشرق إلا حصول ذاتها في ذلك الحيز دون هذا الحيز، ولا شك ولا نزاع أن هذه النسب غير ثابتة في العدم لأن كون الشمس المعدوم حاصلة في المكان المعدوم محال في بداهة العقل وأنتم ساعدتم أيضًا على أنه لا حصول ولا تقرر لأمثال هذه النسب في العدم.
الصورة الرابعة: إنما نعلم امتياز العدم عن الوجود وامتياز النفي المحض والسلب الصرف عن الثبوت ومسمى النفي والصرف والسلب المحض محكوم عليه بكونه متميزا عن الثبوت مع أنه يستحيل أن يكون لمسمى النفي المحض ثبوت؛ لأن المراد من النفي المحض ما هو مقابل لمسمى الثبوت ومنافٍ له، فلو كان له ثبوت بوجه ما لكان منافي الشيء ومناقضه نفس ذلك الشيء وهو محال، ولأن الخصم يساعد على أن مسمى العدم غير ثابت في العدم.
الصورة الخامسة : إنا نحكم على الماهية المعدومة بأنها غير موجودة، فقبل اتصاف تلك الماهية بالوجود نحن نتصور ماهية الوجود ونميز في أذهاننا بين مسمى الوجود وبين مسمى الجوهر والسواد ولذلك فإن الخصم يقول : مسمى الجوهر والسواد ثابت في العدم ومسمى الجوهر غير ثابت في العدم، فثبت أنا نتصور ماهية الوجود وحقيقته قبل صيرورة الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، فلو لزم من مجرد هذا التمييز الذهني والشعور العقلي كون الشعور به حاصلا في العدم لزم كون ماهية الوجود مقررة في العدم وذلك محال لأن العدم نقيض الوجود، والنقيضان لا يجتمعان، ولأن الخصم أيضًا يساعد على أن ماهية الوجود غير متقررة في العدم.
And the answer to the first proof is that we say: We find in ourselves a consciousness (shu'ur) and perception (idrak) in many cases, while there is agreement between us and you that they are neither essences (mahiyat) nor realities (haqa'iq), but rather pure negation (nafy mahd) and absolute non-existence ('adam sarih).
The first example: Knowledge of impossibilities (mumtani'at); for we judge that a partner of God is impossible and we adduce proofs for that. And if we did not conceive a partner of God, it would be impossible for us to judge it as impossible, because affirmation (tasdiq) without conception (tasawwur) is impossible. And we say: a partner of God is impossible, the conjunction of existence and non-existence is impossible, the existence of a single body in two places at one time is impossible. And we distinguish between each of these affirmations and the other. Mental consciousness (shu'ur dhihni) and intellectual discrimination (imtiyaz 'aqli) occur in these cases, while there is agreement among all scholars that these impossibilities are neither essences (dhawat), nor realities (haqa'iq), nor quiddities (mahiyat).
The second example: Knowledge of composites (murakkabat), as when we conceive a sea of mercury and a mountain of emerald. The sea and the mountain are expressions of substances (jawahir) described by composition (tarkib) and arrangement (ta'lif), and described by different colors and specific shapes. According to you, what is fixed in non-existence is only the essences (dhawat) of substances and accidents. But if a substance is described by an accident, that is not in the least fixed in non-existence. The mountain is only a mountain because the substances are described by accidents and compositions. So mental consciousness occurs in this case, while the consciousness of it does not occur at all, neither in non-existence nor in existence.
The third example: Knowledge of relations (nisab) and relative attributes (idafat), such as our knowledge that this body is in this place and not that place, and that the sun will rise tomorrow from the east and not from the west. There is no meaning of the sun rising from the east except the occurrence of its essence in that place rather than this place. And there is no doubt or dispute that these relations are not fixed in non-existence, because the existence of the non-existent sun in the non-existent place is impossible by the first principle of reason. And you also have conceded that there is no occurrence (husul) and no determination (taqarrur) for such relations in non-existence.
The fourth example: We know the distinction (imtiyaz) of non-existence from existence and the distinction of pure negation (nafy mahd) and absolute privation (salb sarih) from affirmation (thubut). The meaning of 'pure negation' is judged to be distinct from affirmation, while it is impossible for pure negation to have any affirmation, because what is meant by pure negation is the opposite of affirmation and contrary to it. So if it had affirmation in any respect, then the opposite of a thing and its contradictory would be that very thing itself, which is impossible. And because the opponent concedes that the meaning of non-existence is not fixed in non-existence.
The fifth example: We judge the non-existent essence (al-mahiyya al-ma'duma) to be non-existent. So before that essence is qualified by existence, we conceive the essence (mahiyya) of existence and distinguish in our minds between the meaning of existence and the meaning of substance and blackness. Therefore, the opponent says: the meaning of substance and blackness is fixed in non-existence, and the meaning of existence is not fixed in non-existence. So it is established that we conceive the essence and reality of existence before the essence becomes qualified by existence. So if from this mere mental discrimination and intellectual consciousness it necessarily followed that the consciousness of it has occurrence in non-existence, then it would necessarily follow that the essence of existence is determined (muqarrar) in non-existence, and that is impossible because non-existence is the opposite of existence, and two opposites cannot coexist. And because the opponent also concedes that the essence of existence is not determined in non-existence.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is the answer (jawaab) to the first proof that the non-existent is a thing. Razi argues that we have mental perception (shu'ur and idrak) of many things that are agreed to be neither essences (mahiyat) nor realities (haqa'iq), but rather pure negation and absolute non-existence. He provides five examples to demonstrate this.

First example: Knowledge of impossibilities (mumtani'at). We conceive and judge that a partner of God is impossible, that the conjunction of existence and non-existence is impossible, etc. These judgments involve mental concepts, yet all scholars agree that these impossibilities have no essences or realities in the external world. Thus mental awareness does not imply external reality.

Second example: Knowledge of composites (murakkabat) like a sea of mercury or a mountain of emerald. These are composed of substances and accidents in our mind, but such composites do not exist in reality (neither in non-existence nor in existence). Yet we have a clear mental picture.

Third example: Knowledge of relations (nisab) and relative attributes (idafat) such as location or future events. These relations are not fixed in non-existence, as even the opponent concedes, yet we have mental awareness of them.

Fourth example: Knowledge of the distinction between non-existence and existence, and between pure negation and affirmation. The concept of pure negation has no affirmation itself, yet we can distinguish it from affirmation. So we perceive something that has no reality.

Fifth example: Knowledge of the essence of existence (mahiyya al-wujud) before an object is qualified by existence. We can conceive existence itself, but existence is not fixed in non-existence (since non-existence is its opposite). So again, mental perception does not require external reality.

Overall, Razi refutes the opponent's claim that mental perception of the non-existent implies it has a fixed reality in non-existence. These five examples show that we can perceive many things that have no reality in the external world.

Key Terms
mumtani'at (impossibilities)
tasawwur (conception)
tasdiq (affirmation)
shu'ur (consciousness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we mentally perceive something, it must have some reality in non-existence.
Correction: Razi's first example shows we perceive impossibilities which no scholar considers real, thus mental perception does not imply reality.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first example, what is required before we can judge something as impossible?
A) That the thing exists in reality.
B) That we first conceive it.
C) That the thing is a composite.
D) That the thing is a relation.

Key Takeaway
Our ability to conceive and judge impossibilities proves that mental perception does not require any external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that Allah has no partner, do you ever feel a gap between your intellectual certainty and your emotional conviction? How does Razi's demonstration that conception alone suffices for judgment help you bridge that gap in your daily worship?
?
Think of a doubt you have struggled with—perhaps about the afterlife or Allah's attributes. Did you ever feel that because you cannot fully conceive something, it might not be true? How does this lesson challenge that feeling, and what concrete step can you take today to strengthen your certainty?
?
Razi shows that impossibilities (like a second god) are mentally perceived but have no reality. How does this affect the way you respond to whispers (waswasa) about Allah's oneness or other fundamentals? Will you allow this lesson to silence those whispers by reminding yourself that the impossibility has no reality at all?

Second Example: Knowledge of Composites
Razi gives the second example: knowledge of composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses knowledge of composites (like a sea of mercury) to show mental perception without external reality.

Core Idea
We can imagine composites that have no existence anywhere.

Razi's second example involves composite objects like a sea of mercury or a mountain of emerald. We can conceive these vividly, yet they have no reality in either existence or non-existence according to the opponent's own principles. This again demonstrates that mental perception does not entail a fixed reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on composites
Read the Arabic and translation for the second example. Notice the description of the conceived objects.
2
Identify the opponent's assumption
Razi notes that according to the opponent, only essences of substances and accidents are fixed in non-existence.
Composites involve accidents (colors, shapes) that are not fixed in non-existence.
3
Analyze the implication
Since the composite relies on accidents, and accidents are not fixed in non-existence, the composite has no reality at all. Yet we perceive it mentally.
4
Conclude
Thus mental perception occurs without any corresponding reality in non-existence or existence.

والجواب عن الحجة الأولى أن نقول : إنا نجد من أنفسنا شعورًا وإدراكًا في صور كثيرة مع حصول الاتفاق بيننا وبينكم على أنها ليست ماهيات ولا حقائق، بل هي نفي محض وعدم صرف.
الصورة الأولى : العلم بالممتنعات؛ وذلك لأنا نحكم بأن شريك الله ممتنع ونقيم الدلائل على ذلك، ولو أنا تصورنا شريك الإله وإلا لاستحال منا أن تحكم عليه بالامتناع، لأن التصديق بدون التصور محال، ولأنا نقول: شريك الإله محال، والجمع بين الوجود والعدم محال، وحصول الجسم الواحد في آن واحد في مكانين محال، ونميز بين كل واحد من هذه التصديقات وبين الآخر، والشعور الذهني والامتياز العقلي حاصل في هذه الصور، مع حصول الاتفاق بين جميع العلماء على أن هذه الممتنعات ليست ذوات ولا حقائق ولا ماهيات.
الصورة الثانية : العلم بالمركبات، كما إذا تصورنا بحرًا من زئبق، وجبلاً من وزبرجد، فإن البحر والجبل عبارة عن جواهر موصوفة بالتركيب والتأليف، وموصوفة بألوان مختلفة وأشكال مخصوصة وعندكم الثابت في العدم إنما هو ذوات الجواهر والأعراض ، فإما أن يكون الجوهر موصوفًا بالعرض فذلك غير ثابت في العدم البتة والجبل إنما يكون جبلا لكون الجواهر موصوفة بالأعراض والتركيبات، فإذن المشعور الذهني حاصل في هذه الصورة مع أن الشعور به غير حاصل البتة، لا في العدم ولا في الوجود.
الصورة الثالثة : العلم بالنسب والإضافات مثل علمنا بحصول هذا الجسم في هذا الحيز دون ذلك الحيز وطلوع الشمس غدًا من المشرق لا من المغرب، ولا معنى لطلوع الشمس من المشرق إلا حصول ذاتها في ذلك الحيز دون هذا الحيز، ولا شك ولا نزاع أن هذه النسب غير ثابتة في العدم لأن كون الشمس المعدوم حاصلة في المكان المعدوم محال في بداهة العقل وأنتم ساعدتم أيضًا على أنه لا حصول ولا تقرر لأمثال هذه النسب في العدم.
الصورة الرابعة: إنما نعلم امتياز العدم عن الوجود وامتياز النفي المحض والسلب الصرف عن الثبوت ومسمى النفي والصرف والسلب المحض محكوم عليه بكونه متميزا عن الثبوت مع أنه يستحيل أن يكون لمسمى النفي المحض ثبوت؛ لأن المراد من النفي المحض ما هو مقابل لمسمى الثبوت ومنافٍ له، فلو كان له ثبوت بوجه ما لكان منافي الشيء ومناقضه نفس ذلك الشيء وهو محال، ولأن الخصم يساعد على أن مسمى العدم غير ثابت في العدم.
الصورة الخامسة : إنا نحكم على الماهية المعدومة بأنها غير موجودة، فقبل اتصاف تلك الماهية بالوجود نحن نتصور ماهية الوجود ونميز في أذهاننا بين مسمى الوجود وبين مسمى الجوهر والسواد ولذلك فإن الخصم يقول : مسمى الجوهر والسواد ثابت في العدم ومسمى الجوهر غير ثابت في العدم، فثبت أنا نتصور ماهية الوجود وحقيقته قبل صيرورة الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، فلو لزم من مجرد هذا التمييز الذهني والشعور العقلي كون الشعور به حاصلا في العدم لزم كون ماهية الوجود مقررة في العدم وذلك محال لأن العدم نقيض الوجود، والنقيضان لا يجتمعان، ولأن الخصم أيضًا يساعد على أن ماهية الوجود غير متقررة في العدم.
And the answer to the first proof is that we say: We find in ourselves a consciousness (shu'ur) and perception (idrak) in many cases, while there is agreement between us and you that they are neither essences (mahiyat) nor realities (haqa'iq), but rather pure negation (nafy mahd) and absolute non-existence ('adam sarih).
The first example: Knowledge of impossibilities (mumtani'at); for we judge that a partner of God is impossible and we adduce proofs for that. And if we did not conceive a partner of God, it would be impossible for us to judge it as impossible, because affirmation (tasdiq) without conception (tasawwur) is impossible. And we say: a partner of God is impossible, the conjunction of existence and non-existence is impossible, the existence of a single body in two places at one time is impossible. And we distinguish between each of these affirmations and the other. Mental consciousness (shu'ur dhihni) and intellectual discrimination (imtiyaz 'aqli) occur in these cases, while there is agreement among all scholars that these impossibilities are neither essences (dhawat), nor realities (haqa'iq), nor quiddities (mahiyat).
The second example: Knowledge of composites (murakkabat), as when we conceive a sea of mercury and a mountain of emerald. The sea and the mountain are expressions of substances (jawahir) described by composition (tarkib) and arrangement (ta'lif), and described by different colors and specific shapes. According to you, what is fixed in non-existence is only the essences (dhawat) of substances and accidents. But if a substance is described by an accident, that is not in the least fixed in non-existence. The mountain is only a mountain because the substances are described by accidents and compositions. So mental consciousness occurs in this case, while the consciousness of it does not occur at all, neither in non-existence nor in existence.
The third example: Knowledge of relations (nisab) and relative attributes (idafat), such as our knowledge that this body is in this place and not that place, and that the sun will rise tomorrow from the east and not from the west. There is no meaning of the sun rising from the east except the occurrence of its essence in that place rather than this place. And there is no doubt or dispute that these relations are not fixed in non-existence, because the existence of the non-existent sun in the non-existent place is impossible by the first principle of reason. And you also have conceded that there is no occurrence (husul) and no determination (taqarrur) for such relations in non-existence.
The fourth example: We know the distinction (imtiyaz) of non-existence from existence and the distinction of pure negation (nafy mahd) and absolute privation (salb sarih) from affirmation (thubut). The meaning of 'pure negation' is judged to be distinct from affirmation, while it is impossible for pure negation to have any affirmation, because what is meant by pure negation is the opposite of affirmation and contrary to it. So if it had affirmation in any respect, then the opposite of a thing and its contradictory would be that very thing itself, which is impossible. And because the opponent concedes that the meaning of non-existence is not fixed in non-existence.
The fifth example: We judge the non-existent essence (al-mahiyya al-ma'duma) to be non-existent. So before that essence is qualified by existence, we conceive the essence (mahiyya) of existence and distinguish in our minds between the meaning of existence and the meaning of substance and blackness. Therefore, the opponent says: the meaning of substance and blackness is fixed in non-existence, and the meaning of existence is not fixed in non-existence. So it is established that we conceive the essence and reality of existence before the essence becomes qualified by existence. So if from this mere mental discrimination and intellectual consciousness it necessarily followed that the consciousness of it has occurrence in non-existence, then it would necessarily follow that the essence of existence is determined (muqarrar) in non-existence, and that is impossible because non-existence is the opposite of existence, and two opposites cannot coexist. And because the opponent also concedes that the essence of existence is not determined in non-existence.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is the answer (jawaab) to the first proof that the non-existent is a thing. Razi argues that we have mental perception (shu'ur and idrak) of many things that are agreed to be neither essences (mahiyat) nor realities (haqa'iq), but rather pure negation and absolute non-existence. He provides five examples to demonstrate this.

First example: Knowledge of impossibilities (mumtani'at). We conceive and judge that a partner of God is impossible, that the conjunction of existence and non-existence is impossible, etc. These judgments involve mental concepts, yet all scholars agree that these impossibilities have no essences or realities in the external world. Thus mental awareness does not imply external reality.

Second example: Knowledge of composites (murakkabat) like a sea of mercury or a mountain of emerald. These are composed of substances and accidents in our mind, but such composites do not exist in reality (neither in non-existence nor in existence). Yet we have a clear mental picture.

Third example: Knowledge of relations (nisab) and relative attributes (idafat) such as location or future events. These relations are not fixed in non-existence, as even the opponent concedes, yet we have mental awareness of them.

Fourth example: Knowledge of the distinction between non-existence and existence, and between pure negation and affirmation. The concept of pure negation has no affirmation itself, yet we can distinguish it from affirmation. So we perceive something that has no reality.

Fifth example: Knowledge of the essence of existence (mahiyya al-wujud) before an object is qualified by existence. We can conceive existence itself, but existence is not fixed in non-existence (since non-existence is its opposite). So again, mental perception does not require external reality.

Overall, Razi refutes the opponent's claim that mental perception of the non-existent implies it has a fixed reality in non-existence. These five examples show that we can perceive many things that have no reality in the external world.

Key Terms
murakkabat (composites)
jawahir (substances)
a'rad (accidents)
tarkib (composition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we can imagine a composite object, its components must have some reality in non-existence.
Correction: Razi shows that composites involve accidents that are not fixed in non-existence, so the composite itself has no reality, yet we perceive it.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that a composite like a sea of mercury has no reality in non-existence?
A) Because mercury does not exist in non-existence.
B) Because composites involve substances described by accidents, and such descriptions are not fixed in non-existence according to the opponent.
C) Because a sea of mercury is contradicting.
D) Because composites are not perceptible.

Key Takeaway
Mental perception of composites shows that we can conceive things that have no reality in either existence or non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often imagine perfect futures or past regrets that are composites of many accidents. Are these mental pictures keeping you from engaging with what is truly fixed and real? Will you today identify one such mental composite and ask yourself if it has any basis in reality?
?
When you hold a grudge, you are mentally reconstructing a past event that no longer exists as such. Razi shows that mental perception can occur without any external reality. Does your heart cling to a 'mountain of emerald' that only exists in your mind? What would it take to release it?
?
You probably spend time daydreaming about things that are not fixed in non-existence. How much of your mental energy is wasted on composites that will never be? Will you commit to redirecting your thoughts toward what is actually present and real?

Third Example: Knowledge of Relations
Razi gives the third example: knowledge of relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that we perceive relations (like location) that have no reality in non-existence.

Core Idea
Relations exist only in the mind, not in external reality.

Razi's third example involves relations and relative attributes, such as the location of a body or the future rising of the sun. These relations are not fixed in non-existence—even the opponent concedes this—yet we have clear mental perception of them. This further demonstrates that mental perception does not require a corresponding reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relations
Read the third example. Notice the examples: location and sunrise.
2
Understand the nature of relations
Relations like 'this body is in this place' involve a connection between two things. Razi says they are not fixed in non-existence.
Even the opponent agrees.
3
Note the concession
Razi states that the opponent concedes that relations have no occurrence or determination in non-existence.
4
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, mental perception of relations occurs without them having any reality in non-existence.

والجواب عن الحجة الأولى أن نقول : إنا نجد من أنفسنا شعورًا وإدراكًا في صور كثيرة مع حصول الاتفاق بيننا وبينكم على أنها ليست ماهيات ولا حقائق، بل هي نفي محض وعدم صرف.
الصورة الأولى : العلم بالممتنعات؛ وذلك لأنا نحكم بأن شريك الله ممتنع ونقيم الدلائل على ذلك، ولو أنا تصورنا شريك الإله وإلا لاستحال منا أن تحكم عليه بالامتناع، لأن التصديق بدون التصور محال، ولأنا نقول: شريك الإله محال، والجمع بين الوجود والعدم محال، وحصول الجسم الواحد في آن واحد في مكانين محال، ونميز بين كل واحد من هذه التصديقات وبين الآخر، والشعور الذهني والامتياز العقلي حاصل في هذه الصور، مع حصول الاتفاق بين جميع العلماء على أن هذه الممتنعات ليست ذوات ولا حقائق ولا ماهيات.
الصورة الثانية : العلم بالمركبات، كما إذا تصورنا بحرًا من زئبق، وجبلاً من وزبرجد، فإن البحر والجبل عبارة عن جواهر موصوفة بالتركيب والتأليف، وموصوفة بألوان مختلفة وأشكال مخصوصة وعندكم الثابت في العدم إنما هو ذوات الجواهر والأعراض ، فإما أن يكون الجوهر موصوفًا بالعرض فذلك غير ثابت في العدم البتة والجبل إنما يكون جبلا لكون الجواهر موصوفة بالأعراض والتركيبات، فإذن المشعور الذهني حاصل في هذه الصورة مع أن الشعور به غير حاصل البتة، لا في العدم ولا في الوجود.
الصورة الثالثة : العلم بالنسب والإضافات مثل علمنا بحصول هذا الجسم في هذا الحيز دون ذلك الحيز وطلوع الشمس غدًا من المشرق لا من المغرب، ولا معنى لطلوع الشمس من المشرق إلا حصول ذاتها في ذلك الحيز دون هذا الحيز، ولا شك ولا نزاع أن هذه النسب غير ثابتة في العدم لأن كون الشمس المعدوم حاصلة في المكان المعدوم محال في بداهة العقل وأنتم ساعدتم أيضًا على أنه لا حصول ولا تقرر لأمثال هذه النسب في العدم.
الصورة الرابعة: إنما نعلم امتياز العدم عن الوجود وامتياز النفي المحض والسلب الصرف عن الثبوت ومسمى النفي والصرف والسلب المحض محكوم عليه بكونه متميزا عن الثبوت مع أنه يستحيل أن يكون لمسمى النفي المحض ثبوت؛ لأن المراد من النفي المحض ما هو مقابل لمسمى الثبوت ومنافٍ له، فلو كان له ثبوت بوجه ما لكان منافي الشيء ومناقضه نفس ذلك الشيء وهو محال، ولأن الخصم يساعد على أن مسمى العدم غير ثابت في العدم.
الصورة الخامسة : إنا نحكم على الماهية المعدومة بأنها غير موجودة، فقبل اتصاف تلك الماهية بالوجود نحن نتصور ماهية الوجود ونميز في أذهاننا بين مسمى الوجود وبين مسمى الجوهر والسواد ولذلك فإن الخصم يقول : مسمى الجوهر والسواد ثابت في العدم ومسمى الجوهر غير ثابت في العدم، فثبت أنا نتصور ماهية الوجود وحقيقته قبل صيرورة الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، فلو لزم من مجرد هذا التمييز الذهني والشعور العقلي كون الشعور به حاصلا في العدم لزم كون ماهية الوجود مقررة في العدم وذلك محال لأن العدم نقيض الوجود، والنقيضان لا يجتمعان، ولأن الخصم أيضًا يساعد على أن ماهية الوجود غير متقررة في العدم.

Commentary
This passage is the answer (jawaab) to the first proof that the non-existent is a thing. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a worry you have about something that might happen in the future. That worry is based on a relation (e.g., 'if X happens, then Y'). According to Razi, this relation has no fixed reality in non-existence. How does this affect the weight you give to that worry? Will you continue to treat it as a real external threat, or can you recognize it as a mental construct and entrust the future to Allah?
?
Think of a past mistake that still haunts you. The relation 'I did that' no longer exists externally—it is only a mental construct. Yet you may feel guilt as if it were still real. How can the understanding that relations are mental help you sincerely repent and move forward, trusting Allah's forgiveness?
?
You have knowledge of many things you have never seen (e.g., Paradise, Hell, the Last Day). Razi's argument shows that mental perception does not require the object to exist externally right now. How does this strengthen your faith in the unseen? Does it challenge you to act on that knowledge as if it were certain, even though its full reality is not yet manifest?

Fourth Example: Distinguishing Non-Existence from Existence
Razi gives the fourth example: knowledge of distinction between non-existence and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that we can distinguish between non-existence and existence, even though pure negation has no reality.

Core Idea
We can conceive and compare things that are pure nothingness.

Razi's fourth example concerns our ability to distinguish between non-existence and existence, and between pure negation and affirmation. Pure negation is defined as the opposite of affirmation; it cannot have any affirmation itself. Yet we clearly understand it and distinguish it from affirmation. This shows that our mental perception extends to concepts that have no reality whatsoever.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on distinction
Read the fourth example. Notice that we distinguish non-existence from existence.
2
Understand the nature of pure negation
Pure negation is the opposite of affirmation; it has no thubut (affirmation).
3
Think about the contradiction
If pure negation had any affirmation, it would be its own opposite, which is impossible.
4
Conclude
Thus we perceive something that has no reality, proving mental perception does not require external reality.

And the answer to the first proof is that we say: We find in ourselves a consciousness (shu'ur) and perception (idrak) in many cases, while there is agreement between us and you that they are neither essences (mahiyat) nor realities (haqa'iq), but rather pure negation (nafy mahd) and absolute non-existence ('adam sarih).
The first example: Knowledge of impossibilities (mumtani'at); for we judge that a partner of God is impossible and we adduce proofs for that. And if we did not conceive a partner of God, it would be impossible for us to judge it as impossible, because affirmation (tasdiq) without conception (tasawwur) is impossible. And we say: a partner of God is impossible, the conjunction of existence and non-existence is impossible, the existence of a single body in two places at one time is impossible. And we distinguish between each of these affirmations and the other. Mental consciousness (shu'ur dhihni) and intellectual discrimination (imtiyaz 'aqli) occur in these cases, while there is agreement among all scholars that these impossibilities are neither essences (dhawat), nor realities (haqa'iq), nor quiddities (mahiyat).
The second example: Knowledge of composites (murakkabat), as when we conceive a sea of mercury and a mountain of emerald. The sea and the mountain are expressions of substances (jawahir) described by composition (tarkib) and arrangement (ta'lif), and described by different colors and specific shapes. According to you, what is fixed in non-existence is only the essences (dhawat) of substances and accidents. But if a substance is described by an accident, that is not in the least fixed in non-existence. The mountain is only a mountain because the substances are described by accidents and compositions. So mental consciousness occurs in this case, while the consciousness of it does not occur at all, neither in non-existence nor in existence.
The third example: Knowledge of relations (nisab) and relative attributes (idafat), such as our knowledge that this body is in this place and not that place, and that the sun will rise tomorrow from the east and not from the west. There is no meaning of the sun rising from the east except the occurrence of its essence in that place rather than this place. And there is no doubt or dispute that these relations are not fixed in non-existence, because the existence of the non-existent sun in the non-existent place is impossible by the first principle of reason. And you also have conceded that there is no occurrence (husul) and no determination (taqarrur) for such relations in non-existence.
The fourth example: We know the distinction (imtiyaz) of non-existence from existence and the distinction of pure negation (nafy mahd) and absolute privation (salb sarih) from affirmation (thubut). The meaning of 'pure negation' is judged to be distinct from affirmation, while it is impossible for pure negation to have any affirmation, because what is meant by pure negation is the opposite of affirmation and contrary to it. So if it had affirmation in any respect, then the opposite of a thing and its contradictory would be that very thing itself, which is impossible. And because the opponent concedes that the meaning of non-existence is not fixed in non-existence.
The fifth example: We judge the non-existent essence (al-mahiyya al-ma'duma) to be non-existent. So before that essence is qualified by existence, we conceive the essence (mahiyya) of existence and distinguish in our minds between the meaning of existence and the meaning of substance and blackness. Therefore, the opponent says: the meaning of substance and blackness is fixed in non-existence, and the meaning of existence is not fixed in non-existence. So it is established that we conceive the essence and reality of existence before the essence becomes qualified by existence. So if from this mere mental discrimination and intellectual consciousness it necessarily followed that the consciousness of it has occurrence in non-existence, then it would necessarily follow that the essence of existence is determined (muqarrar) in non-existence, and that is impossible because non-existence is the opposite of existence, and two opposites cannot coexist. And because the opponent also concedes that the essence of existence is not determined in non-existence.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is the answer (jawaab) to the first proof that the non-existent is a thing. Razi argues that we have mental perception (shu'ur and idrak) of many things that are agreed to be neither essences (mahiyat) nor realities (haqa'iq), but rather pure negation and absolute non-existence. He provides five examples to demonstrate this.

First example: Knowledge of impossibilities (mumtani'at). We conceive and judge that a partner of God is impossible, that the conjunction of existence and non-existence is impossible, etc. These judgments involve mental concepts, yet all scholars agree that these impossibilities have no essences or realities in the external world. Thus mental awareness does not imply external reality.

Second example: Knowledge of composites (murakkabat) like a sea of mercury or a mountain of emerald. These are composed of substances and accidents in our mind, but such composites do not exist in reality (neither in non-existence nor in existence). Yet we have a clear mental picture.

Third example: Knowledge of relations (nisab) and relative attributes (idafat) such as location or future events. These relations are not fixed in non-existence, as even the opponent concedes, yet we have mental awareness of them.

Fourth example: Knowledge of the distinction between non-existence and existence, and between pure negation and affirmation. The concept of pure negation has no affirmation itself, yet we can distinguish it from affirmation. So we perceive something that has no reality.

Fifth example: Knowledge of the essence of existence (mahiyya al-wujud) before an object is qualified by existence. We can conceive existence itself, but existence is not fixed in non-existence (since non-existence is its opposite). So again, mental perception does not require external reality.

Overall, Razi refutes the opponent's claim that mental perception of the non-existent implies it has a fixed reality in non-existence. These five examples show that we can perceive many things that have no reality in the external world.

Key Terms
nafy mahd (pure negation)
salb sarih (absolute privation)
thubut (affirmation)
imtiyaz (distinction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is something real that can be known and distinguished.
Correction: Razi argues that pure negation has no reality at all, yet we can distinguish it from existence.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say pure negation cannot have affirmation?
A) Because it is a mental construct.
B) Because if it did, it would be its own opposite, which is impossible.
C) Because the opponent denies it.
D) Because affirmation is always positive.

Key Takeaway
We can conceive and distinguish pure negation, which has no reality, proving mental perception does not require external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a concept you hold with absolute certainty—maybe a belief or an idea. Is it possible that this concept has no external reality whatsoever? How would you know? Razi's argument forces you to ask: Is my certainty based on something real, or just on my own mental construction?
?
Have you ever been absolutely convinced of something that later turned out to be completely false? What did that experience teach you about the reliability of your own mind? This lesson suggests the mind can perceive even pure non-existence—so how do you test the truth of your inner certainties?
?
If the mind can form clear distinctions about things that do not exist at all, what does that mean for your deepest convictions? Are you willing to examine one such conviction today and ask: 'Could this be a mere mental distinction with no reality?' If not, why not?

Fifth Example: Conception of Existence Before Its Instantiation
Razi gives the fifth example: knowledge of the essence of existence before instantiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that we conceive the essence of existence itself before any object exists, showing mental perception without external reality.

Core Idea
We can conceive existence itself, even before any existent.

Razi's fifth example deals with our conception of the essence of existence (mahiyya al-wujud) prior to any object being qualified by existence. We judge a non-existent essence as non-existent, and in doing so, we must already conceive existence to distinguish it from the essence. Yet existence cannot be fixed in non-existence because non-existence is its opposite. This again demonstrates that mental perception occurs independently of external reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on existence
Read the fifth example. Notice the description of judging a non-existent essence as non-existent.
2
Understand the need to conceive existence first
To say something is non-existent, we must have a concept of existence to contrast it with.
3
Note the opponent's position
The opponent says that the meaning of existence is not fixed in non-existence.
4
Draw the conclusion
Thus we conceive existence before it exists, yet this mental conception does not require existence to be fixed in non-existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الأولى أن نقول : إنا نجد من أنفسنا شعورًا وإدراكًا في صور كثيرة مع حصول الاتفاق بيننا وبينكم على أنها ليست ماهيات ولا حقائق، بل هي نفي محض وعدم صرف.
الصورة الأولى : العلم بالممتنعات؛ وذلك لأنا نحكم بأن شريك الله ممتنع ونقيم الدلائل على ذلك، ولو أنا تصورنا شريك الإله وإلا لاستحال منا أن تحكم عليه بالامتناع، لأن التصديق بدون التصور محال، ولأنا نقول: شريك الإله محال، والجمع بين الوجود والعدم محال، وحصول الجسم الواحد في آن واحد في مكانين محال، ونميز بين كل واحد من هذه التصديقات وبين الآخر، والشعور الذهني والامتياز العقلي حاصل في هذه الصور، مع حصول الاتفاق بين جميع العلماء على أن هذه الممتنعات ليست ذوات ولا حقائق ولا ماهيات.
الصورة الثانية : العلم بالمركبات، كما إذا تصورنا بحرًا من زئبق، وجبلاً من وزبرجد، فإن البحر والجبل عبارة عن جواهر موصوفة بالتركيب والتأليف، وموصوفة بألوان مختلفة وأشكال مخصوصة وعندكم الثابت في العدم إنما هو ذوات الجواهر والأعراض ، فإما أن يكون الجوهر موصوفًا بالعرض فذلك غير ثابت في العدم البتة والجبل إنما يكون جبلا لكون الجواهر موصوفة بالأعراض والتركيبات، فإذن المشعور الذهني حاصل في هذه الصورة مع أن الشعور به غير حاصل البتة، لا في العدم ولا في الوجود.
الصورة الثالثة : العلم بالنسب والإضافات مثل علمنا بحصول هذا الجسم في هذا الحيز دون ذلك الحيز وطلوع الشمس غدًا من المشرق لا من المغرب، ولا معنى لطلوع الشمس من المشرق إلا حصول ذاتها في ذلك الحيز دون هذا الحيز، ولا شك ولا نزاع أن هذه النسب غير ثابتة في العدم لأن كون الشمس المعدوم حاصلة في المكان المعدوم محال في بداهة العقل وأنتم ساعدتم أيضًا على أنه لا حصول ولا تقرر لأمثال هذه النسب في العدم.
الصورة الرابعة: إنما نعلم امتياز العدم عن الوجود وامتياز النفي المحض والسلب الصرف عن الثبوت ومسمى النفي والصرف والسلب المحض محكوم عليه بكونه متميزا عن الثبوت مع أنه يستحيل أن يكون لمسمى النفي المحض ثبوت؛ لأن المراد من النفي المحض ما هو مقابل لمسمى الثبوت ومنافٍ له، فلو كان له ثبوت بوجه ما لكان منافي الشيء ومناقضه نفس ذلك الشيء وهو محال، ولأن الخصم يساعد على أن مسمى العدم غير ثابت في العدم.
الصورة الخامسة : إنا نحكم على الماهية المعدومة بأنها غير موجودة، فقبل اتصاف تلك الماهية بالوجود نحن نتصور ماهية الوجود ونميز في أذهاننا بين مسمى الوجود وبين مسمى الجوهر والسواد ولذلك فإن الخصم يقول : مسمى الجوهر والسواد ثابت في العدم ومسمى الجوهر غير ثابت في العدم، فثبت أنا نتصور ماهية الوجود وحقيقته قبل صيرورة الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، فلو لزم من مجرد هذا التمييز الذهني والشعور العقلي كون الشعور به حاصلا في العدم لزم كون ماهية الوجود مقررة في العدم وذلك محال لأن العدم نقيض الوجود، والنقيضان لا يجتمعان، ولأن الخصم أيضًا يساعد على أن ماهية الوجود غير متقررة في العدم.
And the answer to the first proof is that we say: We find in ourselves a consciousness (shu'ur) and perception (idrak) in many cases, while there is agreement between us and you that they are neither essences (mahiyat) nor realities (haqa'iq), but rather pure negation (nafy mahd) and absolute non-existence ('adam sarih).

And we say: a partner of God is impossible, the conjunction of existence and non-existence is impossible, the existence of a single body in two places at one time is impossible. Commentary
This passage is the answer (jawaab) to the first proof that the non-existent is a thing. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You often think about God's existence. But before you say 'God exists,' you must first have a concept of existence. Does this concept come from your experience of created things, or can you conceive of existence in a way that is not dependent on them? If you cannot conceive of existence independently, then how can you truly affirm the existence of a Creator who is unlike anything you have experienced? This lesson forces you to examine the very tool you use to know God—your mind. Is your conception of existence adequate to reach the reality of the Necessary Existent?
?
You have likely argued with someone about the existence of God using logical proofs. But this lesson reveals that even the concept of existence itself is mentally constructed before any existential judgment. Does that make you uneasy? If your mind can conceive of existence without any external reference, then what else might your mind be constructing? Are you willing to examine every foundational belief you hold and see if it rests on a genuine reality or merely a mental discrimination? This lesson calls you to a radical intellectual honesty—are you ready to face it?

Refuting the Second Argument: Possibility is Not an Attribute of Quiddity
Razi refutes the second argument for the reality of non-existent quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi rejects the claim that possibility being a characteristic of the quiddity proves the quiddity's fixed existence without actual existence.

Core Idea
Possibility describes existence, not the eternal quiddity.

This lesson tackles a key argument for the reality of non-existent quiddities: that since a possible thing can be conceived with or without existence, its quiddity must be able to exist without existence. Razi counters by clarifying that possibility is not an attribute of the quiddity itself, but rather of existence. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the relationship between quiddity and existence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the logical connectors: 'if... then...' and 'rather'.
2
Identify the opponents' argument
Locate the core claim: that the possible is that whose quiddity can be established with or without existence, implying the quiddity can be fixed without existence.
The opponents are defending the view that non-existent things have some reality.
3
Understand Razi's refutation
See why Razi says possibility cannot be an attribute of the quiddity: the quiddity is necessarily fixed and unchanging in both states, so possibility applies only to existence.
This is a key distinction between quiddity (essence) and existence.
4
Grasp the dilemma
Notice Razi's final point: existence is not established in non-existence, so what can have possibility (existence) cannot be in non-existence, and what can be in non-existence cannot have possibility.
This creates a logical stalemate for the opponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية التي عولوا عليها في أن المعدوم شيء، وهو قولهم إن الممكن هو الذي يمكن تقرر ماهيته تارة مع العدم وأخرى مع الوجود وذلك يقتضي جواز تقرر الماهية بدون الوجود. فجوابها : أن الماهية إذا كانت واجبة التقرر حالتي العدم والوجود ممتنعة التغير في نفسها امتنع جعل الإمكان صفة لها بل الإمكان يكون صفة للوجود، ثم إنا توافقنا على أن الوجود غير ثابت في العدم فعاد ما ذكرنا من أن الذي يمكن جعل الإمكان وصفًا له لا يعقل إثباته في العدم وما يمكن إثباته في العدم لا يمكن جعل الإمكان وصفا له.
As for the second argument they relied upon that the non-existent is a thing—namely their statement that the possible is that whose quiddity can be established sometimes with non-existence and sometimes with existence, and that necessitates the permissibility of the quiddity being established without existence. The answer: if the quiddity is necessarily established in both states of non-existence and existence, and impossible to change in itself, then it is impossible to make possibility an attribute of it; rather, possibility is an attribute of existence. Then we agree that existence is not established in non-existence, so what we mentioned returns: that which can be given possibility as an attribute cannot be rationally established in non-existence, and what can be established in non-existence cannot be given possibility as an attribute.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage refutes the argument that because a possible thing can be conceived with existence or non-existence, the quiddity must be able to exist without existence (i.e., fixed in non-existence). Razi responds that the quiddity is considered fixed in both states and unchanging; therefore, possibility (imkān) cannot be an attribute of the quiddity itself. Instead, possibility is an attribute of existence (i.e., a judgment about a thing's existence). Since existence is not present in non-existence, the possible in the sense of an attribute cannot be applied to anything in non-existence. Thus, the argument fails to separate the quiddity from its modal status.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkān)
ماهية (māhiyya)
وجود (wujūd)
ممكن (mumkin)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That possibility (imkān) is an attribute of the quiddity itself, proving the quiddity's independent reality apart from existence.
Correction: Razi argues that possibility is not an attribute of the quiddity because the quiddity is necessarily fixed and unchanging; possibility only applies to existence.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is possibility an attribute of?
A) The quiddity itself.
B) Existence (wujūd).
C) Non-existence.
D) The agent's power.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a modal attribute of existence, not of the underlying quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'this is possible' about your plans and dreams. After this lesson, do you see that possibility actually refers to Allah granting existence, not something inherent in the plan itself? How does that shift your reliance on your own assessments?
?
Have you ever assumed that your intentions or ideas have some 'reality' before they materialize, as if they exist in a waiting state? Razi argues that quiddity is not a thing in non-existence. Does this expose a hidden assumption you hold? What would it mean to truly believe that nothing of your actions exists except when Allah creates it?
?
If possibility is an attribute of existence alone, then every moment of your existence is only possible by Allah's will. Does your daily life reflect this dependence, or do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency? What one habit could you change to live in constant awareness that your existence is a gift that is renewed?

Understanding Possibility (Imkan) and Temporal Origination (Huduth)
Razi defines possibility and temporal origination and states their difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of possibility (imkan) and temporal origination (huduth) and the crucial distinction between them as modal vs. temporal properties.

Core Idea
Possibility is a modal state of the essence; origination is a temporal state of existence.

Before engaging with arguments about the createdness of the world, we must clarify two foundational terms: possibility and temporal origination. Imam Razi begins his first premise by defining each precisely and highlighting their difference. This sets the conceptual groundwork for all that follows in kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice the precise wording for each definition.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا يمتنع وجوده ولا عدمه'.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the definition of possibility (imkan) and the definition of temporal origination (huduth). Write them down in your own words.
Possibility is about the thing itself; origination is about existence.
3
Understand the distinction
Razi says the difference is apparent. Reflect on why: one is a modal property, the other a temporal property. Why does this matter?
This distinction prevents conflation in later arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى : في الفرق بين الإمكان والحدوث : الإمكان عبارة عن كون الشيء في نفسه بحيث لا يمتنع وجوده ولا عدمه، امتناعا واجباً ذاتياً، والحدوث عبارة عن كون الوجود مسبوقا بالعدم، والفرق بين هذين الأمرين ظاهر.
The First Premise: On the difference between possibility (imkan) and temporal origination (huduth). Possibility is that a thing is in itself such that its existence and non-existence are not impossible, with an essential necessary impossibility. And temporal origination is that existence is preceded by non-existence. The difference between these two matters is apparent.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and differentiation between two key terms in kalam: possibility (الإمكان) and origination (الحدوث). Possibility is defined as a property of a thing considered in itself: it is not impossible for it to exist or not exist. In other words, both existence and non-existence are equally possible for it. The definition specifies that the impossibility (of one of the two alternatives) is not essential; rather, the thing itself does not necessitate either. Origination, on the other hand, is defined as a property of existence: it is preceded by non-existence. The difference between the two is clear: possibility is a modal state of the essence, while origination is a temporal state of existence. This sets the stage for later arguments about the need for a determiner.

Key Terms
إمكان
حدوث
وجود
عدم
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing possibility with origination
Correction: Possibility is a modal state (neither necessary nor impossible), while origination is a temporal state (existence after non-existence).

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the definition of possibility (imkan)?
A) Existence preceded by non-existence.
B) The thing is necessary in itself.
C) The thing is such that its existence and non-existence are not impossible.
D) The thing is contingent upon a cause.

Key Takeaway
Possibility concerns the essence's inherent neutrality, while origination concerns existence's beginning in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
আপনার অস্তিত্ব চিন্তা করুন: আপনি যখন নিজের সম্ভাবনা (إمكان) এবং আপনার অস্তিত্বের শুরু (حدوث) - এই দুটি জিনিসের পার্থক্য বুঝতে পারেন, তখন কি আপনার স্রষ্টার প্রতি আপনার কৃতজ্ঞতা গভীর হয়? আপনি কি আজ একটি কাজ করবেন যা এই উপলব্ধিকে আপনার হৃদয়ে স্পষ্ট করে দেবে?
